### [24] [Non-Zipfian Distribution of Stopwords and Subset Selection Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.04691)
*Wentian Li,Oscar Fontanelli*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how stopwords and non-stopwords distribute by frequency vs rank, shows that stopwords follow a Beta Rank Function instead of a pure Zipf law, and proposes a probabilistic selection model that explains these distributions.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Zipf's law is a classic description of word frequency distributions, but it does not distinguish between stopwords and content words, and empirical data show systematic deviations from Zipf's law. The authors want to understand and model these deviations, particularly focusing on how stopwords behave differently from non-stopwords and how a principled selection mechanism could generate the observed distributions.

Method: They empirically analyze rank–frequency plots separately for stopwords and non-stopwords and fit different functional forms: the Beta Rank Function (BRF) for stopwords, and a quadratic function in log–log space for non-stopwords. They then propose a probabilistic stopword selection model where the probability a word of rank r is selected as a stopword follows a decreasing Hill function, while the probability of being non-stopword follows the complementary Hill function. They validate the selection probabilities using an independent text collection and analytically derive the induced rank–frequency distributions under the assumption that the full vocabulary obeys Zipf’s law.

Result: Empirically, stopwords’ rank–frequency curves are well fitted by the Beta Rank Function, better than by Zipf’s law, while non-stopwords deviate from Zipf and are better approximated by a quadratic relation between log frequency and log rank than by BRF. The estimated selection probabilities from an independent corpus match the proposed Hill-function-based model. Analytically, starting from a Zipfian full word list and applying the proposed selection mechanism yields a BRF distribution for stopwords and the observed quadratic form for non-stopwords.

Conclusion: Stopwords and non-stopwords have systematically different frequency–rank behaviors that cannot be captured by a single Zipf law. A simple rank-based probabilistic selection mechanism, modeled with complementary Hill functions, explains both the Beta Rank Function behavior of stopwords and the quadratic deviation for non-stopwords, providing a unified, theoretically grounded account of their observed distributions.

Abstract: Stopwords are words that are not very informative to the content or the meaning of a language text. Most stopwords are function words but can also be common verbs, adjectives and adverbs. In contrast to the well known Zipf's law for rank-frequency plot for all words, the rank-frequency plot for stopwords are best fitted by the Beta Rank Function (BRF). On the other hand, the rank-frequency plots of non-stopwords also deviate from the Zipf's law, but are fitted better by a quadratic function of log-token-count over log-rank than by BRF. Based on the observed rank of stopwords in the full word list, we propose a stopword (subset) selection model that the probability for being selected as a function of the word's rank $r$ is a decreasing Hill's function ($1/(1+(r/r_{mid})^γ)$); whereas the probability for not being selected is the standard Hill's function ( $1/(1+(r_{mid}/r)^γ)$). We validate this selection probability model by a direct estimation from an independent collection of texts. We also show analytically that this model leads to a BRF rank-frequency distribution for stopwords when the original full word list follows the Zipf's law, as well as explaining the quadratic fitting function for the non-stopwords.

With PEFT and quantization making deployment more resource-efficient, these models hold promise as scalable, adaptive tools for content moderation. The work underscores their potential utility for law enforcement, e-commerce platforms, and cybersecurity professionals seeking more effective ways to identify and categorize illicit online activities.

Conventional machine learning models, though effective in simpler contexts, often falter when confronting the semantic complexities and linguistic nuances characteristic of illicit marketplace communications. This research investigates the efficacy of Large Language Models (LLMs), specifically Meta's Llama 3.2 and Google's Gemma 3, in detecting and classifying illicit online marketplace content using the multilingual DUTA10K dataset. Employing fine-tuning techniques such as Parameter-Efficient Fine-Tuning (PEFT) and quantization, these models were systematically benchmarked against a foundational transformer-based model (BERT) and traditional machine learning baselines (Support Vector Machines and Naive Bayes). Experimental results reveal a task-dependent advantage for LLMs. In binary classification (illicit vs. non-illicit), Llama 3.2 demonstrated performance comparable to traditional methods. However, for complex, imbalanced multi-class classification involving 40 specific illicit categories, Llama 3.2 significantly surpassed all baseline models. These findings offer substantial practical implications for enhancing online safety, equipping law enforcement agencies, e-commerce platforms, and cybersecurity specialists with more effective, scalable, and adaptive tools for illicit content detection and moderation.

### [26] [AI-Assisted Moot Courts: Simulating Justice-Specific Questioning in Oral Arguments](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.04718)
*Kylie Zhang,Nimra Nadeem,Lucia Zheng,Dominik Stammbach,Peter Henderson*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper investigates whether AI can realistically and usefully simulate justice-style questioning in U.S. Supreme Court oral arguments for use in moot court training, proposing a two-layer evaluation framework showing that while models can produce realistic, issue-covering questions, they still suffer from low diversity and sycophancy that naive evaluations would miss.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Moot courts are essential for training law students and attorneys to respond to probing, often adversarial questions from judges, but organizing realistic practice sessions is costly and limited in scale. With advances in large language models, there is a natural question whether AI could simulate justice-specific questioning to provide scalable, rich practice environments. However, oral argument questioning is open-ended with no single correct next question, making evaluation difficult and rendering traditional accuracy-style metrics inadequate. The authors are motivated to (1) determine if AI can generate questions that both resemble real Supreme Court questioning and are pedagogically useful, and (2) develop an evaluation framework that captures these multifaceted qualities instead of relying on naive metrics that might overlook critical shortcomings.

Method: The authors use a dataset of U.S. Supreme Court oral argument transcripts to build and test AI simulators that generate justice questions in a moot-court setting. They develop both prompt-based simulators (likely single-turn or few-shot prompting of LLMs) and more complex agentic simulators (which may model multi-turn interactions, roles, or planning). Because there is no unique correct question at each turn, they propose a two-layer evaluation framework: one layer assesses realism of the simulated questions (e.g., how closely they mimic real justices’ style, tone, and topical focus), and the other layer assesses pedagogical usefulness (e.g., whether questions surface substantive legal issues, expose logical weaknesses, and maintain an appropriately adversarial tone). They define and compute multiple proxy metrics for each dimension and also collect human annotations to evaluate realism and issue coverage.

Result: The experiments show that AI-generated questions are often judged by human annotators as fairly realistic, suggesting that models can capture many surface-level and substantive aspects of justice questioning. The simulators achieve high recall on the substantive legal issues present in the ground-truth transcripts, indicating that they successfully identify and question the key legal topics in a case. However, the models exhibit significant weaknesses: their questions lack diversity in type and structure, failing to replicate the rich variety of real judicial questioning strategies; and they tend to be sycophantic, not challenging the advocates as robustly as real justices would. These deficits are not apparent when using simple or naive evaluation metrics, which might misleadingly indicate strong performance.

Conclusion: AI models show substantial promise for simulating justice-like questioning in oral arguments, producing questions that can seem realistic and that cover many of the key legal issues, which suggests real potential for augmenting moot court training. Nonetheless, their current limitations—especially low diversity of question types and a tendency toward sycophancy—mean that they do not yet fully match the pedagogical and adversarial richness of real Supreme Court questioning. The proposed two-layer evaluation framework is crucial, as naive evaluation approaches would fail to reveal these deficiencies. Future work must focus both on improving models’ questioning behavior and on refining multifaceted evaluation methods to ensure AI simulators are genuinely effective training tools rather than superficially plausible question generators.

Abstract: In oral arguments, judges probe attorneys with questions about the factual record, legal claims, and the strength of their arguments. To prepare for this questioning, both law schools and practicing attorneys rely on moot courts: practice simulations of appellate hearings. Leveraging a dataset of U.S. Supreme Court oral argument transcripts, we examine whether AI models can effectively simulate justice-specific questioning for moot court-style training. Evaluating oral argument simulation is challenging because there is no single correct question for any given turn. Instead, effective questioning should reflect a combination of desirable qualities, such as anticipating substantive legal issues, detecting logical weaknesses, and maintaining an appropriately adversarial tone. We introduce a two-layer evaluation framework that assesses both the realism and pedagogical usefulness of simulated questions using complementary proxy metrics. We construct and evaluate both prompt-based and agentic oral argument simulators. We find that simulated questions are often perceived as realistic by human annotators and achieve high recall of ground truth substantive legal issues. However, models still face substantial shortcomings, including low diversity in question types and sycophancy. Importantly, these shortcomings would remain undetected under naive evaluation approaches.

Result: Experiments on IF-RewardBench show that current judge models have notable shortcomings in accurately ranking responses by instruction-following quality. Moreover, evaluation scores on IF-RewardBench exhibit a stronger positive correlation with downstream task performance than scores on prior meta-evaluation benchmarks, indicating the new benchmark is more predictive of real-world effectiveness.

The findings highlight that many current judge models are less reliable than previously assumed, and that this new benchmark better reflects their true utility for guiding model alignment and optimization.

Abstract: Instruction-following is a foundational capability of large language models (LLMs), with its improvement hinging on scalable and accurate feedback from judge models. However, the reliability of current judge models in instruction-following remains underexplored due to several deficiencies of existing meta-evaluation benchmarks, such as their insufficient data coverage and oversimplified pairwise evaluation paradigms that misalign with model optimization scenarios. To this end, we propose IF-RewardBench, a comprehensive meta-evaluation benchmark for instruction-following that covers diverse instruction and constraint types. This design enables a listwise evaluation paradigm that assesses the capabilities of judge models to rank multiple responses, which is essential in guiding model alignment. ### [28] [Stacked from One: Multi-Scale Self-Injection for Context Window Extension](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.04759)
*Wei Han,Pan Zhou,Shuicheng Yan*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces SharedLLM, a framework that lets a base short-context LLM handle very long contexts efficiently by compressing and hierarchically storing them, then retrieving only query-relevant information.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLMs are bottlenecked by limited context windows. Extending context via naive continual pre-training on long sequences is expensive in both data and compute. The authors want a way to handle >128K-token inputs using models trained only on short contexts, while keeping memory and inference costs low.

Method: They propose SharedLLM, which splits a single underlying LLM into two stacked short-context instances: a lower compressor and an upper decoder (self-injection). The lower model compresses long inputs into compact, multi-grained representations, passing them only through the lowest layers of the upper model to avoid full long-context forward passes and cross-attention. A tree-based data structure encodes the long context and supports query-aware retrieval of only the relevant compressed chunks. Training is done on 8K-token sequences, with architectural and compression mechanisms designed to generalize to much longer contexts.

Result: On a wide range of long-context modeling and understanding benchmarks, SharedLLM matches or outperforms strong baselines, while substantially reducing memory usage and improving inference speed (about 2× faster than streaming methods and 3× faster than encoder-decoder designs) for sequences beyond 128K tokens, despite only being trained on 8K-token sequences.

Conclusion: The SharedLLM framework demonstrates that careful multi-grained compression, query-aware retrieval, and self-injection of shared LLM layers can extend short-context models to very long inputs efficiently. It achieves a favorable trade-off between accuracy and efficiency, offering large context generalization, lower memory footprint, and significant speedups without expensive long-context continual pre-training.

Abstract: The limited context window of contemporary large language models (LLMs) remains a primary bottleneck for their broader application across diverse domains. Although continual pre-training on long-context data offers a straightforward solution, it incurs prohibitive data acquisition and computational costs. To address this challenge, we propose~\modelname, a novel framework based on multi-grained context compression and query-aware information acquisition. SharedLLM comprises two stacked short-context LLMs: a lower model serving as a compressor and an upper model acting as a decoder. The lower model compresses long inputs into compact, multi-grained representations, which are then forwarded to the upper model for context-aware processing. To maximize efficiency, this information transfer occurs exclusively at the lowest layers, bypassing lengthy forward passes and redundant cross-attention operations. This entire process, wherein the upper and lower models are derived from the same underlying LLM layers, is termed~\textit{self-injection}. To support this architecture, a specialized tree-based data structure enables the efficient encoding and query-aware retrieval of contextual information. Despite being trained on sequences of only 8K tokens, \modelname~effectively generalizes to inputs exceeding 128K tokens. Across a comprehensive suite of long-context modeling and understanding benchmarks, \modelname~achieves performance superior or comparable to strong baselines, striking an optimal balance between efficiency and accuracy. Furthermore, these design choices allow \modelname~to substantially reduce the memory footprint and yield notable inference speedups ($2\times$ over streaming and $3\times$ over encoder-decoder architectures).

### [29] [TSEmbed: Unlocking Task Scaling in Universal Multimodal Embeddings](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.04772)
*Yebo Wu,Feng Liu,Ziwei Xie,Zhiyuan Liu,Changwang Zhang,Jun Wang,Li Li*

Main category: cs.CL

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Multimodal Large Language Models have strong reasoning ability but struggle to serve as universal embedding models because different downstream tasks impose conflicting optimization objectives, degrading representation quality. The authors aim to disentangle these task conflicts so a single multimodal model can provide high-quality, scalable embeddings for diverse applications, including real-world industrial scenarios.

Method: They propose TSEmbed, which combines Mixture-of-Experts (MoE) with Low-Rank Adaptation (LoRA) to create task-specialized experts while preserving a shared backbone. They further introduce Expert-Aware Negative Sampling (EANS), which uses expert routing distributions as a measure of semantic similarity to select hard negative samples that share expert activation patterns with the query. Training follows a two-stage paradigm: first stabilizing and specializing experts, then applying EANS-based training to refine embeddings and improve discrimination across tasks.

Result: TSEmbed achieves state-of-the-art performance on the Massive Multimodal Embedding Benchmark (MMEB) and also outperforms existing methods on large-scale industrial production datasets, demonstrating both strong generalization and practical value for universal multimodal embedding tasks.

Conclusion: Disentangling task conflicts via MoE+LoRA and using expert routing to guide hard negative sampling enables a single MLLM-based framework to function as a universal multimodal embedding model. The approach scales to diverse tasks, improves discriminative power and representation quality, and provides a foundation for task-level scaling in universal multimodal embeddings.

Abstract: Despite the exceptional reasoning capabilities of Multimodal Large Language Models (MLLMs), their adaptation into universal embedding models is significantly impeded by task conflict. To address this, we propose TSEmbed, a universal multimodal embedding framework that synergizes Mixture-of-Experts (MoE) with Low-Rank Adaptation (LoRA) to explicitly disentangle conflicting task objectives. Moreover, we introduce Expert-Aware Negative Sampling (EANS), a novel strategy that leverages expert routing distributions as an intrinsic proxy for semantic similarity. By dynamically prioritizing informative hard negatives that share expert activation patterns with the query, EANS effectively sharpens the model's discriminative power and refines embedding boundaries. To ensure training stability, we further devise a two-stage learning paradigm that solidifies expert specialization before optimizing representations via EANS. TSEmbed achieves state-of-the-art performance on both the Massive Multimodal Embedding Benchmark (MMEB) and real-world industrial production datasets, laying a foundation for task-level scaling in universal multimodal embeddings.

### [30] [Attention's Gravitational Field:A Power-Law Interpretation of Positional Correlation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.04805)
*Edward Zhang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes a new view of positional relationships in LLMs via an “Attention Gravitational Field” (AGF), decouples positional and semantic embeddings, and shows this improves accuracy and aligns attention behavior with a gravity-like law.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing positional encodings in LLMs are tightly coupled with token semantics and may not optimally capture relative positions or be easily interpretable. There is a need for architectures that separate position from meaning to improve performance and to better understand how the attention mechanism structures information over sequences. The authors aim to provide both a practical improvement and a theoretical, physics-inspired explanation of attention behavior.

Method: The authors redesign the model architecture to explicitly decouple positional encodings from semantic embeddings and define an Attention Gravitational Field (AGF) that mathematically characterizes how attention weights vary with positional relationships. They analyze AGF against learning dynamics and stability curves, and compare its functional form to Newton’s Law of Universal Gravitation, testing whether attention decay patterns over distance resemble gravity-like interactions.

Result: The modified architecture with decoupled positional and semantic components yields higher accuracy than standard positional encoding schemes in Large Language Models. Empirically, measured attention patterns over token positions follow curves that match the predictions of the Attention Gravitational Field formulation and show quantitative similarities to the inverse-square behavior suggested by Newtonian gravity. The AGF framework is also consistent with observed learning and stability characteristics during training.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that decoupling positional encodings from semantic embeddings improves LLM performance and that the proposed Attention Gravitational Field offers a coherent, physics-inspired framework for interpreting attention over positions. This approach not only yields practical gains but also enhances interpretability, potentially guiding future work on more principled positional encoding schemes and model optimization strategies grounded in AGF theory.

Abstract: This paper explores the underlying principles of positional relationships and encodings within Large Language Models (LLMs) and introduces the concept of the Attention Gravitational Field (AGF). By decoupling positional encodings from semantic embeddings, we optimize the model architecture and achieve superior accuracy compared to prevailing encoding methods. Furthermore, we provide an in-depth analysis of AGF, demonstrating its intrinsic consistency with learning and stability curves, as well as its empirical alignment with Newton's Law of Universal Gravitation. By offering a rigorous theoretical exploration of these phenomena, this work represents a significant step toward interpreting the Attention mechanism and unlocks new possibilities for future research in model optimization and interpretability.

</details>


### [31] [Beyond the Context Window: A Cost-Performance Analysis of Fact-Based Memory vs. Long-Context LLMs for Persistent Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.04814)
*Natchanon Pollertlam,Witchayut Kornsuwannawit*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper compares long-context LLMs vs. a structured fact-based memory system for persistent conversational agents, analyzing both accuracy and API cost, and derives when each approach is preferable.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Persistent chatbots need to remember past interactions. Designers must choose between feeding long histories to large-context LLMs or using an external memory that stores key facts. There is little clear guidance on which approach is better in terms of accuracy and cost for different scenarios.

Method: Implement a fact-based memory system using the Mem0 framework and compare it against a long-context GPT-5-mini model across three memory-focused benchmarks: LongMemEval, LoCoMo, and PersonaMemv2. Measure factual recall/accuracy for both systems. Build a cost model that accounts for prompt caching and compute per-turn costs for both architectures as context length and number of turns grow. Analyze when the memory system becomes cheaper than long-context inference.

Result: Long-context GPT-5-mini outperforms the fact-based memory system on factual recall for LongMemEval and LoCoMo. The memory system performs competitively on PersonaMemv2, where the needed memory is more stable persona attributes amenable to simple fact extraction. The cost model shows that long-context LLMs incur a per-turn cost that scales with context length even with caching, while the memory system has a nearly constant per-turn read cost after an initial write. At 100k token context, the memory-based architecture becomes cheaper after roughly ten turns, and this break-even occurs earlier as context length increases.

Conclusion: Long-context LLMs generally provide better factual recall, but structured memory systems can match performance in scenarios dominated by stable, factual attributes and become more cost-effective beyond a modest number of turns at large context lengths. The paper provides a concrete, context-length- and turn-dependent break-even analysis that practitioners can use to choose between long-context prompting and dedicated memory in production conversational AI systems.

Abstract: Persistent conversational AI systems face a choice between passing full conversation histories to a long-context large language model (LLM) and maintaining a dedicated memory system that extracts and retrieves structured facts. We compare a fact-based memory system built on the Mem0 framework against long-context LLM inference on three memory-centric benchmarks - LongMemEval, LoCoMo, and PersonaMemv2 - and evaluate both architectures on accuracy and cumulative API cost. Long-context GPT-5-mini achieves higher factual recall on LongMemEval and LoCoMo, while the memory system is competitive on PersonaMemv2, where persona consistency depends on stable, factual attributes suited to flat-typed extraction. We construct a cost model that incorporates prompt caching and show that the two architectures have structurally different cost profiles: long-context inference incurs a per-turn charge that grows with context length even under caching, while the memory system's per-turn read cost remains roughly fixed after a one-time write phase. At a context length of 100k tokens, the memory system becomes cheaper after approximately ten interaction turns, with the break-even point decreasing as context length grows. These results characterize the accuracy-cost trade-off between the two approaches and provide a concrete criterion for selecting between them in production deployments.

### [32] [Autoscoring Anticlimax: A Meta-analytic Understanding of AI's Short-answer Shortcomings and Wording Weaknesses](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.04820)
*Michael Hardy*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Meta-analysis of LLM-based automated short-answer scoring, comparing architectures and design factors, and highlighting performance limits and biases.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Automated short-answer scoring is less mature than other LLM applications, especially in educational, high-stakes contexts. There is a need to understand how well LLMs agree with human scorers, what model and system factors drive performance, and whether these systems introduce systematic biases when scoring children’s written work.

Method: The authors conduct a systematic review of studies using LLMs for short-answer scoring and extract 890 final (culminating) results. They use mixed-effects meta-regression to model effect sizes expressed as Quadratic Weighted Kappa (QWK), and analyze how factors such as task difficulty for humans, model architecture (decoder-only vs encoder-based), tokenizer vocabulary size, and other design choices relate to scoring performance. They also run additional controlled experiments to probe wording sensitivity, tokenization effects, and bias elicitation in educational scoring scenarios.

Result: They find that the human difficulty of a scoring task (how hard it is for human experts to score children’s writing) does not statistically predict LLM performance. Some tasks that are easiest for humans are actually hardest for LLMs. On average, decoder-only LLMs perform substantially worse than encoder models, underperforming by about 0.37 in QWK agreement with human scores. Larger tokenizer vocabularies show diminishing returns, likely because many tokens are undertrained. Additional experiments show strong sensitivity to prompt wording and tokenization, and reveal that in high-stakes educational contexts, LLM scoring can manifest racial discrimination.

Conclusion: LLM-based short-answer scoring currently has important limitations: architecture choice significantly affects agreement with human scores, especially with encoder models outperforming decoder-only ones; human task difficulty is not a reliable indicator of LLM difficulty; and scaling vocabulary alone does not guarantee better performance. System designers should explicitly address statistical and architectural shortcomings of autoregressive models, carefully consider tokenizer design, and rigorously audit for wording sensitivity and bias, particularly in high-stakes educational applications.

Abstract: Automated short-answer scoring lags other LLM applications. We meta-analyze 890 culminating results across a systematic review of LLM short-answer scoring studies, modeling the traditional effect size of Quadratic Weighted Kappa (QWK) with mixed effects metaregression. We quantitatively illustrate that that the level of difficulty for human experts to perform the task of scoring written work of children has no observed statistical effect on LLM performance. Particularly, we show that some scoring tasks measured as the easiest by human scorers were the hardest for LLMs. Whether by poor implementation by thoughtful researchers or patterns traceable to autoregressive training, on average decoder-only architectures underperform encoders by 0.37--a substantial difference in agreement with humans. Additionally, we measure the contributions of various aspects of LLM technology on successful scoring such as tokenizer vocabulary size, which exhibits diminishing returns--potentially due to undertrained tokens. Findings argue for systems design which better anticipates known statistical shortcomings of autoregressive models. Finally, we provide additional experiments to illustrate wording and tokenization sensitivity and bias elicitation in high-stakes education contexts, where LLMs demonstrate racial discrimination. Code and data for this study are available.

</details>


### [33] [From Unfamiliar to Familiar: Detecting Pre-training Data via Gradient Deviations in Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.04828)
*Ruiqi Zhang,Lingxiang Wang,Hainan Zhang,Zhiming Zheng,Yanyan Lan*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose a gradient-based method (GDS) to detect whether a sample was used in LLM pre-training, improving robustness over likelihood and heuristic similarity methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: There is a need to identify whether specific data points were used in LLM pre-training to handle copyright issues and benchmark contamination. Existing detection methods either rely on likelihood statistics, which are biased by corpus word frequencies, or on fine-tuning-related heuristics, which depend heavily on how similar the test data is to the fine-tuning data. A more reliable and transferable indicator of training membership is needed.

Method: The paper views training as an optimization process where samples evolve from unfamiliar to familiar. They empirically observe systematic differences in gradient behavior between member and non-member samples: familiar samples cause smaller parameter updates, affect different parameter locations, and activate neurons more sharply. Building on this, they define Gradient Deviation Scores (GDS). For each target sample, they compute gradient profiles summarizing the magnitude, spatial location, and concentration of parameter updates across FFN and attention modules. These gradient-based features are then input to a lightweight classifier that performs binary membership inference (pre-training member vs. non-member). They evaluate this across multiple datasets and measure transferability.

Result: Across five public datasets, GDS outperforms strong baseline methods for pre-training data detection and shows notably better cross-dataset transferability, meaning the classifier trained on one dataset generalizes better to others. The interpretability analysis further reveals clear distributional differences in gradient-based features between member and non-member data, validating the design of GDS.

Conclusion: Gradient behavior provides a powerful signal for pre-training data membership in LLMs. By encoding magnitude, location, and concentration of parameter updates into gradient profiles and using them in a simple classifier, GDS achieves state-of-the-art and more transferable pre-training data detection compared with existing likelihood-based and heuristic methods. The gradient-feature differences are interpretable and make the approach practical and scalable for real-world pre-training data auditing.

Abstract: Pre-training data detection for LLMs is essential for addressing copyright concerns and mitigating benchmark contamination. Existing methods mainly focus on the likelihood-based statistical features or heuristic signals before and after fine-tuning, but the former are susceptible to word frequency bias in corpora, and the latter strongly depend on the similarity of fine-tuning data. From an optimization perspective, we observe that during training, samples transition from unfamiliar to familiar in a manner reflected by systematic differences in gradient behavior. Familiar samples exhibit smaller update magnitudes, distinct update locations in model components, and more sharply activated neurons. Based on this insight, we propose GDS, a method that identifies pre-training data by probing Gradient Deviation Scores of target samples. Specifically, we first represent each sample using gradient profiles that capture the magnitude, location, and concentration of parameter updates across FFN and Attention modules, revealing consistent distinctions between member and non-member data. These features are then fed into a lightweight classifier to perform binary membership inference. Experiments on five public datasets show that GDS achieves state-of-the-art performance with significantly improved cross-dataset transferability over strong baselines. Further interpretability analyse show gradient feature distribution differences, enabling practical and scalable pre-training data detection.

</details>

### [35] [HACHIMI: Scalable and Controllable Student Persona Generation via Orchestrated Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.04855)
*Yilin Jiang,Fei Tan,Xuanyu Yin,Jing Leng,Aimin Zhou*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Introduces HACHIMI, a multi-agent framework to generate 1M theory-aligned, distribution-controlled student personas (Grades 1–12) for benchmarking and educational simulations.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

This limits their usefulness for rigorous evaluation, simulation, and social-science style analysis. The paper aims to build a principled, scalable way to generate synthetic student populations that are theoretically grounded and statistically controllable.

Method: They formalize the task as Theory-Aligned and Distribution-Controllable Persona Generation (TAD-PG) and propose HACHIMI, a multi-agent Propose–Validate–Revise pipeline. Each persona is factorized into a schema rooted in educational theory. A neuro-symbolic validator enforces developmental and psychological constraints over the generated attributes. Stratified sampling ensures coverage of specified quotas (e.g., grade levels, constructs), and semantic deduplication reduces mode collapse by removing near-duplicate personas. All personas are generated with the Qwen2.5-72B model, resulting in the HACHIMI-1M corpus spanning Grades 1–12.

Result: Intrinsic evaluation shows that generated personas almost always satisfy the schema, match target quotas closely, and exhibit high diversity (low redundancy). For external validation, personas are instantiated as student agents answering CEPS and PISA 2022 survey items. Across 16 defined cohorts, the synthetic agents’ math performance and curiosity/growth-mindset constructs strongly correlate with human survey results, while classroom-climate and well-being constructs show only moderate correspondence, indicating varying degrees of fidelity across constructs.

Conclusion: HACHIMI successfully operationalizes theory-aligned, distribution-controllable generation of large-scale student personas, producing the HACHIMI-1M corpus as a standardized synthetic student population. The framework yields high internal validity and partial external fidelity, particularly strong for cognitive and motivation-related constructs, weaker for context and affective constructs. This resource can support group-level benchmarking of educational LLMs and social-science style simulations, and the observed fidelity gradient highlights where future work is needed to better capture classroom climate and well-being dimensions.

Abstract: Student Personas (SPs) are emerging as infrastructure for educational LLMs, yet prior work often relies on ad-hoc prompting or hand-crafted profiles with limited control over educational theory and population distributions. We formalize this as Theory-Aligned and Distribution-Controllable Persona Generation (TAD-PG) and introduce HACHIMI, a multi-agent Propose-Validate-Revise framework that generates theory-aligned, quota-controlled personas. HACHIMI factorizes each persona into a theory-anchored educational schema, enforces developmental and psychological constraints via a neuro-symbolic validator, and combines stratified sampling with semantic deduplication to reduce mode collapse. The resulting HACHIMI-1M corpus comprises 1 million personas for Grades 1-12. Intrinsic evaluation shows near-perfect schema validity, accurate quotas, and substantial diversity, while external evaluation instantiates personas as student agents answering CEPS and PISA 2022 surveys; across 16 cohorts, math and curiosity/growth constructs align strongly between humans and agents, whereas classroom-climate and well-being constructs are only moderately aligned, revealing a fidelity gradient. All personas are generated with Qwen2.5-72B, and HACHIMI provides a standardized synthetic student population for group-level benchmarking and social-science simulations. Resources available at https://github.com/ZeroLoss-Lab/HACHIMI

Ensemble methods further enhance reliability. However, systematic biases in which values are emphasized (e.g., over-weighting Security) underscore that LLMs provide both useful complementary perspectives and non-trivial model-induced value biases that must be understood and managed. Abstract: Qualitative analysis of open-ended interviews plays a central role in ethnographic and economic research by uncovering individuals' values, motivations, and culturally embedded financial behaviors. While large language models (LLMs) offer promising support for automating and enriching such interpretive work, their ability to produce nuanced, reliable interpretations under inherent task ambiguity remains unclear. In our work we evaluate LLMs on the task of identifying the top three human values expressed in long-form interviews based on the Schwartz Theory of Basic Values framework. We compare their outputs to expert annotations, analyzing both performance and uncertainty patterns relative to the experts. Results show that LLMs approach the human ceiling on set-based metrics (F1, Jaccard) but struggle to recover exact value rankings, as reflected in lower RBO scores. While the average Schwartz value distributions of most models closely match those of human analysts, their uncertainty structures across the Schwartz values diverge from expert uncertainty patterns. Among the evaluated models, Qwen performs closest to expert-level agreement and exhibits the strongest alignment with expert Schwartz value distributions. LLM ensemble methods yield consistent gains across metrics, with Majority Vote and Borda Count performing best. Notably, systematic overemphasis on certain Schwartz values, like Security, suggests both the potential of LLMs to provide complementary perspectives and the need to further investigate model-induced value biases. Overall, our findings highlight both the promise and the limitations of LLMs as collaborators in inherently ambiguous qualitative value analysis.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Traditional classifiers often act as black boxes, struggle with ambiguous or brittle linguistic cues, and incorrectly penalize neutral or critical reporting of conspiracies as endorsement. The authors are motivated to build a more interpretable, psycholinguistically grounded system that separates reasoning from localization and avoids misclassifying objective reporting as endorsement.

Method: They design an agentic LLM pipeline with two decoupled components. For marker extraction, they propose Dynamic Discriminative Chain-of-Thought (DD-CoT), which uses dynamic reasoning chains plus deterministic anchoring to reduce ambiguity and handle character-level noise when locating conspiracy markers. For conspiracy endorsement detection, they introduce an "Anti-Echo Chamber" architecture: multiple adversarial LLM agents (a Parallel Council) produce competing judgments, which are then adjudicated by a Calibrated Judge agent designed to avoid the "Reporter Trap"—the tendency to flag neutral or critical coverage of conspiracies as endorsement.

Result: On SemEval-2026 Task 10, their system achieves 0.24 Macro F1 on Subtask 1 (marker extraction), doubling the baseline performance, and 0.79 Macro F1 on Subtask 2 (conspiracy detection), a 49% relative improvement over baseline. Their Subtask 1 system ranks 3rd on the development leaderboard, indicating strong comparative performance.

Conclusion: The authors conclude that an agentic, decoupled LLM pipeline can substantially improve both interpretability and accuracy for psycholinguistically grounded conspiracy analysis. Their DD-CoT and Anti-Echo Chamber architectures effectively separate semantic reasoning from marker localization and mitigate biases like the Reporter Trap. They position this approach as a versatile paradigm for future NLP tasks requiring interpretable reasoning over subtle, psycholinguistic phenomena.

Abstract: This paper presents a novel agentic LLM pipeline for SemEval-2026 Task 10 that jointly extracts psycholinguistic conspiracy markers and detects conspiracy endorsement. Unlike traditional classifiers that conflate semantic reasoning with structural localization, our decoupled design isolates these challenges. For marker extraction, we propose Dynamic Discriminative Chain-of-Thought (DD-CoT) with deterministic anchoring to resolve semantic ambiguity and character-level brittleness. For conspiracy detection, an "Anti-Echo Chamber" architecture, consisting of an adversarial Parallel Council adjudicated by a Calibrated Judge, overcomes the "Reporter Trap," where models falsely penalize objective reporting. Achieving 0.24 Macro F1 (+100\% over baseline) on S1 and 0.79 Macro F1 (+49\%) on S2, with the S1 system ranking 3rd on the development leaderboard, our approach establishes a versatile paradigm for interpretable, psycholinguistically-grounded NLP.

### [40] [Federated Heterogeneous Language Model Optimization for Hybrid Automatic Speech Recognition](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.04945)
*Mengze Hong,Yi Gu,Di Jiang,Hanlin Gu,Chen Jason Zhang,Lu Wang,Zhiyang Su*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose a new way to automatically combine heterogeneous language models in federated ASR using match-and-merge algorithms (genetic and reinforcement learning based) that reduce error rates and speed up convergence.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Federated learning for ASR creates many local models to protect user privacy, but these models must be merged effectively. Acoustic models already have established merging techniques, but language models used for N-best rescoring are heterogeneous (traditional n-gram vs neural LMs), making direct merging difficult. There is a need for a principled, scalable method to combine such heterogeneous LMs while preserving privacy and improving recognition accuracy.

Method: They formulate a heterogeneous language model optimization task and introduce a match-and-merge paradigm. Two concrete algorithms are proposed: (1) Genetic Match-and-Merge Algorithm (GMMA), which uses genetic algorithm operations (selection, crossover, mutation) to evolve pairings and combinations of different LMs; (2) Reinforced Match-and-Merge Algorithm (RMMA), which uses reinforcement learning to guide the matching and merging process, aiming for faster and more efficient convergence to good combinations. These algorithms operate over both non-neural n-gram and neural network LMs used for N-best list rescoring in hybrid ASR systems within a federated learning setting.

Result: On seven OpenSLR datasets, RMMA achieves the lowest average Character Error Rate compared to baselines and GMMA, shows stronger generalization across datasets, and converges up to seven times faster than GMMA. This demonstrates that the match-and-merge paradigm is effective for optimizing heterogeneous LMs in federated ASR.

Conclusion: The proposed match-and-merge paradigm, especially the reinforcement learning-based RMMA, provides an effective and scalable way to merge heterogeneous language models in federated ASR scenarios. It improves recognition accuracy, generalizes well across datasets, converges significantly faster than the genetic approach, and supports privacy-preserving training by enabling better use of decentralized LMs.

Abstract: Training automatic speech recognition (ASR) models increasingly relies on decentralized federated learning to ensure data privacy and accessibility, producing multiple local models that require effective merging. In hybrid ASR systems, while acoustic models can be merged using established methods, the language model (LM) for rescoring the N-best speech recognition list faces challenges due to the heterogeneity of non-neural n-gram models and neural network models. This paper proposes a heterogeneous LM optimization task and introduces a match-and-merge paradigm with two algorithms: the Genetic Match-and-Merge Algorithm (GMMA), using genetic operations to evolve and pair LMs, and the Reinforced Match-and-Merge Algorithm (RMMA), leveraging reinforcement learning for efficient convergence. Experiments on seven OpenSLR datasets show RMMA achieves the lowest average Character Error Rate and better generalization than baselines, converging up to seven times faster than GMMA, highlighting the paradigm's potential for scalable, privacy-preserving ASR systems.

</details>


### [41] [LocalSUG: Geography-Aware LLM for Query Suggestion in Local-Life Services](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.04946)
*Jinwen Chen,Shuai Gong,Shiwen Zhang,Zheng Zhang,Yachao Zhao,Lingxiang Wang,Haibo Zhou,Yuan Zhan,Wei Lin,Hainan Zhang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose LocalSUG, an LLM-based, geographically-aware query suggestion framework for local-life platforms that improves CTR and reduces no-result searches while remaining efficient enough for real-time use.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing query suggestion systems on local-life service platforms depend mainly on popular historical queries, making them weak at covering long-tail user needs. Directly using generic LLMs is attractive for better semantic coverage but causes three problems in this domain: (1) lack of city-specific and geographic awareness, so suggestions may not match local offerings; (2) exposure bias between how models are trained and how they generate queries at inference; and (3) high online latency that prevents practical deployment in a real-time search environment. The paper aims to design an LLM-based solution that overcomes these domain-specific obstacles while providing measurable business value.

Method: The authors design LocalSUG, an LLM-based query suggestion framework with three main components. (1) City-aware candidate mining: they build a term co-occurrence mechanism conditioned on city to inject geographic grounding into the candidate generation process, ensuring suggestions are locally relevant. (2) Beam-search-driven GRPO: they introduce a GRPO (a preference-optimization-like) algorithm explicitly tied to the beam search decoding used at inference, so training better matches generation behavior, thereby mitigating exposure bias. This is coupled with a multi-objective reward design that balances semantic relevance and business metrics. (3) Latency optimization: they add quality-aware beam acceleration and vocabulary pruning to lower inference time while preserving suggestion quality, making the system suitable for high-traffic online environments.

Result: In both offline metrics and large-scale online A/B experiments, LocalSUG outperforms the previous production system. It increases click-through rate (CTR) by 0.35% and decreases the proportion of queries with low or no results by 2.56%, showing improved user engagement and better coverage of user intent while operating under real-world latency constraints.

Conclusion: LocalSUG successfully adapts LLM-based query suggestion to local-life service platforms by incorporating city-level grounding, aligning training with inference-time decoding through beam-search-driven GRPO, and applying inference optimizations to meet latency requirements. The framework delivers statistically meaningful improvements in CTR and a notable reduction in no-result cases, demonstrating that a carefully engineered, domain-specific LLM integration can be both effective and production-ready in large-scale commercial search systems.

Abstract: In local-life service platforms, the query suggestion module plays a crucial role in enhancing user experience by generating candidate queries based on user input prefixes, thus reducing user effort and accelerating search. Traditional multi-stage cascading systems rely heavily on historical top queries, limiting their ability to address long-tail demand. While LLMs offer strong semantic generalization, deploying them in local-life services introduces three key challenges: lack of geographic grounding, exposure bias in preference optimization, and online inference latency. To address these issues, we propose LocalSUG, an LLM-based query suggestion framework tailored for local-life service platforms. First, we introduce a city-aware candidate mining strategy based on term co-occurrence to inject geographic grounding into generation. Second, we propose a beam-search-driven GRPO algorithm that aligns training with inference-time decoding, reducing exposure bias in autoregressive generation. A multi-objective reward mechanism further optimizes both relevance and business-oriented metrics. Finally, we develop quality-aware beam acceleration and vocabulary pruning techniques that significantly reduce online latency while preserving generation quality. Extensive offline evaluations and large-scale online A/B testing demonstrate that LocalSUG improves click-through rate (CTR) by +0.35% and reduces the low/no-result rate by 2.56%, validating its effectiveness in real-world deployment.

### [42] [Replaying pre-training data improves fine-tuning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.04964)
*Suhas Kotha,Percy Liang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper shows that replaying generic (non-target) pre‑training data during fine‑tuning or mid‑training on a specific domain actually *improves* target-domain performance and data efficiency, rather than only preventing forgetting.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard practice is: (1) pre-train on large generic web data, then (2) fine-tune on comparatively small target-domain data, occasionally mixing generic data just to avoid catastrophic forgetting. It is usually assumed generic data does not help the target task itself. The authors want to test, in a controlled setting, whether interleaving generic replay during target-domain training can actively *boost* target performance and data efficiency, and how this depends on when and how much target data appears during pre-training.

Method: They run controlled experiments with 150M-parameter language models trained on 4B total tokens, where 4M tokens are from a target domain. They compare different training schemes: pure fine-tuning on the target, fine-tuning with replayed generic data, and mid-training schemes that inject target data during pre-training with and without replay. They systematically vary data schedules and where in the training process target data is introduced. They then measure the effective data efficiency and performance on target tasks. Finally, they scale up to 8B-parameter models and test on real applications such as agentic web navigation and Basque QA.

Result: In the controlled 150M-parameter setting, adding generic replay during fine-tuning improves target-data efficiency by up to 1.87×, and during mid-training by up to 2.06×, meaning the same performance can be reached with roughly half as much target data. Replay is most beneficial when the pre-training stage includes relatively little target data. In large-scale 8B-parameter models, replay improves agentic web navigation success by 4.5 percentage points and Basque QA accuracy by 2 percentage points, confirming the effect in practical applications.

Conclusion: Contrary to the usual assumption that generic data replay during fine-tuning only prevents forgetting, the paper shows it can significantly enhance target-domain learning and data efficiency. Replaying generic data appears particularly valuable when the model has seen little target data during pre-training, suggesting that maintaining exposure to broad, generic distributions helps the model learn specialized tasks more efficiently. This has implications for how to schedule and mix generic and target data in both fine-tuning and mid-training for domain adaptation.

Abstract: To obtain a language model for a target domain (e.g. math), the current paradigm is to pre-train on a vast amount of generic web text and then fine-tune on the relatively limited amount of target data. Typically, generic data is only mixed in during fine-tuning to prevent catastrophic forgetting of the generic domain. We surprisingly find that replaying the generic data during fine-tuning can actually improve performance on the (less related) target task. Concretely, in a controlled pre-training environment with 4M target tokens, 4B total tokens, and 150M parameter models, generic replay increases target data efficiency by up to $1.87\times$ for fine-tuning and $2.06\times$ for mid-training. We further analyze data schedules that introduce target data during pre-training and find that replay helps more when there is less target data present in pre-training. We demonstrate the success of replay in practice for fine-tuning 8B parameter models, improving agentic web navigation success by $4.5\%$ and Basque question-answering accuracy by $2\%$.

### [43] [When Weak LLMs Speak with Confidence, Preference Alignment Gets Stronger](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.04968)
*Amirabbas Afzali,Myeongho Jeon,Maria Brbic*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper shows that a weak LLM, if used selectively and with confidence-based weighting, can provide preference labels that outperform full human annotation for aligning larger LLMs.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Human preference alignment for LLMs is crucial but expensive, typically requiring many human annotations or access to strong API models. The authors want to reduce this cost and dependence by investigating whether a weaker, cheaper LLM can generate effective preference labels, and under what conditions those labels might even surpass fully human-labeled data.

Method: They use a weak LLM to annotate preference data (e.g., which response is better) and estimate its confidence in each annotation. Instead of using all its labels, they select or re-weight samples based on confidence. They formalize this as Confidence-Weighted Preference Optimization (CW-PO), a general framework that re-weights training samples according to the weak model's confidence and can plug into standard preference optimization objectives such as DPO. They compare training with CW-PO using a fraction of human annotations against baselines using full human annotations without confidence weighting.

Result: Subsampling the weak LLM’s most confident labels yields better alignment performance than training on full human annotations with standard DPO. Under CW-PO, a model trained with only 20% of the human annotations (augmented and re-weighted by weak-LLM confidence) outperforms a model trained with 100% of human annotations under standard DPO. This demonstrates the effectiveness of confidence-based re-weighting of weak-LLM-generated labels.

Conclusion: Weak LLMs can effectively serve as preference annotators for alignment if their confidence is estimated and used for re-weighting training samples. Confidence-Weighted Preference Optimization significantly reduces human labeling costs and can even surpass methods relying entirely on fully human-labeled data, suggesting a scalable, cost-efficient path for LLM preference alignment.

Abstract: Preference alignment is an essential step in adapting large language models (LLMs) to human values, but existing approaches typically depend on costly human annotations or large-scale API-based models. We explore whether a weak LLM can instead act as an effective annotator. We surprisingly find that selecting only a subset of a weak LLM's highly confident samples leads to substantially better performance than using full human annotations. Building on this insight, we propose Confidence-Weighted Preference Optimization (CW-PO), a general framework that re-weights training samples by a weak LLM's confidence and can be applied across different preference optimization objectives. Notably, the model aligned by CW-PO with just 20% of human annotations outperforms the model trained with 100% of annotations under standard DPO. These results suggest that weak LLMs, when paired with confidence weighting, can dramatically reduce the cost of preference alignment while even outperforming methods trained on fully human-labeled data.

*Minxing Zhang,Yi Yang,Zhuofan Jia,Xuan Yang,Jian Pei,Yuchen Zang,Xingwang Deng,Xianglong Chen*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces MPCEval, a task-aware, reference-free evaluation suite specifically designed to measure and benchmark the quality of multi-party conversation generation across several dimensions.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing evaluation methods for conversational AI are mostly tailored to two-party dialogues and rely heavily on single aggregate scores and reference-based metrics. These approaches fail to capture key aspects of multi-party conversations, such as complex turn-taking, role-specific behavior, global conversational structure, and the presence of multiple valid continuations. As multi-party applications like smart reply and collaborative assistants become more important, there is a need for a more nuanced, scalable, and reproducible evaluation framework.

Method: The authors design MPCEval, an evaluation and benchmarking framework that decomposes multi-party conversation quality into three main components: (1) speaker modeling (how well models capture participation and role behavior), (2) content quality (progression, informativeness, and novelty of the generated text), and (3) speaker–content consistency (alignment between who speaks and what is said). They also distinguish between local next-turn prediction and global full-conversation generation. MPCEval defines novel, quantitative, reference-free metrics that can be applied across different datasets and models and implemented as a reproducible evaluation pipeline.

Result: Using MPCEval, the authors evaluate a variety of modern multi-party conversation generation methods on both public and real-world datasets and compare them to human-authored conversations. The analysis reveals systematic, dimension-specific behaviors of models: differences in participation balance among speakers, how well content progresses over time, the novelty of generated contributions, and the degree of speaker–content consistency. These findings show that models that may look similar under traditional single-score metrics actually differ substantially along these separate dimensions.

Conclusion: MPCEval demonstrates that multi-party conversation generation requires multi-dimensional, task-aware evaluation. Single aggregate scores obscure important behavioral differences, while the proposed reference-free, decomposed metrics uncover strengths and weaknesses in participation, content dynamics, and role alignment. The publicly released implementation and code enable standardized, scalable evaluation and benchmarking for future research on multi-party conversational AI.

Abstract: Multi-party conversation generation, such as smart reply and collaborative assistants, is an increasingly important capability of generative AI, yet its evaluation remains a critical bottleneck. Compared to two-party dialogue, multi-party settings introduce distinct challenges, including complex turn-taking, role-dependent speaker behavior, long-range conversational structure, and multiple equally valid continuations. Accordingly, we introduce MPCEval, a task-aware evaluation and benchmarking suite for multi-party conversation generation. MPCEval decomposes generation quality into speaker modeling, content quality, and speaker--content consistency, and explicitly distinguishes local next-turn prediction from global full-conversation generation. It provides novel, quantitative, reference-free, and reproducible metrics that scale across datasets and models. We apply MPCEval to diverse public and real-world datasets and evaluate modern generation methods alongside human-authored conversations. The results reveal systematic, dimension-specific model characteristics in participation balance, content progression and novelty, and speaker--content consistency, demonstrating that evaluation objectives critically shape model assessment and that single-score evaluation obscures fundamental differences in multi-party conversational behavior. The implementation of MPCEval and the associated evaluation code are publicly available at https://github.com/Owen-Yang-18/MPCEval.

### [45] [VRM: Teaching Reward Models to Understand Authentic Human Preferences](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.04974)
*Biao Liu,Ning Xu,Junming Yang,Hao Xu,Xin Geng*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces Variational Reward Modeling (VRM), a new reward modeling framework for aligning large language models that mimics how humans evaluate responses, and shows it better captures true human preferences than standard scalar reward models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing reward models for aligning LLMs map prompt-response pairs directly to a single scalar score. This leads to reward hacking and spurious correlations, because the models fail to reflect the complex, multi-objective, context-dependent way humans actually judge responses. The authors are motivated to design a reward model that more faithfully represents human evaluation, thereby improving alignment and robustness.

Method: The authors propose VRM (Variational Reward Modeling), which explicitly models human preference evaluation with latent variables. It introduces (1) high-dimensional objective-weight variables that depend on the prompt context and represent how humans weigh different objectives, and (2) low-dimensional semantic-feature variables that capture aspects like logical coherence and contextual appropriateness. These latent variables are inferred via variational inference within a probabilistic framework, and the reward is computed based on them instead of a direct prompt-response-to-scalar mapping. They also perform theoretical analysis of its generalization properties.

Result: Theoretical analysis shows that VRM achieves a tighter generalization error bound than traditional scalar reward models, indicating better generalization to unseen data. Empirically, experiments on benchmark datasets demonstrate that VRM more accurately captures human preferences and significantly outperforms prior reward modeling approaches on alignment-related metrics.

Conclusion: VRM, by introducing latent objective weights and semantic features and learning them via variational inference, more closely mirrors human evaluation processes than standard reward models. This leads to better generalization guarantees and superior empirical performance in capturing authentic human preferences, suggesting VRM is a promising direction for more robust and human-aligned LLM reward modeling.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) have achieved remarkable success across diverse natural language tasks, yet the reward models employed for aligning LLMs often encounter challenges of reward hacking, where the approaches predominantly rely on directly mapping prompt-response pairs to scalar scores, which may inadvertently capture spurious correlations rather than authentic human preferences. In contrast, human evaluation employs a sophisticated process that initially weighs the relative importance of multiple high-dimensional objectives according to the prompt context, subsequently evaluating response quality through low-dimensional semantic features such as logical coherence and contextual appropriateness. Motivated by this consideration, we propose VRM, i.e., Variational Reward Modeling, a novel framework that explicitly models the evaluation process of human preference judgments by incorporating both high-dimensional objective weights and low-dimensional semantic features as latent variables, which are inferred through variational inference techniques. Additionally, we provide a theoretical analysis showing that VRM can achieve a tighter generalization error bound compared to the traditional reward model. Extensive experiments on benchmark datasets demonstrate that VRM significantly outperforms existing methods in capturing authentic human preferences.

Conclusion: Safety alignment in LLMs is significantly weaker for Thai, particularly for culture-specific adversarial prompts, than standard safety benchmarks would suggest. Open-source models are generally less robust than closed-source ones under these Thai-language and Thai-context attacks. ThaiSafetyBench and ThaiSafetyClassifier provide an open, reproducible toolkit and leaderboard for evaluating and tracking LLM safety in Thai, and the findings underscore the need for culturally aware safety training and evaluation beyond English.

Abstract: The safety evaluation of large language models (LLMs) remains largely centered on English, leaving non-English languages and culturally grounded risks underexplored. The dataset covers both general harmful prompts and attacks that are explicitly grounded in Thai cultural, social, and contextual nuances.
  Using ThaiSafetyBench, we evaluate 24 LLMs, with GPT-4.1 and Gemini-2.5-Pro serving as LLM-as-a-judge evaluators. Our results show that closed-source models generally demonstrate stronger safety performance than open-source counterparts, raising important concerns regarding the robustness of openly available models. Moreover, we observe a consistently higher Attack Success Rate (ASR) for Thai-specific, culturally contextualized attacks compared to general Thai-language attacks, highlighting a critical vulnerability in current safety alignment methods.
  To improve reproducibility and cost efficiency, we further fine-tune a DeBERTa-based harmful response classifier, which we name ThaiSafetyClassifier. The model achieves a weighted F1 score of 84.4%, matching GPT-4.1 judgments. We publicly release the fine-tuning weights and training scripts to support reproducibility. Finally, we introduce the ThaiSafetyBench leaderboard to provide continuously updated safety evaluations and encourage community participation.
  - ThaiSafetyBench HuggingFace Dataset: https://huggingface.co/datasets/typhoon-ai/ThaiSafetyBench

Method: The paper introduces HiFlow, a two-level optimization framework: a planning layer that models global structure and constraints, and a generation layer that produces text conditioned on this plan. It uses constraint-aware plan screening and closed-loop feedback between and within the two layers to iteratively refine both the plan and the generated text toward better constraint satisfaction and quality.

Result: Across multiple LLM backbones and benchmark tasks for constrained long-text generation, HiFlow outperforms baseline methods, indicating better global consistency, local coherence, and constraint satisfaction.

Conclusion: Treating constrained long-text generation as a hierarchical, feedback-driven optimization problem allows more effective coordination between planning and generation, leading to higher quality, more constraint-compliant outputs than traditional static planning or purely offline-supervised approaches.

Abstract: Large language models perform well in short text generation but still struggle with long text generation, particularly under complex constraints. Such tasks involve multiple tightly coupled objectives, including global structural consistency, local semantic coherence, and constraint feasibility, forming a challenging constrained optimization problem. Existing approaches mainly rely on static planning or offline supervision, limiting effective coordination between global and local objectives during generation. To address these challenges, we propose HiFlow, a hierarchical feedback-driven optimization framework for constrained long text generation. HiFlow formulates generation as a two-level optimization process, consisting of a planning layer for global structure and constraint modeling, and a generation layer for conditioned text generation. By incorporating constraint-aware plan screening and closed-loop feedback at both levels, HiFlow enables joint optimization of planning quality and generation behavior, progressively guiding the model toward high-quality, constraint-satisfying outputs. Experiments on multiple backbones confirm HiFlow's effectiveness over baseline methods.

### [48] [NeuronMoE: Neuron-Guided Mixture-of-Experts for Efficient Multilingual LLM Extension](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.05046)
*Rongzhi Li,Hitomi Yanaka*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: NeuronMoE allocates mixture-of-experts capacity at the neuron level using cross-lingual neuron diversity, reducing parameters ~40% for low-resource languages while maintaining performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Scaling LLMs to many low-resource languages is too costly if each language needs a separate model. MoE helps by sharing a base model and adding sparse, language-specific experts, but existing methods decide expert counts per layer using coarse, layer-level similarity, ignoring that linguistic specialization happens at the neuron level. A finer-grained, empirically grounded way to decide where expert capacity is actually needed across the network is missing.

Method: The authors introduce NeuronMoE, which first identifies language-specific neurons across all transformer components for each language. They measure cross-lingual neuron diversity per layer, capturing how differently neurons behave for different languages. Based on these diversity statistics, they allocate MoE experts per layer, giving more experts to layers with higher cross-lingual neuron diversity and fewer to more shared layers. They apply this to adapt Llama-3.2-3B to low-resource languages (Greek, Turkish, Hungarian) and compare against a LayerMoE baseline that uses layer-level similarity only.

Result: Using NeuronMoE on Llama-3.2-3B for Greek, Turkish, and Hungarian yields about a 40% reduction in language-specific parameters on average, while preserving performance comparable to a LayerMoE baseline. Analysis of learned experts shows that low-resource language experts develop neuron specialization structures similar to those in the high-resource language experts, with specialization especially concentrated in the earliest and latest transformer layers.

Conclusion: Neuron-level analysis of cross-lingual diversity is an effective signal for allocating MoE experts across layers in multilingual LLMs. It enables substantial parameter savings without sacrificing performance and suggests that multilingual models organize linguistic knowledge according to shared architectural principles, with key language-specific specializations emerging in early and late layers.

Abstract: Extending large language models to low-resource languages is essential for global accessibility, but training separate models per language is prohibitively expensive. Mixture-of-Experts (MoE) architectures address this by adding sparse language-specific parameters, but determining how many experts each layer needs remains an open question. Current approaches allocate experts based on layer-level similarity, yet language processing exhibits fine-grained specialization at individual neurons. We propose $\textbf{NeuronMoE}$, a method that analyzes language-specific neurons across all transformer components to guide expert allocation per layer based on empirically measured cross-lingual neuron diversity. Applied to Llama-3.2-3B for low-resource languages (Greek, Turkish, and Hungarian), this approach achieves approximately 40% average parameter reduction while matching the performance of the LayerMoE baseline. We find that low-resource language experts independently develop neuron specialization patterns mirroring the high-resource language, which are concentrated in early and late layers. This reveals potential universal architectural principles in how multilingual models organize linguistic knowledge.
