### [50] [ARC-TGI: Human-Validated Task Generators with Reasoning Chain Templates for ARC-AGI](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.05099)
*Jens Lehmann,Syeda Khushbakht,Nikoo Salehfard,Nur A Zarin Nishat,Dhananjay Bhandiwad,Andrei Aioanei,Sahar Vahdati*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They introduce ARC-TGI, a generator-based framework that creates many varied ARC-style reasoning tasks with built-in explanations, enabling scalable and less overfitted evaluation of abstract visual reasoning.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: ARC-AGI evaluates few-shot abstraction and rule induction on visual grids, but current benchmarks use a fixed set of hand-authored puzzles. This causes overfitting, leakage, and memorisation, making it hard to measure true progress and generalisation. The authors want a way to systematically generate many related tasks that share an underlying rule, better matching human-solvable ARC properties and supporting rigorous, scalable evaluation.

Method: They design ARC-TGI, a library of task-family generators written as compact Python programs. Each generator samples diverse ARC-style tasks while preserving a latent transformation rule. For each generated task, ARC-TGI provides: (1) natural-language descriptions of the input and the reasoning steps of the transformation; (2) partially evaluated Python code that implements sampling, transformations, and episode construction. The framework enforces task-level constraints so that sets of training examples collectively reveal enough variations for a solver to infer the underlying rule, overcoming the limitations of independent per-example sampling. They also apply human refinement and local verification procedures to ensure that both the grid patterns and textual reasoning traces remain natural, correct, and robust under variation.

Result: They build and release an inventory of 461 such generators. These cover multiple existing ARC benchmarks: 180 ARC-Mini tasks, 215 ARC-AGI-1 tasks (200 train and 15 test), and 66 ARC-AGI-2 tasks (55 train and 11 test). This inventory makes it possible to sample large, diverse datasets and design controlled benchmarks for abstract visual reasoning systems, all grounded in well-specified rule families with aligned explanations and code.

Conclusion: ARC-TGI turns static ARC-style benchmarks into a generative, extensible resource: instead of a fixed puzzle set prone to overfitting, researchers get principled families of tasks with explicit rules, language reasoning chains, and executable code. This supports scalable training and evaluation, better control over what variations are exposed to models, and more faithful alignment with the human-oriented design principles of ARC tasks.

Abstract: The Abstraction and Reasoning Corpus (ARC-AGI) probes few-shot abstraction and rule induction on small visual grids, but progress is difficult to measure on static collections of hand-authored puzzles due to overfitting, dataset leakage, and memorisation. We introduce ARC-TGI (ARC Task Generators Inventory), an open-source framework for task-family generators: compact Python programs that sample diverse ARC-AGI tasks while preserving a latent rule. ARC-TGI is built around a solver-facing representation: each generated task is paired with natural-language input and transformation reasoning chains and partially evaluated Python code implementing sampling, transformation, and episode construction. Crucially, ARC-TGI supports task-level constraints so that training examples collectively expose the variations needed to infer the underlying rule, a requirement for human-solvable ARC tasks that independent per-example sampling often fails to guarantee. All generators undergo human refinement and local verification to keep both grids and reasoning traces natural and consistent under variation. We release 461 generators covering 180 ARC-Mini tasks, 215 ARC-AGI-1 tasks (200 train, 15 test), and 66 ARC-AGI-2 tasks (55 train, 11 test), enabling scalable dataset sampling and controlled benchmarking.

</details>


### [51] [Measuring the Redundancy of Decoder Layers in SpeechLLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.05121)
*Adel Moumen,Guangzhi Sun,Philip C Woodland*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper investigates how much of the LLM decoder in Speech LLMs is actually necessary, finding substantial redundant capacity that can be safely pruned while preserving performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Speech LLMs reuse large text LLM decoders, which dominate parameter counts and computational cost. It is unclear how much of this decoder capacity is truly needed for speech tasks like ASR and speech translation. Understanding and reducing redundancy could lower inference cost and enable efficient multi-task Speech LLM backbones.

Method: The authors analyze decoder redundancy in Speech LLMs across two LLM families and three model scales (1B–8B parameters). They compare redundancy patterns for text vs. speech inputs, perform structured pruning of decoder layers, and study post-pruning “healing” behavior (fine-tuning or adaptation) to gauge robustness. They extend the analysis from ASR to speech translation and across different speech encoders and languages to test how general the redundancy patterns are.

Result: They find that redundancy in Speech LLM decoders is largely inherited from the underlying pretrained text LLM, with similar sets of redundant blocks for both text and speech inputs. For 7–8B models, good ASR performance is maintained even after pruning down to about 60% of decoder layers; smaller models show the same trend but tolerate less pruning. The same layer blocks tend to be redundant across different speech encoders, tasks (ASR and speech translation), and languages.

Conclusion: A substantial portion of the decoder in Speech LLMs is redundant, and this redundancy follows a global structure inherited from the base LLM. This enables aggressive pruning of decoder layers while retaining strong performance, and suggests that a single pruned decoder backbone can support multiple speech tasks and languages efficiently.

Abstract: Speech Large Language Models route speech encoder representations into an LLM decoder that typically accounts for over 90% of total parameters. We study how much of this decoder capacity is actually needed for speech tasks. Across two LLM families and three scales (1-8B), we show that decoder redundancy is largely inherited from the pretrained LLM: text and speech inputs yield similar redundant blocks. We then measure excess capacity by pruning decoder layers and analysing post-pruning healing to increase robustness. Our findings show that 7-8B models retain good ASR performance with only 60% of decoder layers, and the same trend extends to smaller scales with reduced pruning tolerance. We then generalise to speech translation, and show that the same blocks of layers are redundant across speech encoders, tasks and languages, indicating that a more global redundancy structure exists, enabling a single pruned and multi-tasks SpeechLLM backbone to be deployed.

Method: They design a hierarchical Large autoBidding Model (LBM) with two components: (1) LBM-Think, a high-level LLM-based module for reasoning and planning about bidding strategies; and (2) LBM-Act, a low-level module that generates precise bid actions. Result: Empirical evaluations show that their LBM-based generative backbone outperforms existing auto-bidding baselines, achieving better bidding performance, more efficient training, and stronger generalization to new or dynamic ad environments. The experiments also indicate that the dual-embedding fusion and GQPO fine-tuning contribute significantly to the gains, especially by stabilizing LLM reasoning and improving action quality.

Conclusion: A hierarchical architecture that separates high-level LLM-based reasoning from low-level action generation, coupled with multimodal (language + numeric) embeddings and offline RL-style fine-tuning, can effectively harness LLMs for auto-bidding in online ad auctions. This design mitigates hallucinations, improves interpretability and generalization, and yields superior practical performance compared with prior black-box offline RL and generative approaches.

Abstract: The growing scale of ad auctions on online advertising platforms has intensified competition, making manual bidding impractical and necessitating auto-bidding to help advertisers achieve their economic goals. Current auto-bidding methods have evolved to use offline reinforcement learning or generative methods to optimize bidding strategies, but they can sometimes behave counterintuitively due to the black-box training manner and limited mode coverage of datasets, leading to challenges in understanding task status and generalization in dynamic ad environments. Large language models (LLMs) offer a promising solution by leveraging prior human knowledge and reasoning abilities to improve auto-bidding performance. However, directly applying LLMs to auto-bidding faces difficulties due to the need for precise actions in competitive auctions and the lack of specialized auto-bidding knowledge, which can lead to hallucinations and suboptimal decisions. To address these challenges, we propose a hierarchical Large autoBidding Model (LBM) to leverage the reasoning capabilities of LLMs for developing a superior auto-bidding strategy. This includes a high-level LBM-Think model for reasoning and a low-level LBM-Act model for action generation. Specifically, we propose a dual embedding mechanism to efficiently fuse two modalities, including language and numerical inputs, for language-guided training of the LBM-Act; then, we propose an offline reinforcement fine-tuning technique termed GQPO for mitigating the LLM-Think's hallucinations and enhancing decision-making performance without simulation or real-world rollout like previous multi-turn LLM-based methods. Experiments demonstrate the superiority of a generative backbone based on our LBM, especially in an efficient training manner and generalization ability.

  <summary>Details</summary>

This paper is motivated by the gap between externally imposed representations (e.g., feature vectors) and human self-representations, and the need for a systematic way to detect and categorize mismatches that might undermine the legitimacy of decisions.

Method: The authors formalize Representation Fidelity as the distance between two representations of the same person: (1) an externally prescribed input representation used by the algorithm to make a decision, and (2) a natural language self-description provided by the human subject and used only for validation. They conceptualize different types of mismatches between these representations and develop a typology that links mismatch types to degrees of fidelity. They then build a benchmark: a corpus of 30,000 synthetic natural language self-descriptions derived from the German Credit Dataset’s loan-applicant records, paired with the original structured representations and annotated by experts for different mismatch types and severities.

Result: The main results are: (a) a formal notion of Representation Fidelity grounded in distances between system representations and human self-descriptions, (b) a generic typology of representation mismatches determining degrees of fidelity, and (c) the Loan-Granting Self-Representations Corpus 2025, a benchmark dataset with 30,000 synthetic self-descriptions and expert annotations of representation mismatches for each applicant in the German Credit Dataset. This provides a concrete resource for training and evaluating models that measure or improve representation fidelity in algorithmic decision-making.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that Representation Fidelity is a meaningful and operationalizable dimension for validating algorithmic decisions about people. By comparing externally imposed representations with individuals’ self-descriptions and categorizing discrepancies, one can assess whether decisions are based on reasonable grounds. The released benchmark in the loan-granting context demonstrates feasibility and is intended to stimulate further research on methods for detecting, quantifying, and ultimately mitigating misrepresentations in human-centric algorithmic systems.

Abstract: This paper introduces a new dimension for validating algorithmic decisions about humans by measuring the fidelity of their representations. Representation Fidelity measures if decisions about a person rest on reasonable grounds. We propose to operationalize this notion by measuring the distance between two representations of the same person: (1) an externally prescribed input representation on which the decision is based, and (2) a self-description provided by the human subject of the decision, used solely to validate the input representation. We examine the nature of discrepancies between these representations, how such discrepancies can be quantified, and derive a generic typology of representation mismatches that determine the degree of representation fidelity. We further present the first benchmark for evaluating representation fidelity based on a dataset of loan-granting decisions. Our Loan-Granting Self-Representations Corpus 2025 consists of a large corpus of 30 000 synthetic natural language self-descriptions derived from corresponding representations of applicants in the German Credit Dataset, along with expert annotations of representation mismatches between each pair of representations.

### [54] [Feature Resemblance: On the Theoretical Understanding of Analogical Reasoning in Transformers](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.05143)
*Ruichen Xu,Wenjing Yan,Ying-Jun Angela Zhang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how analogical reasoning emerges in transformer language models, provides theory showing how it arises from training structure and representation alignment, and validates the theory with experiments up to 1.5B parameters.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing evaluations of reasoning in large language models mix many reasoning types, making it hard to understand what mechanisms underlie specific capabilities. Analogical reasoning, where models infer shared properties from known similarities, is a core form of inductive reasoning but is poorly isolated and understood. The authors aim to give a clean, mechanistic account of how analogical reasoning can emerge in transformers and what training conditions are necessary.

Method: The authors build a theoretical analysis of transformers trained on synthetic data involving similarity relations between entities and attribute attributions. They mathematically characterize when analogical reasoning can be implemented via learned representations, proving conditions on joint versus sequential training and on data structure (e.g., the presence of identity bridges). They also show that two-hop reasoning can be reframed as analogical reasoning under certain conditions. They then run experiments on transformer architectures up to 1.5B parameters to test these predictions, examine learned representation geometries, and connect geometric structure to reasoning behavior.

Result: The theory yields three main results: (1) If models are trained jointly on similarity and attribution premises, they can perform analogical reasoning by aligning representations so that entities with similar properties cluster in representation space; (2) In sequential training, analogical reasoning only emerges when the model first learns the similarity structure before learning specific attribute mappings, indicating a necessary curriculum order; (3) Two-hop reasoning of the form a→b, b→c ⇒ a→c can be reduced to analogical reasoning that relies on explicit identity statements b=b in the training data. Experiments across multiple transformer sizes confirm these theoretical predictions and show that analogical and multi-hop reasoning performance correlates with the geometry of internal representations.

Conclusion: Analogical reasoning in transformers arises from a unified mechanism: entities that share properties are encoded into similar internal representations, allowing attributes to transfer by feature alignment. This mechanism depends critically on how the model is trained—joint vs. sequential training and the order in which relational and attribute information are learned—and on the explicit presence of structural cues like identity bridges in the data. The work clarifies a concrete, mechanistic connection between representational geometry and inductive reasoning in large language models.

Abstract: Understanding reasoning in large language models is complicated by evaluations that conflate multiple reasoning types. We isolate analogical reasoning (inferring shared properties between entities based on known similarities) and analyze its emergence in transformers. We theoretically prove three key results: (1) Joint training on similarity and attribution premises enables analogical reasoning through aligned representations; (2) Sequential training succeeds only when similarity structure is learned before specific attributes, revealing a necessary curriculum; (3) Two-hop reasoning ($a \to b, b \to c \implies a \to c$) reduces to analogical reasoning with identity bridges ($b = b$), which must appear explicitly in training data. These results reveal a unified mechanism: transformers encode entities with similar properties into similar representations, enabling property transfer through feature alignment. Experiments with architectures up to 1.5B parameters validate our theory and demonstrate how representational geometry shapes inductive reasoning capabilities.

</details>


### [55] [C2-Faith: Benchmarking LLM Judges for Causal and Coverage Faithfulness in Chain-of-Thought Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.05167)
*Avni Mittal,Rauno Arike*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: This paper evaluates how reliably large language models can judge the faithfulness of chain-of-thought reasoning, focusing on whether reasoning steps are causally valid and sufficiently complete.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs are widely used as automatic judges of chain-of-thought quality, but it is unclear if they truly verify the reasoning process (process faithfulness) rather than just checking whether the final answer seems plausible. This uncertainty undermines trust in LLM-based evaluation for reasoning tasks.

Method: The authors build C2-Faith, a benchmark derived from PRM800K, targeting two aspects of faithfulness: (1) causality—whether each reasoning step logically follows from previous context, and (2) coverage—whether essential intermediate inferences are present. They construct controlled perturbations by replacing a single step with an acausal variant (yielding known error locations) and by deleting reasoning steps at varying rates to simulate missing coverage. They then test three state-of-the-art LLM judges on three tasks: binary detection of a causal error, localization of the exact erroneous step, and scoring of coverage level compared with reference labels.

Result: The evaluation reveals that: (1) model performance and relative ranking depend heavily on how the judging task is framed; no single LLM judge performs best on all tasks, (2) all judges can often detect that some error exists but perform noticeably worse at pinpointing the specific erroneous step, and (3) judges tend to overestimate coverage, assigning overly positive scores even when key reasoning steps are missing.

Conclusion: LLM judges are not uniformly reliable at assessing process-level faithfulness of chain-of-thought: they are better at coarse error detection than fine-grained localization and systematically overrate the completeness of reasoning. The C2-Faith benchmark exposes these weaknesses and helps practitioners choose and configure LLM judges more appropriately for different process-evaluation use cases.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) are increasingly used as judges of chain-of-thought (CoT) reasoning, but it remains unclear whether they can reliably assess process faithfulness rather than just answer plausibility. We introduce C2-Faith, a benchmark built from PRM800K that targets two complementary dimensions of faithfulness: causality (does each step logically follow from prior context?) and coverage (are essential intermediate inferences present?). Using controlled perturbations, we create examples with known causal error positions by replacing a single step with an acausal variant, and with controlled coverage deletions at varying deletion rates (scored against reference labels). We evaluate three frontier judges under three tasks: binary causal detection, causal step localization, and coverage scoring. The results show that model rankings depend strongly on task framing, with no single judge dominating all settings; all judges exhibit a substantial gap between detecting an error and localizing it; and coverage judgments are systematically inflated for incomplete reasoning. These findings clarify when LLM judges are dependable and where they fail, and provide practical guidance for selecting judges in process-level evaluation

Sparse‑BitNet demonstrates that LLMs can be aggressively quantized and sparsified while remaining trainable and accurate, yielding tangible speedups. This suggests that jointly exploiting extreme quantization and structured sparsity is a promising path toward more efficient large‑scale language models, especially when paired with hardware support such as sparse tensor cores.

Abstract: Semi-structured N:M sparsity and low-bit quantization (e.g., 1.58-bit BitNet) are two promising approaches for improving the efficiency of large language models (LLMs), yet they have largely been studied in isolation. In this work, we investigate their interaction and show that 1.58-bit BitNet is naturally more compatible with N:M sparsity than full-precision models. To study this effect, we propose Sparse-BitNet, a unified framework that jointly applies 1.58-bit quantization and dynamic N:M sparsification while ensuring stable training for the first time. Across multiple model scales and training regimes (sparse pretraining and dense-to-sparse schedules), 1.58-bit BitNet consistently exhibits smaller performance degradation than full-precision baselines at the same sparsity levels and can tolerate higher structured sparsity before accuracy collapse. Moreover, using our custom sparse tensor core, Sparse-BitNet achieves substantial speedups in both training and inference, reaching up to 1.30X. These results highlight that combining extremely low-bit quantization with semi-structured N:M sparsity is a promising direction for efficient LLMs. Code available at https://github.com/AAzdi/Sparse-BitNet

These relations represent positive and negative argumentative connections, conjunctive reasoning structures, the correspondence between legal norms and case facts, and semantic equivalence between propositions. In addition, it establishes a standardized annotation workflow and consistency control mechanisms to ensure reproducibility and reliability of the annotated data. ### [58] [Transducing Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.05193)
*Vésteinn Snæbjarnarson,Samuel Kiegeland,Tianyu Liu,Reda Boumasmoud,Ryan Cotterell,Tim Vieira*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose a general framework to turn any language model plus a deterministic string-to-string transformation into a new, valid language model over the transformed space, using finite-state transducers to propagate probabilities exactly or approximately.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Language models usually define probabilities over a particular string representation (e.g., tokens, BPE, DNA bases), but many applications need outputs in a different format (e.g., words, bytes, amino acids). Existing approaches use deterministic post-processing but don’t rigorously treat the transformed outputs as being generated by an induced probabilistic model or provide general inference methods for conditioning on these transformed outputs.

Method: They formalize deterministic string-to-string mappings as finite-state transducers (FSTs) and treat the language model as a distribution over input strings. By composing the LM with the FST, they define an induced distribution over output strings. They develop algorithms to marginalize over all input strings that map to a given output, effectively pushing the LM probabilities through the FST. They provide an exact composition/marginalization algorithm, an efficient approximate algorithm, and theoretical analysis of their properties and complexity. These algorithms enable conditioning on outputs in the transformed space without retraining or changing LM parameters.

Result: They implement the framework in three settings: (1) converting token-level language models to byte-level models, (2) converting token-level models to word-level models, and (3) converting DNA-sequence models to amino-acid-sequence models. Across these domains, the methods successfully adapt pretrained LMs at inference time to operate in the desired output space, achieving correct probability calculations and practical efficiency, with approximation methods trading accuracy for speed as analyzed.

Conclusion: Deterministic string-to-string transformations, represented as FSTs, can be used to systematically derive new, fully functional language models from existing ones without retraining. Their algorithms for exact and approximate probability propagation through transducers enable principled inference and conditioning in the transformed space, allowing flexible adaptation of pretrained models to diverse application-specific output requirements.

Abstract: Modern language models define distributions over strings, but downstream tasks often require different output formats. For instance, a model that generates byte-pair strings does not directly produce word-level predictions, and a DNA model does not directly produce amino-acid sequences. In such cases, a deterministic string-to-string transformation can convert the model's output to the desired form. This is a familiar pattern in probability theory: applying a function $f$ to a random variable $X\sim p$ yields a transformed random variable $f(X)$ with an induced distribution. While such transformations are occasionally used in language modeling, prior work does not treat them as yielding new, fully functional language models. We formalize this perspective and introduce a general framework for language models derived from deterministic string-to-string transformations. We focus on transformations representable as finite-state transducers -- a commonly used state-machine abstraction for efficient string-to-string mappings. We develop algorithms that compose a language model with an FST to *marginalize* over source strings mapping to a given target, propagating probabilities through the transducer without altering model parameters and enabling *conditioning* on transformed outputs. We present an exact algorithm, an efficient approximation, and a theoretical analysis. We conduct experiments in three domains: converting language models from tokens to bytes, from tokens to words, and from DNA to amino acids. These experiments demonstrate inference-time adaptation of pretrained language models to match application-specific output requirements.

</details>


### [59] [Diffusion LLMs can think EoS-by-EoS](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.05197)
*Sarah Breckner,Sebastian Schuster*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper shows that diffusion-based large language models perform better on complex reasoning tasks when allowed to generate many extra end-of-sequence (EoS) tokens, which they seem to use as a hidden scratchpad for intermediate computation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Diffusion LLMs sometimes outperform autoregressive models on reasoning tasks, particularly when they generate longer outputs padded with EoS tokens. It is unclear why unnecessary extra tokens should help reasoning, and whether these EoS tokens carry meaningful intermediate computations, motivating a systematic study of their role.

Method: The authors study several diffusion LLMs (LLaDA1.5, LLaDA2.0-mini, Dream-v0) on structured reasoning tasks (Addition, Entity Tracking, Sudoku). They run controlled prompting experiments where they vary the number of EoS tokens appended to the output. They then perform causal interventions by patching the hidden states of EoS tokens with those from counterfactual generations and observe how this affects final outputs.

Result: Adding extra EoS tokens reliably improves performance of diffusion LLMs on the tested reasoning tasks. Moreover, when the hidden representations of the EoS tokens are replaced by those from a counterfactual run, the models’ final answers often switch to the counterfactual solution, demonstrating that EoS representations encode crucial problem-specific information.

Conclusion: Diffusion LLMs do not treat EoS tokens as empty padding; instead, they appear to "think EoS-by-EoS," using EoS token representations as a hidden scratchpad to carry out intermediate reasoning. This provides evidence that extending generation length in diffusion LLMs can enhance reasoning by offering more internal computation space, even if the externally visible answer does not require that many tokens.

Abstract: Diffusion LLMs have been proposed as an alternative to autoregressive LLMs, excelling especially at complex reasoning tasks with interdependent sub-goals. Curiously, this is particularly true if the generation length, i.e., the number of tokens the model has to output, is set to a much higher value than is required for providing the correct answer to the task, and the model pads its answer with end-of-sequence (EoS) tokens. We hypothesize that diffusion models think EoS-by-EoS, that is, they use the representations of EoS tokens as a hidden scratchpad, which allows them to solve harder reasoning problems. We experiment with the diffusion models LLaDA1.5, LLaDA2.0-mini, and Dream-v0 on the tasks Addition, Entity Tracking, and Sudoku. In a controlled prompting experiment, we confirm that adding EoS tokens improves the LLMs' reasoning capabilities. To further verify whether they serve as space for hidden computations, we patch the hidden states of the EoS tokens with those of a counterfactual generation, which frequently changes the generated output to the counterfactual. The success of the causal intervention underscores that the EoS tokens, which one may expect to be devoid of meaning, carry information on the problem to solve. The behavioral experiments and the causal interventions indicate that diffusion LLMs can indeed think EoS-by-EoS.

### [60] [Distilling Formal Logic into Neural Spaces: A Kernel Alignment Approach for Signal Temporal Logic](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.05198)
*Sara Candussio,Gabriele Sarti,Gaia Saveri,Luca Bortolussi*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They distill a symbolic robustness kernel for formal specifications into a neural Transformer encoder, learning continuous embeddings that preserve semantic similarity of formulas while being fast, scalable, and invertible.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing methods to compare or embed formal specifications are either symbolic kernels, which preserve behavioral semantics but are slow, require anchors, and are non-invertible, or syntax-based neural embeddings that are fast but miss the true semantic structure. There is a need for a representation that is both semantically faithful and computationally efficient, and ideally invertible, to support scalable neuro-symbolic reasoning and formula reconstruction.

Method: They use a teacher-student framework where a symbolic robustness kernel over formal specifications (here, Signal Temporal Logic formulas) acts as the teacher, and a Transformer encoder is the student. Instead of traditional contrastive learning, they define a continuous, geometric alignment loss weighted by the kernel, so that embedding errors are penalized in proportion to semantic discrepancies. The Transformer is trained so that its learned latent space reproduces the geometry induced by the kernel. After training, the encoder can compute embeddings in a single forward pass, approximating the kernel’s similarity behavior. They also leverage the structure of the learned latent space to reconstruct formulas (invertibility).

Result: The trained Transformer encoder produces latent representations in one forward pass that closely match the semantic similarity structure defined by the expensive robustness kernel. On Signal Temporal Logic, the learned embeddings preserve semantic similarity relations between formulas, can accurately predict robustness values and constraint satisfaction outcomes, and are shown to be intrinsically invertible, enabling formula reconstruction from embeddings.

Conclusion: By distilling a symbolic robustness kernel into a neural encoder with a continuous geometric alignment objective, the paper achieves fast, semantically faithful, and invertible representations of formal specifications. This allows scalable neuro-symbolic reasoning and reconstruction of logical formulas without repeatedly computing costly kernels at inference time, effectively combining the strengths of symbolic kernels and neural embeddings.

Abstract: We introduce a framework for learning continuous neural representations of formal specifications by distilling the geometry of their semantics into a latent space. Existing approaches rely either on symbolic kernels -- which preserve behavioural semantics but are computationally prohibitive, anchor-dependent, and non-invertible -- or on syntax-based neural embeddings that fail to capture underlying structures. Our method bridges this gap: using a teacher-student setup, we distill a symbolic robustness kernel into a Transformer encoder. Unlike standard contrastive methods, we supervise the model with a continuous, kernel-weighted geometric alignment objective that penalizes errors in proportion to their semantic discrepancies. Once trained, the encoder produces embeddings in a single forward pass, effectively mimicking the kernel's logic at a fraction of its computational cost. We apply our framework to Signal Temporal Logic (STL), demonstrating that the resulting neural representations faithfully preserve the semantic similarity of STL formulae, accurately predict robustness and constraint satisfaction, and remain intrinsically invertible. Our proposed approach enables highly efficient, scalable neuro-symbolic reasoning and formula reconstruction without repeated kernel computation at runtime.

</details>


### [61] [Balancing Coverage and Draft Latency in Vocabulary Trimming for Faster Speculative Decoding](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.05210)
*Ofir Ben Shoham*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes a method to speed up speculative decoding for large language models by aggressively trimming the draft model’s vocabulary in a principled way, preserving accuracy while reducing latency and increasing throughput.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Speculative decoding uses a small draft model to propose tokens that are then verified by a larger target model, but the draft model often becomes the latency bottleneck. Its language modeling head becomes especially expensive as vocabulary size grows. Designers face a trade-off: larger vocabularies improve agreement with the target model but slow down inference; smaller vocabularies are faster but risk missing needed tokens and hurting draft quality. Moreover, many domain-specific applications use only a small subset of the full vocabulary, suggesting that a tailored vocabulary could yield large efficiency gains without major accuracy loss.

Method: The authors introduce vocabulary trimming for draft models used in speculative decoding, formulated as a constrained optimization problem that trades off token coverage against latency. They measure coverage by examining which tokens appear in assistant responses within the training data, and estimate latency using architecture-aware FLOPs focused on the cost of the language modeling head as a function of vocabulary size. They define a utility function that encodes the desired trade-off and apply Tree-structured Parzen Estimator (TPE) optimization to explore candidate trimmed vocabularies along the coverage-latency Pareto frontier, subject to a minimum required coverage constraint. This produces a tailored reduced vocabulary for the draft model while keeping the rest of the architecture fixed.

Result: With optimized trimmed vocabularies, the draft models retain high token coverage while drastically reducing the size of the language modeling head—up to 97% reduction in vocabulary size. This leads to substantial performance gains in speculative decoding: on domain-specific tasks, draft latency is reduced by up to 16%, and end-to-end throughput increases by up to 20%. Even on diverse out-of-distribution tasks, where the vocabulary is less perfectly matched, the method still yields up to 6.7% throughput improvements, demonstrating robustness beyond the training domain.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that carefully optimizing the draft model’s vocabulary is an effective way to break the coverage-latency trade-off in speculative decoding. By viewing vocabulary selection as a constrained optimization over token coverage and architecture-aware latency, and solving it with TPE, they can aggressively shrink the draft vocabulary while preserving the tokens that matter most. This significantly accelerates speculative decoding with minimal loss in effectiveness, particularly for domain-specific workloads that naturally use a limited token set, and still yields benefits on broader tasks.

Abstract: Speculative decoding accelerates inference for Large Language Models by using a lightweight draft model to propose candidate tokens that are verified in parallel by a larger target model. Prior work shows that the draft model often dominates speculative decoding latency, since it generates tokens sequentially and incurs high cost from its language modeling head as vocabulary size grows. This exposes a fundamental trade-off in draft model design: larger vocabularies improve token coverage and agreement with the target model, but incur higher draft latency, while smaller vocabularies reduce latency at the risk of missing tokens required for accurate draft generation. We address this trade-off through vocabulary trimming for draft models, motivated by the observation that domain-specific workloads use only a small fraction of the full vocabulary. We cast draft vocabulary selection as a constrained optimization problem that balances token coverage and draft latency. Coverage is computed over assistant responses in the training data, while latency is estimated using architecture-aware FLOPs that capture the cost of the language modeling head as a function of vocabulary size. We optimize a utility function with a Tree-structured Parzen Estimator to efficiently explore the coverage-latency Pareto frontier under a minimum coverage constraint. Experiments show improved speculative decoding throughput while reducing draft vocabularies by up to 97% with high coverage. On domain-specific tasks, we achieve up to 16% latency reduction and 20% throughput improvement, and up to 6.7% throughput gains on diverse out-of-distribution tasks.

Abstract: We present the Multilingual Cloud Corpus, the first national-scale, parallel, multimodal linguistic dataset of Bangladesh's ethnic and indigenous languages. Post-field processing included IPA transcription by 10 linguists with independent adjudication by 6 reviewers. The complete dataset is publicly accessible through the Multilingual Cloud platform (multiling.cloud), providing searchable access to annotated audio and textual data for all documented varieties. We describe the corpus design, fieldwork methodology, dataset structure, and per-language coverage, and discuss implications for endangered language documentation, low-resource NLP, and digital preservation in linguistically diverse developing countries.

Abstract: Assessing whether an article supports an assertion is essential for hallucination detection and claim verification. While large language models (LLMs) have the potential to automate this task, achieving strong performance requires frontier models such as GPT-5 that are prohibitively expensive to deploy at scale. To efficiently perform biomedical evidence attribution, we present Med-V1, a family of small language models with only three billion parameters. Trained on high-quality synthetic data newly developed in this study, Med-V1 substantially outperforms (+27.0% to +71.3%) its base models on five biomedical benchmarks unified into a verification format. Despite its smaller size, Med-V1 performs comparably to frontier LLMs such as GPT-5, along with high-quality explanations for its predictions. We use Med-V1 to conduct a first-of-its-kind use case study that quantifies hallucinations in LLM-generated answers under different citation instructions. Results show that the format instruction strongly affects citation validity and hallucination, with GPT-5 generating more claims but exhibiting hallucination rates similar to GPT-4o. Additionally, we present a second use case showing that Med-V1 can automatically identify high-stakes evidence misattributions in clinical practice guidelines, revealing potentially negative public health impacts that are otherwise challenging to identify at scale. Overall, Med-V1 provides an efficient and accurate lightweight alternative to frontier LLMs for practical and real-world applications in biomedical evidence attribution and verification tasks. Med-V1 is available at https://github.com/ncbi-nlp/Med-V1.

### [68] [DiSCTT: Consensus-Guided Self-Curriculum for Efficient Test-Time Adaptation in Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.05357)
*Mohammad Mahdi Moradi,Sudhir Mudur*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces DiSCTT, a difficulty-aware, consensus-guided self-curriculum method that improves test-time adaptation for reasoning LLMs by tailoring optimization strategies to each input’s estimated difficulty and uncertainty.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing test-time adaptation methods for large language models use a single, uniform optimization strategy for all test inputs. This is inefficient and can be unstable because reasoning tasks vary widely in difficulty and uncertainty. The authors want a way to leverage the model’s own signals (like trajectory agreement) to recognize which instances are easy vs. hard and adjust the adaptation strategy accordingly, improving both performance and efficiency without extra supervision.

Method: They propose DiSCTT, which first samples multiple reasoning trajectories for each input and measures epistemic uncertainty via their agreement (consensus). For high-consensus (easy/low-uncertainty) instances, DiSCTT performs supervised fine-tuning at test time, using majority-voted answers as pseudo-labels. For low-consensus (hard/high-uncertainty) instances, it uses reinforcement learning at test time with a consensus-regularized objective that encourages diverse yet relevant reasoning. This forms a self-curriculum where the adaptation strategy is dynamically chosen per instance based on difficulty.

Result: On multiple mathematical and general reasoning benchmarks, DiSCTT outperforms strong test-time adaptation baselines. It yields higher accuracy, reduced performance variance, and significantly lower computational and wall-clock adaptation cost compared with prior methods that use uniform objectives across inputs.

Conclusion: Incorporating instance-level difficulty and epistemic uncertainty into test-time adaptation leads to more stable, efficient, and effective improvement of reasoning in large language models. DiSCTT’s difficulty-aware, consensus-guided self-curriculum shows that tailoring adaptation strategies to each input is better than applying a single optimization objective to all test cases.

Abstract: Test-time adaptation offers a promising avenue for improving reasoning performance in large language models without additional supervision, but existing approaches often apply a uniform optimization objective across all inputs, leading to inefficient or unstable adaptation on heterogeneous reasoning problems. We propose DiSCTT, a difficulty-aware, consensus-guided self-curriculum framework that dynamically allocates test-time optimization strategies based on instance-level epistemic uncertainty estimated from agreement among sampled reasoning trajectories. Inputs with high consensus are consolidated via supervised fine-tuning using majority-agreed solutions as pseudo-labels, while low-consensus inputs are optimized via reinforcement learning with a consensus-regularized objective that encourages diversity under relevance constraints. Across a broad suite of mathematical and general reasoning benchmarks, DiSCTT consistently outperforms strong test-time adaptation baselines, achieving higher accuracy with reduced variance and substantially lower computation and wall-clock training times. These results demonstrate that explicitly accounting for instance difficulty and uncertainty enables more stable, efficient, and effective test-time adaptation for reasoning models.

</details>


### [69] [Progressive Residual Warmup for Language Model Pretraining](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.05369)
*Tianhao Chen,Xin Xu,Lu Yin,Hao Chen,Yang Wang,Shizhe Diao,Can Yang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces Progressive Residual Warmup (ProRes), a training scheme for Transformers where residual connections of deeper layers are gradually activated over time, leading to more stable and faster pretraining and better downstream performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Transformer-based Large Language Models often face issues with pretraining stability and slow convergence, especially as depth and scale increase. Because layers are sequentially dependent, allowing all layers to learn aggressively from the start can destabilize optimization and hinder efficient use of compute. The authors are motivated to exploit this hierarchical dependency to improve training dynamics, stability, and final task performance.

Method: The authors propose Progressive Residual Warmup (ProRes), which applies a time-dependent scalar to each layer’s residual connection. For each Transformer layer, the residual output is multiplied by a scalar that starts at 0 and is gradually increased to 1 during pretraining. Shallower layers reach full strength earlier, while deeper layers have longer warmup schedules. This effectively implements an "early layers learn first" regime, where upper layers remain partially inactive until lower layers have stabilized. The method is tested across multiple model sizes and combined with different normalization and initialization schemes.

Result: Experiments on language model pretraining show that ProRes stabilizes optimization, reduces training instabilities, and enables faster convergence compared to standard training. The approach yields stronger generalization and improved downstream task performance across various model scales and with diverse normalization/initialization settings, indicating robustness of the method.

Conclusion: Progressively warming up residual connections by depth in Transformer models leads to a more stable and efficient pretraining process. By letting earlier layers converge to a stable representation space before fully activating deeper layers, ProRes creates a favorable optimization trajectory that accelerates convergence and improves downstream performance. The technique is simple to implement and broadly applicable across different Transformer configurations.

Abstract: Transformer architectures serve as the backbone for most modern Large Language Models, therefore their pretraining stability and convergence speed are of central concern. Motivated by the logical dependency of sequentially stacked layers, we propose Progressive Residual Warmup (ProRes) for language model pretraining. ProRes implements an "early layer learns first" philosophy by multiplying each layer's residual with a scalar that gradually warms up from 0 to 1, with deeper layers taking longer warmup steps. In this way, deeper layers wait for early layers to settle into a more stable regime before contributing to learning. We demonstrate the effectiveness of ProRes through pretraining experiments across various model scales, as well as normalization and initialization schemes. Comprehensive analysis shows that ProRes not only stabilizes pretraining but also introduces a unique optimization trajectory, leading to faster convergence, stronger generalization and better downstream performance. Our code is available at https://github.com/dandingsky/ProRes.

</details>


### [70] [An Exploration-Analysis-Disambiguation Reasoning Framework for Word Sense Disambiguation with Low-Parameter LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.05400)

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper shows that carefully fine-tuned small LLMs (<4B parameters) can match or surpass larger models like GPT-4-Turbo for word sense disambiguation by leveraging reasoning-focused training and Chain-of-Thought techniques.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Word Sense Disambiguation is still difficult, especially for rare and domain-specific word senses. While large LLMs perform well, they are computationally expensive and energy-intensive, which limits their practical use. The authors want to know whether much smaller, cheaper models can achieve similar WSD performance if trained with the right strategies, focusing on explicit reasoning.

Method: The authors use the FEWS WSD dataset and augment it with semi-automated annotations that include rationales explaining sense choices. They fine-tune eight small open-source LLMs (e.g., Gemma, Qwen) with fewer than 4B parameters using reasoning-centric strategies such as Chain-of-Thought prompting and neighbour-word analysis. They then compare these fine-tuned models against GPT-4-Turbo (in zero-shot mode), medium-parameter baselines, and prior state-of-the-art systems. They also test cross-domain generalization on the "Fool Me If You Can" dataset without task-specific fine-tuning.

Result: Chain-of-Thought-based reasoning combined with neighbour-word analysis allows small LLMs to reach performance comparable to GPT-4-Turbo in zero-shot WSD. Specifically, Gemma-3-4B and Qwen-3-4B consistently outperform all medium-sized baselines and existing state-of-the-art models on the FEWS dataset while maintaining strong generalization to unseen senses. On the out-of-domain "Fool Me If You Can" dataset, these models still perform strongly without additional fine-tuning, indicating good cross-domain adaptability.

Conclusion: Reasoning-centric fine-tuning, especially with CoT and neighbour-word analysis, enables low-parameter LLMs to deliver accurate, robust word sense disambiguation while greatly reducing computation and energy costs. This suggests that carefully designed training and reasoning strategies can partially substitute for sheer model scale in WSD tasks, making small LLMs a practical and efficient alternative to very large models.

Abstract: Word Sense Disambiguation (WSD) remains a key challenge in Natural Language Processing (NLP), especially when dealing with rare or domain-specific senses that are often misinterpreted. While modern high-parameter Large Language Models (LLMs) such as GPT-4-Turbo have shown state-of-the-art WSD performance, their computational and energy demands limit scalability. This study investigates whether low-parameter LLMs (<4B parameters) can achieve comparable results through fine-tuning strategies that emphasize reasoning-driven sense identification. Using the FEWS dataset augmented with semi-automated, rationale-rich annotations, we fine-tune eight small-scale open-source LLMs (e.g. Gemma and Qwen). Our results reveal that Chain-of-Thought (CoT)-based reasoning combined with neighbour-word analysis achieves performance comparable to GPT-4-Turbo in zero-shot settings. Importantly, Gemma-3-4B and Qwen-3-4B models consistently outperform all medium-parameter baselines and state-of-the-art models on FEWS, with robust generalization to unseen senses. Furthermore, evaluation on the unseen "Fool Me If You Can'' dataset confirms strong cross-domain adaptability without task-specific fine-tuning. This work demonstrates that with carefully crafted reasoning-centric fine-tuning, low-parameter LLMs can deliver accurate WSD while substantially reducing computational and energy demands.

</details>


### [71] [FlashAttention-4: Algorithm and Kernel Pipelining Co-Design for Asymmetric Hardware Scaling](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.05451)
*Ted Zadouri,Markus Hoehnerbach,Jay Shah,Timmy Liu,Vijay Thakkar,Tri Dao*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: FlashAttention-4 redesigns attention kernels for NVIDIA Blackwell GPUs, exploiting their asymmetric scaling to achieve higher utilization and large speedups over cuDNN and Triton, implemented efficiently in a Python-embedded CuTe-DSL.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing attention kernels like FlashAttention-3 are optimized for Hopper (H100) GPUs and become suboptimal on newer Blackwell GPUs whose tensor core performance has scaled faster than other units such as shared memory and exponentiation hardware. This architectural shift creates new bottlenecks for long-context attention and large language models that must be addressed to fully utilize Blackwell hardware.

Method: The authors introduce FlashAttention-4 with several Blackwell-specific optimizations: (1) new kernel pipelines that use fully asynchronous MMA operations and larger tile sizes to better match tensor core characteristics, (2) software-emulated exponentials and conditional softmax rescaling to reduce reliance on slower non-matrix-multiply units, and (3) use of tensor memory and a 2-CTA MMA mode to cut down shared memory traffic and atomic operations in the backward pass. They implement these kernels using the CuTe-DSL embedded in Python for faster compilation and flexible expression of complex pipelines.

Result: On NVIDIA B200 GPUs with BF16, FlashAttention-4 delivers up to 1.3× speedup over cuDNN 9.13 and up to 2.7× over Triton, attaining as much as 1613 TFLOPs/s, which corresponds to about 71% of peak hardware utilization. The implementation in CuTe-DSL achieves 20–30× faster compile times than equivalent C++ template-based kernels while preserving expressivity.

Conclusion: FlashAttention-4 effectively adapts attention computation to the asymmetric scaling characteristics of Blackwell GPUs, shifting work toward tensor cores and away from newly emerging bottlenecks. This yields substantial performance gains for attention workloads and demonstrates that high-performance GPU kernels can be developed in a Python-embedded DSL without sacrificing speed, suggesting a practical path for rapidly evolving kernels alongside GPU architectures.

Abstract: Attention, as a core layer of the ubiquitous Transformer architecture, is the bottleneck for large language models and long-context applications. While FlashAttention-3 optimized attention for Hopper GPUs through asynchronous execution and warp specialization, it primarily targets the H100 architecture. The AI industry has rapidly transitioned to deploying Blackwell-based systems such as the B200 and GB200, which exhibit fundamentally different performance characteristics due to asymmetric hardware scaling: tensor core throughput doubles while other functional units (shared memory bandwidth, exponential units) scale more slowly or remain unchanged. We develop several techniques to address these shifting bottlenecks on Blackwell GPUs: (1) redesigned pipelines that exploit fully asynchronous MMA operations and larger tile sizes, (2) software-emulated exponential and conditional softmax rescaling that reduces non-matmul operations, and (3) leveraging tensor memory and the 2-CTA MMA mode to reduce shared memory traffic and atomic adds in the backward pass. We demonstrate that our method, FlashAttention-4, achieves up to 1.3$\times$ speedup over cuDNN 9.13 and 2.7$\times$ over Triton on B200 GPUs with BF16, reaching up to 1613 TFLOPs/s (71% utilization). Beyond algorithmic innovations, we implement FlashAttention-4 entirely in CuTe-DSL embedded in Python, achieving 20-30$\times$ faster compile times compared to traditional C++ template-based approaches while maintaining full expressivity.

  <summary>Details</summary>

The experiments show that domain-specific fine-tuning on this dataset is crucial for achieving robust performance in a low-resource language like Bangla. This resource can thus support future research and system development for Bangla QA and potentially inform approaches to other low-resource languages.

Abstract: Reading comprehension systems for low-resource languages face significant challenges in handling unanswerable questions. These systems tend to produce unreliable responses when correct answers are absent from context. To solve this problem, we introduce NCTB-QA, a large-scale Bangla question answering dataset comprising 87,805 question-answer pairs extracted from 50 textbooks published by Bangladesh's National Curriculum and Textbook Board. Unlike existing Bangla datasets, NCTB-QA maintains a balanced distribution of answerable (57.25%) and unanswerable (42.75%) questions. NCTB-QA also includes adversarially designed instances containing plausible distractors. We benchmark three transformer-based models (BERT, RoBERTa, ELECTRA) and demonstrate substantial improvements through fine-tuning. BERT achieves 313% relative improvement in F1 score (0.150 to 0.620). Semantic answer quality measured by BERTScore also increases significantly across all models. Our results establish NCTB-QA as a challenging benchmark for Bangla educational question answering. This study demonstrates that domain-specific fine-tuning is critical for robust performance in low-resource settings.

*Artem Vazhentsev,Maria Marina,Daniil Moskovskiy,Sergey Pletenev,Mikhail Seleznyov,Mikhail Salnikov,Elena Tutubalina,Vasily Konovalov,Irina Nikishina,Alexander Panchenko,Viktor Moskvoretskii*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces and evaluates fact-checking methods that work without external retrieval, showing that approaches using internal model representations (especially the proposed INTRA method) outperform logit-based techniques and generalize better across tasks and languages.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing fact-checking solutions for LLMs mostly rely on retrieving external knowledge and then asking an LLM to check if claims match the retrieved evidence. This creates dependencies on retrieval quality and data availability and underuses the model’s own internal knowledge and reasoning. The authors are motivated to understand and exploit LLMs’ intrinsic factual verification abilities and to provide a framework that can systematically evaluate such retrieval-free approaches across diverse and challenging settings.

Method: 1) Formulate the task of “fact-checking without retrieval,” where arbitrary natural language claims must be evaluated for factuality using only the LLM’s internal knowledge. 2) Build a broad evaluation framework that spans 9 datasets and tests generalization across long-tail knowledge, diverse claim sources, multilingual claims, and long-form generations. 3) Systematically compare 18 different methods and 3 underlying models, including logit-based techniques and those that leverage hidden/internal representations. 4) Propose INTRA, a new method that specifically exploits interactions between internal representations inside the model to better distinguish true from false claims and to improve robustness and generalization.

Result: Empirical evaluation shows that methods relying purely on output logits generally underperform methods that tap into internal model representations. The newly proposed INTRA method, which leverages interactions between these internal representations, achieves state-of-the-art performance on the fact-checking-without-retrieval task across the diverse benchmark setup, demonstrating strong generalization across knowledge tails, source variation, languages, and generation types.

Conclusion: Fact-checking without retrieval is both feasible and effective, and it opens a complementary research direction to standard retrieval-based fact-checking. By tapping into internal representations, especially via the INTRA method, systems can achieve better generalization and scalability, reduce reliance on external knowledge sources, and potentially serve as internal reward signals during training or as integrated components in generation pipelines. This supports the view that LLMs’ intrinsic knowledge and representations are valuable resources for improving trustworthiness and factuality in agentic AI systems.

Abstract: Trustworthiness is a core research challenge for agentic AI systems built on Large Language Models (LLMs). To enhance trust, natural language claims from diverse sources, including human-written text, web content, and model outputs, are commonly checked for factuality by retrieving external knowledge and using an LLM to verify the faithfulness of claims to the retrieved evidence. As a result, such methods are constrained by retrieval errors and external data availability, while leaving the models intrinsic fact-verification capabilities largely unused. We propose the task of fact-checking without retrieval, focusing on the verification of arbitrary natural language claims, independent of their source. To study this setting, we introduce a comprehensive evaluation framework focused on generalization, testing robustness to (i) long-tail knowledge, (ii) variation in claim sources, (iii) multilinguality, and (iv) long-form generation. Across 9 datasets, 18 methods and 3 models, our experiments indicate that logit-based approaches often underperform compared to those that leverage internal model representations. Building on this finding, we introduce INTRA, a method that exploits interactions between internal representations and achieves state-of-the-art performance with strong generalization. More broadly, our work establishes fact-checking without retrieval as a promising research direction that can complement retrieval-based frameworks, improve scalability, and enable the use of such systems as reward signals during training or as components integrated into the generation process.

### [75] [Reasoning Theater: Disentangling Model Beliefs from Chain-of-Thought](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.05488)
*Siddharth Boppana,Annabel Ma,Max Loeffler,Raphael Sarfati,Eric Bigelow,Atticus Geiger,Owen Lewis,Jack Merullo*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies when large reasoning models already “know” the final answer internally but keep generating long chain-of-thought, and shows we can detect this from activations and stop early to save tokens.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Reasoning LMs often produce long chains-of-thought that may not reflect genuine step-by-step reasoning. This makes it unclear whether CoT text is a true reasoning process or a performance learned during training. Understanding this distinction matters for trust, interpretability, efficiency, and for designing better reasoning systems.

Method: The authors compare three approaches on two large models (DeepSeek-R1 671B and GPT-OSS 120B): (1) activation/attention probing to decode the model’s internal belief about the final answer at each step of CoT; (2) early forced answering where the model is required to answer at intermediate points; and (3) an external CoT monitor that tries to judge when the model has reached a stable answer. They analyze behavior across tasks with different difficulty: recall-style MMLU questions vs. hard multihop GPQA-Diamond questions, and examine inflection points like backtracking and ‘aha’ moments in relation to shifts in probed beliefs. They also implement probe-guided early exit to stop generation once the internal answer stabilizes.

Result: For easier, recall-based tasks (MMLU), the correct final answer can be reliably decoded from internal activations much earlier in the CoT than the monitor can detect or than the model actually outputs, revealing a “performative” phase of unnecessary reasoning text. For more difficult, multihop GPQA-Diamond questions, internal beliefs and CoT evolve together in a way that looks more like genuine reasoning. Inflection points such as backtracking or sudden insight almost always coincide with large shifts in the probed internal belief, suggesting these phenomena reflect real uncertainty rather than scripted behavior. Using probe-guided early exit, they cut the number of generated tokens by up to ~80% on MMLU and ~30% on GPQA-Diamond with little or no loss in accuracy.

Conclusion: Reasoning models often already hold a confident internal answer before finishing their chain-of-thought, especially on easy questions, and the extra CoT can be performative rather than functional. Attention/activation probing can detect when the model’s belief has stabilized long before the final answer appears, enabling significant token savings via early exit while preserving accuracy. At the same time, genuine belief shifts align with observable reasoning phenomena, suggesting that not all CoT is mere “theater.” Overall, activation probing is a promising tool for distinguishing performative from genuine reasoning and for implementing adaptive, compute-efficient inference in reasoning LMs.

Abstract: We provide evidence of performative chain-of-thought (CoT) in reasoning models, where a model becomes strongly confident in its final answer, but continues generating tokens without revealing its internal belief. Our analysis compares activation probing, early forced answering, and a CoT monitor across two large models (DeepSeek-R1 671B & GPT-OSS 120B) and find task difficulty-specific differences: The model's final answer is decodable from activations far earlier in CoT than a monitor is able to say, especially for easy recall-based MMLU questions. We contrast this with genuine reasoning in difficult multihop GPQA-Diamond questions. Despite this, inflection points (e.g., backtracking, 'aha' moments) occur almost exclusively in responses where probes show large belief shifts, suggesting these behaviors track genuine uncertainty rather than learned "reasoning theater." Finally, probe-guided early exit reduces tokens by up to 80% on MMLU and 30% on GPQA-Diamond with similar accuracy, positioning attention probing as an efficient tool for detecting performative reasoning and enabling adaptive computation.

</details>

### [76] [SkillNet: Create, Evaluate, and Connect AI Skills](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.04448)

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although modern AI agents can flexibly use tools and complete complex tasks, they lack a systematic way to accumulate, consolidate, and transfer skills over time. As a result, agents repeatedly rediscover solutions instead of reusing prior strategies, leading to inefficiency and limited long-term improvement. The field lacks a unified representation and evaluation framework for skills that would enable large-scale sharing, composition, and refinement.

Method: The authors design SkillNet, an infrastructure that (1) defines a unified ontology for representing skills, (2) supports ingesting skills from diverse sources, (3) encodes rich relations between skills, and (4) evaluates skills along five dimensions: Safety, Completeness, Executability, Maintainability, and Cost-awareness. SkillNet is instantiated as (a) a large repository containing more than 200,000 skills, (b) an interactive platform for exploration and use, and (c) a Python toolkit for programmatic access and integration with agents. They then integrate SkillNet into existing AI agents and benchmark performance on several environments.

Result: When integrated with agents and tested on ALFWorld, WebShop, and ScienceWorld across several backbone models, SkillNet leads to substantial performance gains: average task rewards increase by about 40%, and the number of execution steps needed decreases by about 30%. These improvements hold across different task domains and model backbones, indicating that structured skill reuse provides consistent benefits.

Conclusion: SkillNet demonstrates that formalizing skills as evolving, composable assets—organized in a unified ontology and evaluated along multiple dimensions—enables agents to convert transient problem-solving experiences into durable, reusable mastery. The infrastructure’s large-scale repository, platform, and toolkit offer a practical foundation for building more capable, efficient, and safety-aware AI agents that improve over time by accumulating and reusing skills instead of repeatedly solving tasks from scratch.

Abstract: Current AI agents can flexibly invoke tools and execute complex tasks, yet their long-term advancement is hindered by the lack of systematic accumulation and transfer of skills. Without a unified mechanism for skill consolidation, agents frequently ``reinvent the wheel'', rediscovering solutions in isolated contexts without leveraging prior strategies. To overcome this limitation, we introduce SkillNet, an open infrastructure designed to create, evaluate, and organize AI skills at scale. SkillNet structures skills within a unified ontology that supports creating skills from heterogeneous sources, establishing rich relational connections, and performing multi-dimensional evaluation across Safety, Completeness, Executability, Maintainability, and Cost-awareness. Our infrastructure integrates a repository of over 200,000 skills, an interactive platform, and a versatile Python toolkit. Experimental evaluations on ALFWorld, WebShop, and ScienceWorld demonstrate that SkillNet significantly enhances agent performance, improving average rewards by 40% and reducing execution steps by 30% across multiple backbone models. By formalizing skills as evolving, composable assets, SkillNet provides a robust foundation for agents to move from transient experience to durable mastery.

### [77] [Capability Thresholds and Manufacturing Topology: How Embodied Intelligence Triggers Phase Transitions in Economic Geography](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.04457)
*Xinmin Fang,Lingfeng Tao,Zhengxiong Li*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper argues that embodied AI in robotics will not just make factories more efficient but will fundamentally change where and how manufacturing happens, enabling distributed, demand-proximal micro-factories and a new geography of production.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Manufacturing topology has stayed within the Fordist paradigm for over a century: large centralized factories near labor, optimized by systems like TPS and Industry 4.0 but never structurally transformed. The authors are motivated to understand whether and how embodied intelligence in robots could break this paradigm by altering the economic geography of manufacturing rather than just improving efficiency.

Method: The paper conceptualizes a Capability Space C = (d, g, r, t), where d = dexterity, g = generalization, r = reliability, and t = tactile-vision fusion. It analyzes how crossing certain capability thresholds in this multidimensional space leads to topological changes in the objective function used for factory site selection. The authors identify and theoretically examine three mechanisms—weight inversion, batch collapse, and human-infrastructure decoupling—through which embodied AI alters optimal manufacturing configurations and locations. They also define the concept of Machine Climate Advantage to model new siting criteria once human labor constraints are removed.

Result: The analysis shows that beyond certain AI and robotics capability thresholds, the optimal configuration of manufacturing changes discontinuously: demand-proximal, small-scale micro-factories become economically viable; the pattern of “manufacturing deserts” is reduced or eliminated; and the historical trend toward geographic concentration driven by labor arbitrage is reversed. Additionally, siting decisions become driven by factors like humidity, irradiance, and thermal stability rather than proximity to labor pools.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that embodied intelligence induces phase transitions in the spatial and structural logic of manufacturing, ushering in a post-Fordist manufacturing topology characterized by distributed, machine-optimized production. It proposes “Embodied Intelligence Economics” as a new field to study how physical AI capability thresholds reshape production geography and supply chain organization.

Abstract: The fundamental topology of manufacturing has not undergone a paradigm-level transformation since Henry Ford's moving assembly line in 1913. Every major innovation of the past century, from the Toyota Production System to Industry 4.0, has optimized within the Fordist paradigm without altering its structural logic: centralized mega-factories, located near labor pools, producing at scale. We argue that embodied intelligence is poised to break this century-long stasis, not by making existing factories more efficient, but by triggering phase transitions in manufacturing economic geography itself. When embodied AI capabilities cross critical thresholds in dexterity, generalization, reliability, and tactile-vision fusion, the consequences extend far beyond cost reduction: they restructure where factories are built, how supply chains are organized, and what constitutes viable production scale. We formalize this by defining a Capability Space C = (d, g, r, t) and showing that the site-selection objective function undergoes topological reorganization when capability vectors cross critical surfaces. Through three pathways, weight inversion, batch collapse, and human-infrastructure decoupling, we show that embodied intelligence enables demand-proximal micro-manufacturing, eliminates "manufacturing deserts," and reverses geographic concentration driven by labor arbitrage. We further introduce Machine Climate Advantage: once human workers are removed, optimal factory locations are determined by machine-optimal conditions (low humidity, high irradiance, thermal stability), factors orthogonal to traditional siting logic, creating a production geography with no historical precedent. This paper establishes Embodied Intelligence Economics, the study of how physical AI capability thresholds reshape the spatial and structural logic of production.

### [78] [Progressive Refinement Regulation for Accelerating Diffusion Language Model Decoding](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.04514)
*Lipeng Wan,Jianhui Gu,Junjie Ma,Jianguo Huang,Shiguang Sun,Siyuan Li,Xuguang Lan*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose PRR, a dynamic controller that selectively refines tokens in diffusion language models based on estimated convergence progress, speeding up decoding without hurting quality.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Diffusion language models denoise all tokens uniformly at every step, but different tokens stabilize at different rates. This causes unnecessary refinement steps, wasting computation and slowing decoding. Existing refinement control methods only use local, per-step signals and assume a fixed decoding rule, but true convergence depends on the whole future refinement trajectory, which is itself changed by the control rule. This mismatch motivates a trajectory-aware, dynamic refinement control mechanism.

Method: They introduce Progressive Refinement Regulation (PRR). First, they run full decoding rollouts to obtain future refinement trajectories for tokens and define an empirical measure of each token’s convergence progress over time. Using this trajectory-grounded signal, they train a lightweight, token-wise controller that regulates how much to refine each token by shaping its output distribution via temperature adjustment. Training is progressive and self-evolving: as the controller changes the refinement rule, new trajectories are collected under the updated process and used to further refine the controller, aligning it with the evolving dynamics.

Result: Empirically, PRR significantly accelerates decoding for diffusion language models by reducing redundant token refinements. At the same time, it maintains comparable generation quality to the original, fully refined decoding, as measured by standard evaluation metrics reported in the experiments.

Conclusion: Refinement control in diffusion language models is inherently dynamic and should be based on entire refinement trajectories instead of local, step-wise heuristics. PRR provides an effective framework for trajectory-grounded, token-level refinement regulation using a lightweight controller and temperature-based distribution shaping, achieving faster decoding without degrading text quality.

Abstract: Diffusion language models generate text through iterative denoising under a uniform refinement rule applied to all tokens. However, tokens stabilize at different rates in practice, leading to substantial redundant refinement and motivating refinement control over the denoising process. Existing approaches typically assess refinement necessity from instantaneous, step-level signals under a fixed decoding process. In contrast, whether a token has converged is defined by how its prediction changes along its future refinement trajectory. Moreover, changing the refinement rule reshapes future refinement trajectories, which in turn determine how refinement rules should be formulated, making refinement control inherently dynamic. We propose \emph{Progressive Refinement Regulation} (PRR), a progressive, trajectory-grounded refinement control framework that derives a token-level notion of empirical convergence progress from full decoding rollouts. Based on this signal, PRR learns a lightweight token-wise controller to regulate refinement via temperature-based distribution shaping under a progressive self-evolving training scheme. Experiments show that PRR substantially accelerates diffusion language model decoding while preserving generation quality.

</details>


### [79] [Discovering mathematical concepts through a multi-agent system](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.04528)

TL;DR: The paper introduces a multi-agent system that independently forms, tests, and refines mathematical conjectures, successfully rediscovering the concept of homology from polyhedral data.

  <summary>Details</summary>
