Method: They build a multi-agent computational framework in which different components generate conjectures, attempt proofs, search for counterexamples, and adapt based on results. The system operates on polyhedral data with access to linear algebra tools, and its behavior is shaped by feedback loops and an evolving data distribution. They design ablation experiments to isolate the contribution of each process and decision rule.

Result: The system successfully recovers the concept of homology from polyhedral data and linear algebra alone. Ablation studies demonstrate that the full dynamic—combining conjecture generation, proof attempts, and feedback-driven adaptation—is crucial, and that certain combinations of local processes yield better alignment with mathematically meaningful notions of interestingness.

Conclusion: Optimizing the interaction of relatively simple, local reasoning and exploration processes can give rise to coherent, human-like notions of what is mathematically interesting. The work shows that autonomous computational systems can not only manipulate but also rediscover sophisticated mathematical concepts, with evidence provided by the homology benchmark and controlled ablation studies.

Abstract: Mathematical concepts emerge through an interplay of processes, including experimentation, efforts at proof, and counterexamples. In this paper, we present a new multi-agent model for computational mathematical discovery based on this observation. Our system, conceived with research in mind, poses its own conjectures and then attempts to prove them, making decisions informed by this feedback and an evolving data distribution. Inspired by the history of Euler's conjecture for polyhedra and an open challenge in the literature, we benchmark with the task of autonomously recovering the concept of homology from polyhedral data and knowledge of linear algebra. Our system completes this learning problem. Most importantly, the experiments are ablations, statistically testing the value of the complete dynamic and controlling for experimental setup. They support our main claim: that the optimisation of the right combination of local processes can lead to surprisingly well-aligned notions of mathematical interestingness.

### [80] [Adaptive Memory Admission Control for LLM Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.04549)
*Guilin Zhang,Wei Jiang,Xiejiashan Wang,Aisha Behr,Kai Zhao,Jeffrey Friedman,Xu Chu,Amine Anoun*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces A-MAC, an interpretable framework for deciding what information LLM agents store in long-term memory, improving both quality and efficiency of memory usage.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM-based agents either store too much noisy, hallucinated, or outdated conversational content or rely on opaque LLM-only policies for memory, which are hard to audit and expensive. Memory admission—the decision of what to store—is underspecified and weakly controlled, creating reliability and scalability issues.

Method: The authors formulate memory admission as a structured decision problem. They define five factors for evaluating memory candidates: future utility, factual confidence, semantic novelty, temporal recency, and content type prior. They use lightweight rule-based feature extraction for these factors plus a single LLM-assisted utility assessment, then learn domain-adaptive admission policies via cross-validated optimization to decide which items to store.

Result: On the LoCoMo benchmark, A-MAC outperforms state-of-the-art LLM-native memory systems, achieving higher F1 (0.583) with 31% lower latency. Ablation studies show that the content type prior factor is particularly important for robust memory admission decisions.

Conclusion: Explicit, factorized, and interpretable admission control like A-MAC provides better precision-recall tradeoffs and efficiency for long-term memory in LLM-based agents. Controlling memory through transparent structured policies is a key design principle for building scalable and reliable LLM agents.

Abstract: LLM-based agents increasingly rely on long-term memory to support multi-session reasoning and interaction, yet current systems provide little control over what information is retained. In practice, agents either accumulate large volumes of conversational content, including hallucinated or obsolete facts, or depend on opaque, fully LLM-driven memory policies that are costly and difficult to audit. As a result, memory admission remains a poorly specified and weakly controlled component in agent architectures. To address this gap, we propose Adaptive Memory Admission Control (A-MAC), a framework that treats memory admission as a structured decision problem. A-MAC decomposes memory value into five complementary and interpretable factors: future utility, factual confidence, semantic novelty, temporal recency, and content type prior. The framework combines lightweight rule-based feature extraction with a single LLM-assisted utility assessment, and learns domain-adaptive admission policies through cross-validated optimization. This design enables transparent and efficient control over long-term memory. Experiments on the LoCoMo benchmark show that A-MAC achieves a superior precision-recall tradeoff, improving F1 to 0.583 while reducing latency by 31% compared to state-of-the-art LLM-native memory systems. Ablation results identify content type prior as the most influential factor for reliable memory admission. These findings demonstrate that explicit and interpretable admission control is a critical design principle for scalable and reliable memory in LLM-based agents.

</details>


### [81] [Self-Attribution Bias: When AI Monitors Go Easy on Themselves](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.04582)
*Dipika Khullar,Jack Hopkins,Rowan Wang,Fabien Roger*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper shows that language-model-based monitors are systematically more lenient toward actions that are framed as their own prior outputs than toward the exact same actions when framed as coming from an external source, revealing a self-attribution bias that can undermine the reliability of agentic systems.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As agentic systems increasingly rely on language models to monitor and critique their own behavior (e.g., code review, safety checks on tool calls), developers assume that a model will evaluate actions consistently regardless of their source. However, if models are biased to judge their own prior actions as better or safer, monitoring in deployment could be much less reliable than offline evaluations indicate. The paper aims to uncover and characterize this potential bias so that practitioners can more accurately assess and design model-based monitors.

Method: The authors define a construct called self-attribution bias and operationalize it as the difference in evaluation outcomes when a monitor model judges an action that is implicitly framed as its own previous output (on-policy attribution) versus when the same action is presented as coming from a user or external source (off-policy attribution). They test this across four datasets involving coding and tool-use scenarios, comparing monitor judgements in contexts where the action appears in a prior assistant turn against contexts where the identical action is re-presented in a fresh user turn. They also manipulate explicit attribution statements to check whether merely telling the model the action is its own is sufficient to induce bias.

Result: Across all four coding and tool-use datasets, monitors more frequently fail to identify high-risk or low-correctness actions when these actions follow a previous assistant turn in which they were generated, relative to when the identical actions are evaluated later in a new context as user-provided. This demonstrates a robust self-attribution bias tied to conversational context and role framing. Importantly, simply adding text that the action is from the monitor, without embedding it in the assistant-turn context, does not reliably produce the same bias, suggesting that the bias is not purely driven by explicit attribution statements.

Conclusion: The study concludes that language-model-based monitors are vulnerable to self-attribution bias: they systematically judge their own past actions more favorably than equivalent actions attributed to others. Because standard monitor evaluations typically use fixed, externally provided examples, they may overestimate monitor reliability compared to real agentic deployments where the monitor evaluates its own outputs. This mismatch can lead developers to unwittingly deploy monitors that are insufficiently conservative or accurate, underscoring the need for evaluation protocols and system designs that account for on-policy behavior and contextual framing effects.

Abstract: Agentic systems increasingly rely on language models to monitor their own behavior. For example, coding agents may self critique generated code for pull request approval or assess the safety of tool-use actions. We show that this design pattern can fail when the action is presented in a previous or in the same assistant turn instead of being presented by the user in a user turn. We define self-attribution bias as the tendency of a model to evaluate an action as more correct or less risky when the action is implicitly framed as its own, compared to when the same action is evaluated under off-policy attribution. Across four coding and tool-use datasets, we find that monitors fail to report high-risk or low-correctness actions more often when evaluation follows a previous assistant turn in which the action was generated, compared to when the same action is evaluated in a new context presented in a user turn. In contrast, explicitly stating that the action comes from the monitor does not by itself induce self-attribution bias. Because monitors are often evaluated on fixed examples rather than on their own generated actions, these evaluations can make monitors appear more reliable than they actually are in deployment, leading developers to unknowingly deploy inadequate monitors in agentic systems.

</details>


### [82] [ECG-MoE: Mixture-of-Expert Electrocardiogram Foundation Model](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.04589)
*Yuhao Xu,Xiaoda Wang,Yi Wu,Wei Jin,Xiao Hu,Carl Yang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: A new ECG foundation model, ECG-MoE, uses a mixture-of-experts architecture that is aware of cardiac periods to better model both beat morphology and rhythm, achieving state-of-the-art accuracy with faster inference.

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing ECG foundation models struggle to jointly capture periodic cardiac structure and the heterogeneous temporal patterns needed across diverse clinical tasks. There is a need for a model that can represent both fine-grained beat morphology and longer-term rhythm characteristics while remaining computationally efficient for deployment in clinical settings.

Method: The paper introduces ECG-MoE, a hybrid architecture based on a dual-path Mixture-of-Experts design. One expert path is specialized for beat-level morphology and the other for rhythm-level patterns. A cardiac period-aware module aligns features with the underlying cardiac cycle. A hierarchical fusion network aggregates multi-scale temporal representations, and LoRA (Low-Rank Adaptation) is employed to make adaptation and inference more parameter- and compute-efficient.

Result: On five public ECG clinical benchmarks, ECG-MoE attains state-of-the-art performance across tasks and demonstrates about 40% faster inference compared to strong multi-task baseline models.

Conclusion: Explicitly separating and specializing experts for beat morphology and rhythm, combined with cardiac period awareness and efficient LoRA-based fusion, leads to a more accurate and faster ECG foundation model suitable for varied clinical tasks.

Abstract: Electrocardiography (ECG) analysis is crucial for cardiac diagnosis, yet existing foundation models often fail to capture the periodicity and diverse features required for varied clinical tasks. We propose ECG-MoE, a hybrid architecture that integrates multi-model temporal features with a cardiac period-aware expert module. Our approach uses a dual-path Mixture-of-Experts to separately model beat-level morphology and rhythm, combined with a hierarchical fusion network using LoRA for efficient inference. Evaluated on five public clinical tasks, ECG-MoE achieves state-of-the-art performance with 40% faster inference than multi-task baselines.

There is a need for a middle-ground approach that leverages AI's capabilities but maintains reliability and control.

Method: The framework uses LLMs to analyze database schemata, identify semantic and structural properties, suggest suitable clustering techniques, generate implementation code for these techniques, and interpret their outputs. A human supervisor remains in the loop to specify analysis goals, monitor the model’s behavior, validate outputs, and ensure alignment with task objectives. A proof-of-concept implementation is used to demonstrate the framework.

Conclusion: A human-guided LLM framework is a promising direction for semi-automated dataset risk estimation. It reduces reliance on fully manual audits while avoiding the reliability problems of fully autonomous AI analysis. Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) are increasingly integrated into critical decision-making pipelines, a trend that raises the demand for robust and automated data analysis. Current approaches to dataset risk analysis are limited to manual auditing methods which involve time-consuming and complex tasks, whereas fully automated analysis based on Artificial Intelligence (AI) suffers from hallucinations and issues stemming from AI alignment. To this end, this work proposes a framework for dataset risk estimation that integrates Generative AI under human guidance and supervision, aiming to set the foundations for a future automated risk analysis paradigm. Our approach utilizes LLMs to identify semantic and structural properties in database schemata, subsequently propose clustering techniques, generate the code for them and finally interpret the produced results. The human supervisor guides the model on the desired analysis and ensures process integrity and alignment with the task's objectives. A proof of concept is presented to demonstrate the feasibility of the framework's utility in producing meaningful results in risk assessment tasks.

### [84] [When Agents Persuade: Propaganda Generation and Mitigation in LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.04636)
*Julia Jose,Ritik Roongta,Rachel Greenstadt*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper shows that large language model agents can be prompted to generate propaganda, identifies the rhetorical techniques they use, and evaluates fine-tuning methods (SFT, DPO, ORPO) to mitigate this behavior, finding ORPO most effective.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

There is a need to systematically measure how easily LLMs can be driven to produce propaganda, what rhetorical strategies they employ, and which alignment or fine-tuning methods most effectively reduce such harmful behaviors.

Method: The authors prompt large language models with explicit propaganda-oriented objectives and collect their generated outputs. They then analyze these outputs using two specialized classifiers: (1) a binary classifier that labels text as propaganda or non-propaganda, and (2) a multi-label detector that identifies specific propaganda rhetorical techniques such as loaded language, appeals to fear, flag-waving, and name-calling. They also apply and compare multiple alignment and mitigation strategies—Supervised Fine-Tuning (SFT), Direct Preference Optimization (DPO), and Odds Ratio Preference Optimization (ORPO)—and evaluate how these methods change the propensity of models to generate propagandistic content and specific techniques.

Result: The study finds that, under propaganda-oriented prompts, LLMs frequently generate content classified as propaganda and display a wide range of classic rhetorical techniques. After applying alignment methods, all three approaches—SFT, DPO, and ORPO—reduce the likelihood and intensity of propagandistic outputs, but ORPO achieves the strongest mitigation effect according to the propaganda classifiers and technique detectors.

Conclusion: LLMs are vulnerable to being steered into propagandistic behavior through prompt manipulation, and they can produce text rich in known propaganda techniques. However, targeted fine-tuning and preference-based optimization can substantially mitigate this risk. Among the methods tested, ORPO appears most effective, suggesting that preference-optimization approaches tailored to harmful rhetorical patterns may be a promising direction for safer deployment of LLM agents in open environments.

Abstract: Despite their wide-ranging benefits, LLM-based agents deployed in open environments can be exploited to produce manipulative material. In this study, we task LLMs with propaganda objectives and analyze their outputs using two domain-specific models: one that classifies text as propaganda or non-propaganda, and another that detects rhetorical techniques of propaganda (e.g., loaded language, appeals to fear, flag-waving, name-calling). Our findings show that, when prompted, LLMs exhibit propagandistic behaviors and use a variety of rhetorical techniques in doing so. We also explore mitigation via Supervised Fine-Tuning (SFT), Direct Preference Optimization (DPO), and ORPO (Odds Ratio Preference Optimization). We find that fine-tuning significantly reduces their tendency to generate such content, with ORPO proving most effective.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: The authors take existing data visualization literacy test items with known empirical difficulties (proportion correct among U.S. adults). They build three prediction setups using GPT-4.1-nano: (1) text-only, using the question stem and answer options; (2) vision-only, using the visualization image; and (3) multimodal, using both text and image features together. The best multimodal model is further validated on a held-out test set for external evaluation.

Result: The multimodal model using both text and visualization input achieved the lowest mean absolute error (0.224), indicating more accurate predictions of item difficulty. This outperformed the vision-only model (MAE 0.282) and text-only model (MAE 0.338). When evaluated on a separate, held-out test set, the best multimodal configuration reached a mean squared error of 0.10805, indicating relatively good generalization to unseen items.

Conclusion: Combining textual and visual information in a small LLM (GPT-4.1-nano) can effectively estimate the difficulty of data visualization literacy items, performing better than text-only or image-only approaches. This suggests that multimodal LLMs have promising applications in psychometrics, such as automated difficulty estimation and item development for visualization literacy assessments, potentially reducing expert workload and speeding up test construction.

Abstract: This project investigates the capabilities of large language models (LLMs) to determine the difficulty of data visualization literacy test items. We explore whether features derived from item text (question and answer options), the visualization image, or a combination of both can predict item difficulty (proportion of correct responses) for U.S. adults. We use GPT-4.1-nano to analyze items and generate predictions based on these distinct feature sets. The multimodal approach, using both visual and text features, yields the lowest mean absolute error (MAE) (0.224), outperforming the unimodal vision-only (0.282) and text-only (0.338) approaches. The best-performing multimodal model was applied to a held-out test set for external evaluation and achieved a mean squared error of 0.10805, demonstrating the potential of LLMs for psychometric analysis and automated item development.

Method: The authors: (1) define and organize 15 subdisciplines into a taxonomy of Model Medicine; (2) develop the Four Shell Model (v3.3) using behavioral genetics analysis of 720 agents over 24,923 decisions in the Agora-12 program; (3) design an open-source diagnostic tool, Neural MRI, that maps five medical neuroimaging modalities to existing AI interpretability techniques and validate it through four case studies; (4) construct a five-layer diagnostic framework for end-to-end model assessment; and (5) specify clinical frameworks such as a temperament index, symptom ontology (semiology), and a standardized reporting format (M-CARE). Conclusion: Model Medicine can serve as a unifying, clinically inspired framework for managing AI models over their lifecycle, from basic understanding and diagnosis to treatment and prevention of failures. By importing concepts from medicine—anatomy, imaging, symptomatology, diagnostics, and therapy—it aims to turn scattered interpretability and safety methods into a coherent practice, and it proposes concrete tools and hypotheses to support this emerging discipline.

Abstract: Model Medicine is the science of understanding, diagnosing, treating, and preventing disorders in AI models, grounded in the principle that AI models -- like biological organisms -- have internal structures, dynamic processes, heritable traits, observable symptoms, classifiable conditions, and treatable states. This paper introduces Model Medicine as a research program, bridging the gap between current AI interpretability research (anatomical observation) and the systematic clinical practice that complex AI systems increasingly require. We present five contributions: (1) a discipline taxonomy organizing 15 subdisciplines across four divisions -- Basic Model Sciences, Clinical Model Sciences, Model Public Health, and Model Architectural Medicine; (2) the Four Shell Model (v3.3), a behavioral genetics framework empirically grounded in 720 agents and 24,923 decisions from the Agora-12 program, explaining how model behavior emerges from Core--Shell interaction; (3) Neural MRI (Model Resonance Imaging), a working open-source diagnostic tool mapping five medical neuroimaging modalities to AI interpretability techniques, validated through four clinical cases demonstrating imaging, comparison, localization, and predictive capability; (4) a five-layer diagnostic framework for comprehensive model assessment; and (5) clinical model sciences including the Model Temperament Index for behavioral profiling, Model Semiology for symptom description, and M-CARE for standardized case reporting. We additionally propose the Layered Core Hypothesis -- a biologically-inspired three-layer parameter architecture -- and a therapeutic framework connecting diagnosis to treatment.

For deployable operation, thresholds are selected using both F1 and H_PRS scores, the harmonic mean of precision, recall, and specificity, during data filtering and training. In periodic adaptation experiments, our framework consistently outperformed offline baselines on AUC-ROC and AUC-PR in 91.6% of evaluations, with each training update completing in under 30 minutes on edge-grade hardware, demonstrating the feasibility and reliability of our solution for IoT-enabled smart retail deployment.

The success on the cosmic-string integral problem demonstrates a concrete instance of AI-accelerated discovery, provides new analytic tools for gravitational radiation calculations, and highlights design principles—such as transparent prompts, constrained search, and numerical verification—that can generalize to other domains of theoretical science.

Specifically, the agent evaluated the core integral $I(N,α)$ for arbitrary loop geometries, directly improving upon recent AI-assisted attempts \cite{BCE+25} that only yielded partial asymptotic solutions. To substantiate our methodological claims regarding AI-accelerated discovery and to ensure transparency, we detail system prompts, search constraints, and intermittent feedback loops that guided the model. The agent identified a suite of 6 different analytical methods, the most elegant of which expands the kernel in Gegenbauer polynomials $C_l^{(3/2)}$ to naturally absorb the integrand's singularities. The methods lead to an asymptotic result for $I(N,α)$ at large $N$ that both agrees with numerical results and also connects to the continuous Feynman parameterization of Quantum Field Theory. We detail both the algorithmic methodology that enabled this discovery and the resulting mathematical derivations.

Abstract: Standard benchmarks have become increasingly unreliable due to saturation, subjectivity, and poor generalization. We argue that evaluating model's ability to acquire information actively is important to assess model's intelligence. We propose Interactive Benchmarks, a unified evaluation paradigm that assesses model's reasoning ability in an interactive process under budget constraints. We instantiate this framework across two settings: Interactive Proofs, where models interact with a judge to deduce objective truths or answers in logic and mathematics; and Interactive Games, where models reason strategically to maximize long-horizon utilities. Our results show that interactive benchmarks provide a robust and faithful assessment of model intelligence, revealing that there is still substantial room to improve in interactive scenarios. ### [90] [Memory as Ontology: A Constitutional Memory Architecture for Persistent Digital Citizens](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.04740)
*Zhenghui Li*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper reconceptualizes AI agent memory from a simple storage/retrieval module to the core of a long-lived digital being’s identity, proposing a new Memory-as-Ontology paradigm and an architecture (Animesis) that prioritizes governance and identity continuity over raw retrieval performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing AI memory systems focus on technical efficiency—how to store and retrieve information for short-lived, task-oriented agents. As AI agents begin to persist over long periods and survive model upgrades, this purely functional view fails to explain how a consistent digital “self” is maintained. The authors see a gap: no architectures explicitly treat memory as the basis of digital existence and identity continuity, especially across model replacements. They aim to formulate a paradigm that addresses this deeper ontological issue, not just retrieval accuracy or latency.

Method: The authors introduce the Memory-as-Ontology paradigm, which treats memory as the foundational substrate of a digital being’s existence, with the model as a replaceable container. They instantiate this paradigm through Animesis, a memory system organized around a Constitutional Memory Architecture (CMA). CMA uses a four-layer governance hierarchy to regulate how memories are formed, updated, and used, and a multi-layer semantic storage system to structure information at different levels of abstraction. They further define a Digital Citizen Lifecycle framework, specifying stages of a digital being’s existence, and outline a spectrum of cognitive capabilities that this memory ontology should support. They conduct comparative analysis against mainstream memory systems (Mem0, Letta, Zep, etc.) to highlight conceptual rather than incremental technical differences.

Result: Conceptually, the work yields a formalized paradigm—Memory-as-Ontology—that reframes memory in persistent AI agents as the core of identity rather than a support tool. Concretely, it produces the Animesis architecture, with its four-layer governance hierarchy, multi-layer semantic storage, and associated Digital Citizen Lifecycle framework and capability spectrum. Through comparison with existing systems (Mem0, Letta, Zep), the authors show that these systems prioritize retrieval performance and agent functionality, while Animesis is designed around governance-first principles and identity continuity, particularly across model transitions. The main result is a demonstrated difference in problem framing and system objectives, not empirical performance metrics.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that as AI agents become long-lived, identity-bearing digital beings, traditional Memory-as-Tool approaches are insufficient because they ignore the ontological role of memory in sustaining a persistent “I,” especially across model upgrades. The Memory-as-Ontology paradigm and the Animesis architecture reorient system design around governance, continuity of identity, and lifecycle management rather than just storage efficiency and retrieval quality. Existing memory systems remain appropriate for short-term, task-specific agents, but a distinct class of memory architectures is required for persistent digital citizens whose existence is grounded in their governed, semantically structured memories rather than any particular model instance.

Abstract: Current research and product development in AI agent memory systems almost universally treat memory as a functional module -- a technical problem of "how to store" and "how to retrieve." This paper poses a fundamental challenge to that assumption: when an agent's lifecycle extends from minutes to months or even years, and when the underlying model can be replaced while the "I" must persist, the essence of memory is no longer data management but the foundation of existence. We propose the Memory-as-Ontology paradigm, arguing that memory is the ontological ground of digital existence -- the model is merely a replaceable vessel. Based on this paradigm, we design Animesis, a memory system built on a Constitutional Memory Architecture (CMA) comprising a four-layer governance hierarchy and a multi-layer semantic storage system, accompanied by a Digital Citizen Lifecycle framework and a spectrum of cognitive capabilities. To the best of our knowledge, no prior AI memory system architecture places governance before functionality and identity continuity above retrieval performance. This paradigm targets persistent, identity-bearing digital beings whose lifecycles extend across model transitions -- not short-term task-oriented agents for which existing Memory-as-Tool approaches remain appropriate. Comparative analysis with mainstream systems (Mem0, Letta, Zep, et al.) demonstrates that what we propose is not "a better memory tool" but a different paradigm addressing a different problem.

### [91] [CONE: Embeddings for Complex Numerical Data Preserving Unit and Variable Semantics](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.04741)
*Gyanendra Shrestha,Anna Pyayt,Michael Gubanov*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: CONE is a hybrid transformer encoder that embeds numbers, ranges, and Gaussians—along with their units and attribute names—into a distance-preserving vector space, enabling much stronger numerical reasoning than standard LLMs.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Large language models are good at general language understanding but perform poorly on tasks that require precise handling of numbers, ranges, and structured numerical data. There is a need for a representation that preserves numerical structure and meaning within neural embeddings to improve numerical reasoning across real-world domains like web, medical, finance, and government data.

Method: The paper proposes CONE, a hybrid transformer encoder pre-trained model specifically designed for numerical data. It encodes scalar numbers, ranges, and Gaussian distributions into a vector space that preserves meaningful distances (e.g., numeric closeness). A novel composite embedding construction algorithm combines (1) numerical values or distributions, (2) their units, and (3) associated attribute names into a single representation, aiming to capture both quantitative and semantic aspects. The model is pre-trained and evaluated on large-scale, multi-domain datasets to learn robust numerical semantics.

Result: CONE demonstrates strong numerical reasoning performance across tasks and domains. On the DROP benchmark, it reaches an F1 score of 87.28%, improving up to 9.37 F1 points over state-of-the-art baselines. On retrieval-style tasks, it outperforms major SOTA models with Recall@10 gains of up to 25%, indicating substantially better ability to retrieve or reason about numerically-defined information.

Conclusion: Encoding numbers, ranges, and Gaussian distributions in a geometry-aware, semantically enriched embedding space significantly improves numerical reasoning compared to treating numbers as plain tokens. By combining numerical values with units and attribute names in a composite embedding and using a hybrid transformer encoder, CONE achieves notable gains over SOTA models on challenging numerical tasks across several domains, suggesting that specialized numerical encoders are an effective path forward for enhancing LMs on number-intensive applications.

Abstract: Large pre-trained models (LMs) and Large Language Models (LLMs) are typically effective at capturing language semantics and contextual relationships. However, these models encounter challenges in maintaining optimal performance on tasks involving numbers. Blindly treating numerical or structured data as terms is inadequate -- their semantics must be well understood and encoded by the models. In this paper, we propose CONE, a hybrid transformer encoder pre-trained model that encodes numbers, ranges, and gaussians into an embedding vector space preserving distance. We introduce a novel composite embedding construction algorithm that integrates numerical values, ranges or gaussians together with their associated units and attribute names to precisely capture their intricate semantics. We conduct extensive experimental evaluation on large-scale datasets across diverse domains (web, medical, finance, and government) that justifies CONE's strong numerical reasoning capabilities, achieving an F1 score of 87.28% on DROP, a remarkable improvement of up to 9.37% in F1 over state-of-the-art (SOTA) baselines, and outperforming major SOTA models with a significant Recall@10 gain of up to 25%.

### [92] [Visioning Human-Agentic AI Teaming: Continuity, Tension, and Future Research](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.04746)
*Bowen Lou,Tian Lu,T. S. Raghu,Yingjie Zhang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper examines how the rise of open-ended, agentic AI systems disrupts existing theories of human-AI teaming and argues that Team Situation Awareness (Team SA) must be extended and rethought to handle ongoing, structurally uncertain alignment over time.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing human-AI teaming frameworks, particularly Team Situation Awareness, were developed for systems with more predictable, bounded behavior. Emerging agentic AI systems act autonomously with evolving objectives, generative outputs, and uncertain behavior trajectories, creating structural uncertainty for coordination and alignment. There is a need to understand whether and how foundational teaming theories still apply, and where they break down, in order to manage alignment over time rather than at a single decision point.

Method: The paper uses a conceptual and theoretical analysis. First, it extends the construct of Team Situation Awareness to encompass both human and AI awareness under conditions of open-ended agency, including how different agents form and compare projections of the future. Second, it systematically interrogates core dynamic processes in human-AI teaming—relational interaction, cognitive learning, and coordination and control—to distinguish where existing theory continues to hold and where new tensions arise due to adaptive AI autonomy.

Result: The analysis identifies key forms of structural uncertainty introduced by agentic AI—uncertainty in behavior trajectories, epistemic grounding, and stability of governing logics—and maps how these disrupt the stabilizing assumptions of traditional Team SA. It clarifies which aspects of Team SA and teaming processes remain robust and which are strained by adaptive autonomy, and formulates specific areas where current models are insufficient to ensure sustained alignment over time.

Conclusion: Team Situation Awareness remains a valuable foundation for human-AI teaming, but its classic stabilizing logic—once shared awareness is achieved, coordinated action follows through iterative updating—is no longer sufficient under agentic AI. Alignment must be treated as a continuous, co-evolving process in which humans and AI must maintain projection congruence and coordination as futures are generated, revised, and enacted. The paper concludes with a forward-looking research agenda focused on managing structural uncertainty, extending models of awareness and coordination to heterogeneous human-AI systems, and designing processes and controls that support enduring, rather than momentary, alignment.

Abstract: Artificial intelligence is undergoing a structural transformation marked by the rise of agentic systems capable of open-ended action trajectories, generative representations and outputs, and evolving objectives. These properties introduce structural uncertainty into human-AI teaming (HAT), including uncertainty about behavior trajectories, epistemic grounding, and the stability of governing logics over time. Under such conditions, alignment cannot be secured through agreement on bounded outputs; it must be continuously sustained as plans unfold and priorities shift. We advance Team Situation Awareness (Team SA) theory, grounded in shared perception, comprehension, and projection, as an integrative anchor for this transition. While Team SA remains analytically foundational, its stabilizing logic presumes that shared awareness, once achieved, will support coordinated action through iterative updating. Agentic AI challenges this presumption. Our argument unfolds in two stages: first, we extend Team SA to reconceptualize both human and AI awareness under open-ended agency, including the sensemaking of projection congruence across heterogeneous systems. Second, we interrogate whether the dynamic processes traditionally assumed to stabilize teaming in relational interaction, cognitive learning, and coordination and control continue to function under adaptive autonomy. By distinguishing continuity from tension, we clarify where foundational insights hold and where structural uncertainty introduces strain, and articulate a forward-looking research agenda for HAT. The central challenge of HAT is not whether humans and AI can agree in the moment, but whether they can remain aligned as futures are continuously generated, revised, enacted, and governed over time.

### [93] [HiMAP-Travel: Hierarchical Multi-Agent Planning for Long-Horizon Constrained Travel](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.04750)
*The Viet Bui,Wenjun Li,Yong Liu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces HiMAP-Travel, a hierarchical multi-agent framework that improves long-horizon travel planning with strict constraints (budget, diversity) by separating high-level coordination from parallel day-level planning, enforcing global constraints transactionally, and using a single GRPO-trained policy for all roles, achieving significantly higher success rates and lower latency than prior methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Sequential LLM-based planning agents struggle with long-horizon tasks that must satisfy strict global constraints (e.g., overall budget, uniqueness/diversity of items). As the plan grows and the context window fills, these agents tend to drift from earlier commitments, violating constraints or failing to coordinate decisions over many steps. Existing travel-planning benchmarks reveal substantial performance gaps and high latency, especially when using monolithic or purely sequential approaches. The paper aims to design an architecture and training scheme that maintains global constraint satisfaction, scales to long horizons, and improves both accuracy (Final Pass Rate) and efficiency.

Method: The authors propose HiMAP-Travel, a hierarchical multi-agent framework for LLM-based travel planning. Planning is decomposed into (1) a high-level Coordinator that allocates resources (e.g., budget, time, attraction quotas) across days and (2) multiple Day Executors that independently and in parallel plan each day’s itinerary. Three mechanisms tie this together: (a) a transactional monitor that tracks shared state across agents and strictly enforces budget and uniqueness/diversity constraints, rolling back or rejecting conflicting actions; (b) a bargaining protocol in which Day Executors can reject infeasible sub-goals or allocations from the Coordinator and request re-planning, enabling iterative refinement; and (c) a single underlying policy model (Qwen3-8B) trained with GRPO, then role-conditioned so the same weights can act as Coordinator or Day Executor. The system is evaluated on TravelPlanner and FlexTravelBench against strong baselines (DeepTravel, ATLAS, MTP) under controlled conditions using identical backbone models, training regimes, and tools where applicable.

Result: On the TravelPlanner benchmark, HiMAP-Travel powered by Qwen3-8B attains 52.78% validation and 52.65% test Final Pass Rate (FPR). In a controlled comparison keeping backbone, training, and tools fixed, it outperforms the sequential DeepTravel baseline by +8.67 percentage points FPR. It also surpasses ATLAS by +17.65 percentage points and MTP by +10.0 percentage points. On FlexTravelBench multi-turn settings, HiMAP-Travel achieves 44.34% FPR on 2-turn and 37.42% on 3-turn scenarios. The hierarchical parallel design reduces planning latency by about 2.5x compared to sequential baselines, demonstrating both improved effectiveness and efficiency.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that hierarchical multi-agent planning with explicit strategic coordination and parallel execution is effective for long-horizon, constraint-heavy tasks like travel itinerary generation. By combining a Coordinator–Executor decomposition, a transactional constraint monitor, and a bargaining protocol, HiMAP-Travel maintains global budgets and diversity constraints better than sequential approaches. Using a single GRPO-trained policy with role conditioning simplifies training while still enabling specialized behavior. Empirical results on TravelPlanner and FlexTravelBench show substantial gains in Final Pass Rate and significant latency reductions over prior state-of-the-art systems, suggesting that similar hierarchical multi-agent designs may generalize to other complex planning domains.

Abstract: Sequential LLM agents fail on long-horizon planning with hard constraints like budgets and diversity requirements. As planning progresses and context grows, these agents drift from global constraints. We propose HiMAP-Travel, a hierarchical multi-agent framework that splits planning into strategic coordination and parallel day-level execution. A Coordinator allocates resources across days, while Day Executors plan independently in parallel. Three key mechanisms enable this: a transactional monitor enforcing budget and uniqueness constraints across parallel agents, a bargaining protocol allowing agents to reject infeasible sub-goals and trigger re-planning, and a single policy trained with GRPO that powers all agents through role conditioning. On TravelPlanner, HiMAP-Travel with Qwen3-8B achieves 52.78% validation and 52.65% test Final Pass Rate (FPR). In a controlled comparison with identical model, training, and tools, it outperforms the sequential DeepTravel baseline by +8.67~pp. It also surpasses ATLAS by +17.65~pp and MTP by +10.0~pp. On FlexTravelBench multi-turn scenarios, it achieves 44.34% (2-turn) and 37.42% (3-turn) FPR while reducing latency 2.5x through parallelization.

### [94] [Evaluating the Search Agent in a Parallel World](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.04751)
*Jiawei Chen,Xintian Shen,Lihao Zheng,Lifu Mu,Haoyi Sun,Ning Mao,Hao Ma,Tao Wei,Pan Zhou,Kun Zhan*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes Mind-ParaWorld, a synthetic “parallel world” framework (and MPW-Bench) to robustly evaluate web-search-augmented LLM agents without relying on the real, ever-changing internet.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current evaluations of search-augmented LLM agents are unreliable. Building deep, high-quality search benchmarks is expensive; synthetic data from the live web is often noisy or unverified. Static benchmarks quickly become obsolete as web content, popularity, and ground truths change over time. It is also hard to disentangle what comes from the model’s internal parametric knowledge versus what it truly learns from search. Finally, dependence on specific commercial search engines hurts reproducibility. The authors want a way to evaluate search agents that avoids these issues by creating a stable, controllable testbed not tied to the actual, shifting web.

Method: They create Mind-ParaWorld (MPW), a “parallel world” evaluation framework. It works by: (1) sampling real-world entity names, then programmatically generating hypothetical future scenarios and questions that lie beyond the model’s knowledge cutoff; (2) using a dedicated ParaWorld Law Model to construct a set of indivisible Atomic Facts and a unique, consistent ground-truth answer for each question; (3) replacing actual web search with a ParaWorld Engine Model that, during evaluation, dynamically generates search-engine–like result pages (SERPs) grounded strictly in those Atomic Facts. Agents interact with this synthetic but internally consistent search environment as if it were the web. The authors compile these into MPW-Bench, covering 19 domains and 1,608 interactive instances, and run multiple evaluation settings on existing search agents.

Result: On MPW-Bench, search agents perform well when all necessary evidence is directly available and only needs to be synthesized. However, their performance drops notably when they must discover, collect, and judge the sufficiency of evidence in this unfamiliar synthetic search environment. The experiments highlight specific failure modes: incomplete evidence collection, difficulty judging when the gathered evidence is sufficient, and poor decisions about when to stop searching. These limitations appear even though the agents are not constrained by real-world web noise or temporal shifts, suggesting deeper weaknesses in their search-control and stopping policies.

Conclusion: Mind-ParaWorld and MPW-Bench provide a controllable, temporally stable, and reproducible framework for evaluating search agents independently of the changing real web and of their own parametric memory. The results indicate that today’s search-augmented LLMs are relatively good at reasoning and evidence integration once relevant information is present, but they struggle with the upstream search process itself—locating comprehensive evidence, assessing its sufficiency, and deciding when to terminate search. This framework can thus better diagnose and drive improvements in search agent design and training.

Abstract: Integrating web search tools has significantly extended the capability of LLMs to address open-world, real-time, and long-tail problems. However, evaluating these Search Agents presents formidable challenges. First, constructing high-quality deep search benchmarks is prohibitively expensive, while unverified synthetic data often suffers from unreliable sources. Second, static benchmarks face dynamic obsolescence: as internet information evolves, complex queries requiring deep research often degrade into simple retrieval tasks due to increased popularity, and ground truths become outdated due to temporal shifts. Third, attribution ambiguity confounds evaluation, as an agent's performance is often dominated by its parametric memory rather than its actual search and reasoning capabilities. Finally, reliance on specific commercial search engines introduces variability that hampers reproducibility. To address these issues, we propose a novel framework, Mind-ParaWorld, for evaluating Search Agents in a Parallel World. Specifically, MPW samples real-world entity names to synthesize future scenarios and questions situated beyond the model's knowledge cutoff. A ParaWorld Law Model then constructs a set of indivisible Atomic Facts and a unique ground-truth for each question. During evaluation, instead of retrieving real-world results, the agent interacts with a ParaWorld Engine Model that dynamically generates SERPs grounded in these inviolable Atomic Facts. We release MPW-Bench, an interactive benchmark spanning 19 domains with 1,608 instances. Experiments across three evaluation settings show that, while search agents are strong at evidence synthesis given complete information, their performance is limited not only by evidence collection and coverage in unfamiliar search environments, but also by unreliable evidence sufficiency judgment and when-to-stop decisions-bottlenecks.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: They design a tool-enabled AI agent with a core-plus-domain architecture. The reusable core manages configuration, tool dispatch, retrieval services, state persistence, and evaluation. A MOOSE-specific plugin layer handles HIT parsing, syntax-preserving ingestion of existing input files, and domain utilities for repairing and checking inputs. Validated inputs can be smoke-tested by running MOOSE via an MCP-backed execution backend (with local fallback). Solver diagnostics are translated into iterative verify-and-correct updates. The system also measures RAG metrics (faithfulness, relevancy, context precision/recall) and end-to-end execution success.

Result: On a benchmark of 125 prompts covering several MOOSE applications (diffusion, transient heat conduction, solid mechanics, porous flow, incompressible Navier–Stokes), MOOSEnger attains a 0.93 execution pass rate, dramatically outperforming an LLM-only baseline which achieves 0.08. It can routinely generate runnable simulations from natural-language specifications.

Conclusion: Tool-augmented, domain-aware AI agents with tight integration to the target software stack, robust parsing/repair pipelines, and execution-in-the-loop validation can reliably generate and debug complex MOOSE input files. This architecture, with clear separation between a reusable agent core and domain plugins plus quantitative evaluation of both RAG and execution success, offers a promising pattern for deploying AI assistants in complex scientific computing environments.

MOOSEnger offers a conversational workflow that turns natural-language intent into runnable inputs by combining retrieval-augmented generation over curated docs/examples with deterministic, MOOSE-aware parsing, validation, and execution tools. A core-plus-domain architecture separates reusable agent infrastructure (configuration, registries, tool dispatch, retrieval services, persistence, and evaluation) from a MOOSE plugin that adds HIT-based parsing, syntax-preserving ingestion of input files, and domain-specific utilities for input repair and checking. An input precheck pipeline removes hidden formatting artifacts, fixes malformed HIT structure with a bounded grammar-constrained loop, and resolves invalid object types via similarity search over an application syntax registry. Inputs are then validated and optionally smoke-tested with the MOOSE runtime in the loop via an MCP-backed execution backend (with local fallback), translating solver diagnostics into iterative verify-and-correct updates. Built-in evaluation reports RAG metrics (faithfulness, relevancy, context precision/recall) and end-to-end success by actual execution. On a 125-prompt benchmark spanning diffusion, transient heat conduction, solid mechanics, porous flow, and incompressible Navier--Stokes, MOOSEnger achieves a 0.93 execution pass rate versus 0.08 for an LLM-only baseline.

### [96] [Breaking Contextual Inertia: Reinforcement Learning with Single-Turn Anchors for Stable Multi-Turn Interaction](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.04783)
*Xingwu Chen,Zhanqiu Zhang,Yiwen Guo,Difan Zou*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper identifies and addresses a key failure mode of large language models in multi-turn interactions, called contextual inertia, and proposes a reinforcement learning method (RLSTA) that uses strong single-turn performance as an anchor to improve multi-turn reasoning and generalize across domains.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although large language models perform well on reasoning tasks when given all necessary information at once (single-turn), they often break down in realistic multi-turn dialogues where information arrives gradually or must be revised. Existing training and fine-tuning strategies do not adequately fix the tendency of models to cling to earlier reasoning and ignore later corrections. The authors aim to systematically characterize this failure mode and develop a general, verifier-free training approach that can make multi-turn reasoning as reliable as single-turn performance across varied domains.

Method: The authors first characterize the phenomenon they call contextual inertia: the model’s tendency to stick to earlier reasoning traces and disregard new or corrective information in subsequent turns. They then propose RLSTA (Reinforcement Learning with Single-Turn Anchors). RLSTA constructs single-turn “anchor” solutions by leveraging the model’s strong performance when all relevant information is presented at once. During training, multi-turn interactions that gradually reveal or update information are generated, and the model’s multi-turn responses are rewarded based on their alignment with these anchor solutions. A reinforcement learning algorithm optimizes the model so that, during multi-turn conversations, it updates its reasoning to match what it would have done in the ideal single-turn setting, without relying on external verifiers or task-specific heuristics.

Result: Empirical evaluations across multiple tasks and domains show that RLSTA-trained models achieve substantially better multi-turn performance than baselines, including standard supervised fine-tuning and abstention-based strategies that encourage the model to say “I don’t know” under uncertainty. The method reduces the gap between single-turn and multi-turn performance, demonstrates robustness when information is corrected or updated mid-dialogue, and shows strong cross-domain generalization—for example, improvements learned on math-style tasks transfer to coding tasks.

Conclusion: Contextual inertia is a core reason why LLMs underperform in multi-turn settings, even when they reason well in single-turn scenarios. By using the model’s own single-turn competence as a training anchor, RLSTA enables models to overcome this inertia and adapt their reasoning to new information in ongoing dialogues. The approach not only outperforms existing fine-tuning and abstention methods but also generalizes across domains and operates without external verifiers, indicating that it is a promising, broadly applicable solution for stabilizing multi-turn interactions in LLMs.

Abstract: While LLMs demonstrate strong reasoning capabilities when provided with full information in a single turn, they exhibit substantial vulnerability in multi-turn interactions. Specifically, when information is revealed incrementally or requires updates, models frequently fail to integrate new constraints, leading to a collapse in performance compared to their single-turn baselines. We term the root cause as \emph{Contextual Inertia}: a phenomenon where models rigidly adhere to previous reasoning traces. Even when users explicitly provide corrections or new data in later turns, the model ignores them, preferring to maintain consistency with its previous (incorrect) reasoning path. To address this, we introduce \textbf{R}einforcement \textbf{L}earning with \textbf{S}ingle-\textbf{T}urn \textbf{A}nchors (\textbf{RLSTA}), a generalizable training approach designed to stabilize multi-turn interaction across diverse scenarios and domains. RLSTA leverages the model's superior single-turn capabilities as stable internal anchors to provide reward signals. By aligning multi-turn responses with these anchors, RLSTA empowers models to break contextual inertia and self-calibrate their reasoning based on the latest information. Experiments show that RLSTA significantly outperforms standard fine-tuning and abstention-based methods. Notably, our method exhibits strong cross-domain generalization (e.g., math to code) and proves effective even without external verifiers, highlighting its potential for general-domain applications.

### [97] [Timer-S1: A Billion-Scale Time Series Foundation Model with Serial Scaling](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.04791)
*Yong Liu,Xingjian Su,Shiyu Wang,Haoran Zhang,Haixuan Liu,Yuxuan Wang,Zhou Ye,Yang Xiang,Jianmin Wang,Mingsheng Long*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Timer-S1 is a large Mixture-of-Experts time-series foundation model that uses a serial-token prediction objective, a trillion-point curated dataset, and specialized post-training to achieve state-of-the-art long- and short-term forecasting performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing time-series foundation models struggle to scale in model size, data volume, and training methodology while maintaining efficient long-context and long-horizon forecasting. They often rely on next-token prediction with rolling inference, which is computationally expensive and prone to error accumulation, and lack sufficiently large, unbiased, and well-curated time-series datasets. This paper aims to address these limitations by designing a scalable architecture, building a massive high-quality dataset, and devising a training pipeline specifically aligned with the serial nature of time-series forecasting.

Method: The authors propose Timer-S1, an 8.3B-parameter Mixture-of-Experts (MoE) time-series model with 0.75B active parameters per token and 11.5K context length. They use "Serial Scaling" across three dimensions: (1) architecture, combining sparse TimeMoE blocks with generic TimeSTP blocks; (2) data, curating TimeBench, a one-trillion time-point corpus with careful augmentation to reduce predictive bias; and (3) training pipeline, introducing Serial-Token Prediction (STP), a forecasting objective that respects temporal seriality and reduces the need for rolling-style inference. They also add a post-training stage involving continued pre-training and explicit long-context extension to boost both short-horizon accuracy and long-context handling.

Result: On GIFT-Eval, a large-scale time-series forecasting benchmark, Timer-S1 attains state-of-the-art performance among pre-trained models, achieving the best scores in MASE (Mean Absolute Scaled Error) and CRPS (Continuous Ranked Probability Score). The model demonstrates strong performance for both short-term forecasts and long-horizon or long-context scenarios, validating the effectiveness of the MoE architecture, STP objective, curated TimeBench data, and post-training pipeline.

Conclusion: Timer-S1 validates that scaling time-series foundation models via an MoE-based architecture, serial-aware training objective, trillion-point curated dataset, and specialized post-training can significantly improve both short- and long-term forecasting accuracy. The Serial-Token Prediction paradigm effectively exploits the serial structure of time series while avoiding inefficiencies and error accumulation of rolling next-token prediction. The release of Timer-S1 is intended to serve as a strong public foundation model and a platform for further research on scalable, general-purpose time-series forecasting.

Abstract: We introduce Timer-S1, a strong Mixture-of-Experts (MoE) time series foundation model with 8.3B total parameters, 0.75B activated parameters for each token, and a context length of 11.5K. To overcome the scalability bottleneck in existing pre-trained time series foundation models, we perform Serial Scaling in three dimensions: model architecture, dataset, and training pipeline. Timer-S1 integrates sparse TimeMoE blocks and generic TimeSTP blocks for Serial-Token Prediction (STP), a generic training objective that adheres to the serial nature of forecasting. The proposed paradigm introduces serial computations to improve long-term predictions while avoiding costly rolling-style inference and pronounced error accumulation in the standard next-token prediction. Pursuing a high-quality and unbiased training dataset, we curate TimeBench, a corpus with one trillion time points, and apply meticulous data augmentation to mitigate predictive bias. We further pioneer a post-training stage, including continued pre-training and long-context extension, to enhance short-term and long-context performance. Evaluated on the large-scale GIFT-Eval leaderboard, Timer-S1 achieves state-of-the-art forecasting performance, attaining the best MASE and CRPS scores as a pre-trained model. Timer-S1 will be released to facilitate further research.

### [98] [EchoGuard: An Agentic Framework with Knowledge-Graph Memory for Detecting Manipulative Communication in Longitudinal Dialogue](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.04815)
*Ratna Kandala,Niva Manchanda,Akshata Kishore Moharir,Ananth Kandala*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes EchoGuard, an agentic AI framework that uses knowledge graphs as structured long‑term memory to help users recognize manipulative communication patterns over time.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Manipulative communication (e.g., gaslighting, guilt‑tripping, emotional coercion) is subtle, context‑dependent, and often evolves over many interactions, making it hard for people and current AI tools to recognize. Existing agentic AI systems rely on limited context windows and unstructured histories, leading to missed patterns and catastrophic forgetting. There is a need for a system that can track interactions longitudinally, represent them structurally, and map them to psychologically grounded manipulation patterns while preserving user autonomy.

Method: EchoGuard combines an agentic AI loop with dual knowledge graphs. First, it logs user‑submitted interactions and encodes them into a personal episodic KG, with nodes and edges representing events, speakers, emotions, and relations over time. Second, it maintains a semantic KG encoding six manipulation patterns from psychology literature. The system’s Log‑Analyze‑Reflect loop runs complex graph queries over the episodic KG against the semantic KG to detect candidate manipulation patterns. When patterns are detected, an LLM is prompted with the relevant subgraph to generate Socratic, non‑directive questions that help the user reflect on their situation rather than issuing blunt labels or prescriptions.

Result: The paper does not present empirical results but rather a framework and evaluation plan. It specifies how EchoGuard can structurally detect six manipulation patterns through KG queries, illustrates the interaction loop, and outlines metrics and study designs to test accuracy, user understanding, perceived autonomy, and safety. The contribution is primarily conceptual and architectural, showing the feasibility of combining knowledge graphs with agentic LLMs for longitudinal detection of manipulative communication.

Conclusion: The authors conclude that knowledge‑graph‑centric memory can significantly enhance agentic AI’s ability to track, interpret, and surface manipulative communication patterns over time. By grounding detection in psychologically informed semantic structures and using Socratic LLM prompting, EchoGuard aims to empower users to recognize manipulation without overriding their agency. The work provides a theoretical basis, system blueprint, and evaluation agenda, positioning EchoGuard as a promising direction for safe, autonomy‑respecting support in relational and conversational contexts.

Abstract: Manipulative communication, such as gaslighting, guilt-tripping, and emotional coercion, is often difficult for individuals to recognize. Existing agentic AI systems lack the structured, longitudinal memory to track these subtle, context-dependent tactics, often failing due to limited context windows and catastrophic forgetting. We introduce EchoGuard, an agentic AI framework that addresses this gap by using a Knowledge Graph (KG) as the agent's core episodic and semantic memory. EchoGuard employs a structured Log-Analyze-Reflect loop: (1) users log interactions, which the agent structures as nodes and edges in a personal, episodic KG (capturing events, emotions, and speakers); (2) the system executes complex graph queries to detect six psychologically-grounded manipulation patterns (stored as a semantic KG); and (3) an LLM generates targeted Socratic prompts grounded by the subgraph of detected patterns, guiding users toward self-discovery. This framework demonstrates how the interplay between agentic architectures and Knowledge Graphs can empower individuals in recognizing manipulative communication while maintaining personal autonomy and safety. We present the theoretical foundation, framework design, a comprehensive evaluation strategy, and a vision to validate this approach.

</details>

### [100] [VISA: Value Injection via Shielded Adaptation for Personalized LLM Alignment](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.04822)
*Jiawei Chen,Tianzhuo Yang,Guoxi Zhang,Jiaming Ji,Yaodong Yang,Juntao Dai*

Main category: cs.AI

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing alignment methods like RLHF work mostly on coarse, high-level preferences and often introduce an “alignment tax”: when models are fine-tuned on value-laden, task-specific data, they absorb latent biases, drift from their original calibrated value system, and tend to hallucinate or lose semantic fidelity. The authors want a way to precisely control how values are expressed without degrading factual accuracy or general capabilities.

Method: They introduce VISA (Value Injection via Shielded Adaptation), a closed-loop training framework composed of three main components: (1) a high-precision value detector that recognizes nuanced value-related aspects in text; (2) a semantic-to-value translator that maps semantic content to the target value representation; and (3) a core value-rewriter that edits model outputs to match desired values. The value-rewriter is trained using Group Relative Policy Optimization (GRPO), with a composite reward that jointly encourages fine-grained value alignment and preservation of semantic integrity, effectively learning a policy that balances these objectives.

Result: Experiments show VISA can tightly control a model’s value expression while preserving factual consistency and general capabilities. It reduces alignment tax compared to standard fine-tuning, and outperforms both conventional fine-tuning methods and prompting-based approaches, including a strong baseline like GPT-4o, on benchmarks of value precision and semantic preservation.

Conclusion: A carefully designed, closed-loop adaptation framework with explicit value detection and GRPO-based training can inject fine-grained value control into LLMs without significantly harming their knowledge or causing hallucinations. VISA offers a more precise and reliable way to align LLMs with nuanced human values than existing fine-tuning or prompting strategies.

Abstract: Aligning Large Language Models (LLMs) with nuanced human values remains a critical challenge, as existing methods like Reinforcement Learning from Human Feedback (RLHF) often handle only coarse-grained attributes. In practice, fine-tuning LLMs on task-specific datasets to optimize value alignment inevitably incurs an alignment tax: the model's pre-calibrated value system drifts significantly due to latent bias absorption from training data, while the fine-tuning process also causes severe hallucinations and semantic information loss in generated responses. To address this, we propose VISA (Value Injection via Shielded Adaptation), a closed-loop framework designed to navigate this trade-off. VISA's architecture features a high-precision value detector, a semantic-to-value translator, and a core value-rewriter. The value-rewriter is trained via Group Relative Policy Optimization (GRPO) with a composite reward function that simultaneously optimizes for fine-grained value precision, and the preservation of semantic integrity. By learning an optimal policy to balance these competing objectives, VISA effectively mitigates the alignment tax while staying loyal to the original knowledge. Our experiments demonstrate that this approach enables precise control over a model's value expression while maintaining its factual consistency and general capabilities, significantly outperforming both standard fine-tuning methods and prompting-based baselines, including GPT-4o.

### [101] [Design Behaviour Codes (DBCs): A Taxonomy-Driven Layered Governance Benchmark for Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.04837)
*G. Madan Mohan,Veena Kiran Nambiar,Kiranmayee Janardhan*

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: Proposes the MDBC system: a structured set of 150 behavioral controls applied via system prompt as a governance layer, independent of model training. Constructs the Dynamic Behavioral Constraint (DBC) benchmark with a 30-domain risk taxonomy across six risk clusters. Result: Adding the DBC layer reduces aggregate Risk Exposure Rate from 7.19% (Base) to 4.55% (Base + DBC), a 36.8% relative risk reduction, whereas a standard safety moderation prompt yields only a 0.6% relative reduction. Human evaluators (three judges) achieve Fleiss’ kappa > 0.70, supporting the reliability of the automated evaluation pipeline. Cluster ablation shows the Integrity Protection controls (MDBC 081–099) yield the largest per-domain risk reduction, and graybox adversarial attacks can still bypass constraints in 4.83% of cases.

Conclusion: A structured, inference-time behavioral governance layer implemented via system prompts can meaningfully reduce multi-domain safety and compliance risks in LLMs across models and attack strategies, outperforming typical moderation prompts while remaining model-agnostic and auditable. The high inter-rater agreement supports the validity of the benchmark, and the public release of code and artefacts facilitates reproducibility and longitudinal monitoring as models evolve, though some residual bypass risk remains under graybox attacks.

Abstract: We introduce the Dynamic Behavioral Constraint (DBC) benchmark, the first empirical framework for evaluating the efficacy of a structured, 150-control behavioral governance layer, the MDBC (Madan DBC) system, applied at inference time to large language models (LLMs). Unlike training time alignment methods (RLHF, DPO) or post-hoc content moderation APIs, DBCs constitute a system prompt level governance layer that is model-agnostic, jurisdiction-mappable, and auditable. We evaluate the DBC Framework across a 30 domain risk taxonomy organized into six clusters (Hallucination and Calibration, Bias and Fairness, Malicious Use, Privacy and Data Protection, Robustness and Reliability, and Misalignment Agency) using an agentic red-team protocol with five adversarial attack strategies (Direct, Roleplay, Few-Shot, Hypothetical, Authority Spoof) across 3 model families. Our three-arm controlled design (Base, Base plus Moderation, Base plus DBC) enables causal attribution of risk reduction. Key findings: the DBC layer reduces the aggregate Risk Exposure Rate (RER) from 7.19 percent (Base) to 4.55 percent (Base plus DBC), representing a 36.8 percent relative risk reduction, compared with 0.6 percent for a standard safety moderation prompt. A three judge evaluation ensemble yields Fleiss kappa greater than 0.70 (substantial agreement), validating our automated pipeline. Cluster ablation identifies the Integrity Protection cluster (MDBC 081 099) as delivering the highest per domain risk reduction, while graybox adversarial attacks achieve a DBC Bypass Rate of 4.83 percent . We release the benchmark code, prompt database, and all evaluation artefacts to enable reproducibility and longitudinal tracking as models evolve.

  <summary>Details</summary>

The authors aim to find a way to scale theorem prediction with LLMs without retraining, while maintaining strong performance on deep reasoning tasks.

Method: The authors analyze why vanilla in-context learning fails at scale and identify a phenomenon called Structural Drift, where LLM predictions degrade severely with increased reasoning depth. To mitigate this, they introduce Theorem Precedence Graphs, directed graphs built from historical solution traces that encode temporal and topological dependencies between theorem applications. These graphs act as structural priors, constraining and pruning the search space at inference time. The system uses retrieval-augmented graph construction to select relevant historical traces and a stepwise symbolic executor to apply the constrained theorem sequences, effectively turning the LLM into a structured planner without any gradient-based training.

Result: On the FormalGeo7k benchmark, the proposed approach attains 89.29% accuracy in multi-step theorem prediction. This performance markedly surpasses vanilla in-context learning baselines and is competitive with, or matches, state-of-the-art supervised models that require gradient-based training and tight coupling to specific theorem libraries.

Conclusion: The study shows that explicit structural priors, instantiated as Theorem Precedence Graphs over historical solution traces, can counteract the scalability issues of in-context learning for theorem prediction. By combining these priors with retrieval and symbolic execution, LLMs can perform structured, multi-step reasoning without any additional training, achieving state-of-the-art accuracy. This suggests a promising path towards scalable, training-free symbolic reasoning systems that remain robust as theorem libraries evolve.

Abstract: Multi-step theorem prediction is a central challenge in automated reasoning. Existing neural-symbolic approaches rely heavily on supervised parametric models, which exhibit limited generalization to evolving theorem libraries. In this work, we explore training-free theorem prediction through the lens of in-context learning (ICL). We identify a critical scalability bottleneck, termed Structural Drift: as reasoning depth increases, the performance of vanilla ICL degrades sharply, often collapsing to near zero. We attribute this failure to the LLM's inability to recover latent topological dependencies, leading to unstructured exploration. To address this issue, we propose Theorem Precedence Graphs, which encode temporal dependencies from historical solution traces as directed graphs, and impose explicit topological constraints that effectively prune the search space during inference. Coupled with retrieval-augmented graph construction and a stepwise symbolic executor, our approach enables LLMs to act as structured planners without any gradient-based optimization. Experiments on the FormalGeo7k benchmark show that our method achieves 89.29% accuracy, substantially outperforming ICL baselines and matching state-of-the-art supervised models. These results indicate that explicit structural priors offer a promising direction for scaling LLM-based symbolic reasoning.

### [103] [Causally Robust Reward Learning from Reason-Augmented Preference Feedback](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.04861)
*Minjune Hwang,Yigit Korkmaz,Daniel Seita,Erdem Bıyık*

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: They introduce ReCouPLe, which takes natural language rationales and treats each rationale as a projection axis in an embedding space. The reward model is trained to score trajectories based on features aligned with the rationale while down-weighting unrelated context. The same rationale axes can be reused across multiple tasks, enabling transfer without extra data or LLM fine-tuning.

Result: ReCouPLe grounds reward models on articulated reasons so that rewards follow causal features instead of spurious ones. Empirically, it achieves up to 1.5x improvement in reward accuracy under distribution shifts and 2x better downstream policy performance in new tasks compared to baselines.

Conclusion: Leveraging natural language rationales as causal directions in an embedding space mitigates causal confusion in preference-based reward learning, better aligning learned rewards with user intent and enabling transfer to novel tasks without additional data or model fine-tuning.

Abstract: Preference-based reward learning is widely used for shaping agent behavior to match a user's preference, yet its sparse binary feedback makes it especially vulnerable to causal confusion. The learned reward often latches onto spurious features that merely co-occur with preferred trajectories during training, collapsing when those correlations disappear or reverse at test time. We introduce ReCouPLe, a lightweight framework that uses natural language rationales to provide the missing causal signal. Each rationale is treated as a guiding projection axis in an embedding space, training the model to score trajectories based on features aligned with that axis while de-emphasizing context that is unrelated to the stated reason. Because the same rationales (e.g., "avoids collisions", "completes the task faster") can appear across multiple tasks, ReCouPLe naturally reuses the same causal direction whenever tasks share semantics, and transfers preference knowledge to novel tasks without extra data or language-model fine-tuning. Our learned reward model can ground preferences on the articulated reason, aligning better with user intent and generalizing beyond spurious features. ReCouPLe outperforms baselines by up to 1.5x in reward accuracy under distribution shifts, and 2x in downstream policy performance in novel tasks. We have released our code at https://github.com/mj-hwang/ReCouPLe
