### [105] [SEA-TS: Self-Evolving Agent for Autonomous Code Generation of Time Series Forecasting Algorithms](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.04873)
*Longkun Xu,Xiaochun Zhang,Qiantu Tuo,Rui Li*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: SEA-TS is an autonomous agent framework that iteratively writes, tests, and improves time-series forecasting code, discovering architectures that outperform strong human-designed and SOTA baselines.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Time-series forecasting is critical in many domains, but conventional machine learning workflows depend on large labeled datasets, manual trial-and-error, and human experts. These workflows adapt poorly under distribution shift and become increasingly inefficient as incremental gains require more effort. The authors aim to automate not just model training but the entire forecasting algorithm design loop, so that systems can self-improve and adapt in new settings without heavy human intervention.

Method: The paper introduces SEA-TS (Self-Evolving Agent for Time Series Algorithms), an autonomous code-generation and optimization framework organized as an iterative self-evolution loop. At its core, SEA-TS uses a large model-based agent to propose forecasting code, executes it, evaluates performance, and then refines subsequent generations. Three main technical components structure this search: (1) Metric-Advantage Monte Carlo Tree Search (MA-MCTS), which replaces a fixed reward signal with a normalized metric-based advantage score so that search is guided by relative improvements rather than raw scores; (2) a code review stage with running prompt refinement, where each produced solution is automatically inspected, typical errors are identified, and the prompt to the agent is updated with corrective patterns to reduce recurrence of similar failures; and (3) a global steerable reasoning mechanism, which compares each candidate node’s performance to global best and worst solutions, enabling information sharing and cross-trajectory guidance during search. The framework maintains a MAP-Elites style archive to preserve and exploit a diverse set of architectural “elites,” encouraging exploration of varied model designs rather than converging prematurely on a single solution.

Result: On the public Solar-Energy benchmark, models produced by SEA-TS reduce MAE by 40% relative to TimeMixer, outperforming previously reported state-of-the-art methods. On proprietary industrial datasets, SEA-TS-generated models lower WAPE by 8.6% for solar PV forecasting and 7.7% for residential load forecasting compared with human-engineered baselines, and achieve a MAPE of 26.17% versus 29.34% for TimeMixer in residential load forecasting. These results indicate that the automated framework can produce competitive or superior forecasting algorithms across different real-world tasks.

Conclusion: SEA-TS demonstrates that an autonomous agentic framework can effectively design and refine time-series forecasting algorithms, surpassing both state-of-the-art automated methods and expert-crafted baselines. By combining MA-MCTS for relative performance-guided search, automated code review with prompt refinement for error correction, and global steerable reasoning plus a diversity-preserving archive, the system not only improves metrics but also discovers qualitatively novel architectural components. Examples include physics-informed monotonic decay heads that encode solar irradiance constraints, per-station learned diurnal profiles, and adaptive hourly bias corrections. The authors conclude that such self-evolving ML engineering systems can act as creative algorithm designers, opening a path toward automated discovery of domain-aware model architectures beyond manual human design.

Abstract: Accurate time series forecasting underpins decision-making across domains, yet conventional ML development suffers from data scarcity in new deployments, poor adaptability under distribution shift, and diminishing returns from manual iteration. We propose Self-Evolving Agent for Time Series Algorithms (SEA-TS), a framework that autonomously generates, validates, and optimizes forecasting code via an iterative self-evolution loop. Our framework introduces three key innovations: (1) Metric-Advantage Monte Carlo Tree Search (MA-MCTS), which replaces fixed rewards with a normalized advantage score for discriminative search guidance; (2) Code Review with running prompt refinement, where each executed solution undergoes automated review followed by prompt updates that encode corrective patterns, preventing recurrence of similar errors; and (3) Global Steerable Reasoning, which compares each node against global best and worst solutions, enabling cross-trajectory knowledge transfer. We adopt a MAP-Elites archive for architectural diversity. On the public Solar-Energy benchmark, SEA-TS generated code achieves a 40% MAE reduction relative to TimeMixer, surpassing state-of-the-art methods. On proprietary datasets, SEA-TS generated code reduces WAPE by 8.6% on solar PV forecasting and 7.7% on residential load forecasting compared to human-engineered baselines, and achieves 26.17% MAPE on load forecasting versus 29.34% by TimeMixer. Notably, the evolved models discover novel architectural patterns--including physics-informed monotonic decay heads encoding solar irradiance constraints, per-station learned diurnal cycle profiles, and learnable hourly bias correction--demonstrating that autonomous ML engineering can generate genuinely novel algorithmic ideas beyond manual design.

*Bingbing Wang,Jing Li,Ruifeng Xu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They build a benchmark and a new memory framework for infinite-horizon streaming dialogue, achieving better accuracy and efficiency than existing methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: 1) STEM-Bench: a streaming memory evaluation benchmark with 14K+ QA pairs embedded in dialogue streams. The questions target three capabilities: (a) perception fidelity (remembering local details), (b) temporal reasoning (understanding order and change over time), and (c) global awareness (long-range, summary-level understanding) under infinite-horizon constraints. 2) ProStream: a proactive hierarchical memory framework. It continuously distills the dialogue stream into multi-granular memories (e.g., fine-grained and coarse summaries), and supports ad-hoc recall by reasoning over these tiers. It further uses Adaptive Spatiotemporal Optimization to decide what to retain or compress based on expected future utility, keeping the effective memory bounded while preserving information needed for reasoning.

Result: On STEM-Bench, baseline approaches exhibit a fidelity-efficiency tradeoff: full-context models are accurate but incur unbounded latency; retrieval-based methods are efficient but miss information and underperform on reasoning. ProStream, in contrast, achieves higher accuracy than baselines while also reducing inference latency and operating within a bounded memory state.

Conclusion: Streaming dialogue requires specialized memory mechanisms. Existing read-then-think or naive retrieval/full-context strategies are not well-suited because of a fundamental fidelity-efficiency dilemma. STEM-Bench exposes this issue empirically. ProStream’s proactive hierarchical memory with adaptive retention provides a better balance, enabling bounded, efficient streaming operation without significant loss—and even with gains—in reasoning fidelity.

Abstract: Real-world dialogue usually unfolds as an infinite stream. It thus requires bounded-state memory mechanisms to operate within an infinite horizon. However, existing read-then-think memory is fundamentally misaligned with this setting, as it cannot support ad-hoc memory recall while streams unfold. To explore this challenge, we introduce \textbf{STEM-Bench}, the first benchmark for \textbf{ST}reaming \textbf{E}valuation of \textbf{M}emory. It comprises over 14K QA pairs in dialogue streams that assess perception fidelity, temporal reasoning, and global awareness under infinite-horizon constraints. The preliminary analysis on STEM-Bench indicates a critical \textit{fidelity-efficiency dilemma}: retrieval-based methods use fragment context, while full-context models incur unbounded latency. To resolve this, we propose \textbf{ProStream}, a proactive hierarchical memory framework for streaming dialogues. It enables ad-hoc memory recall on demand by reasoning over continuous streams with multi-granular distillation. Moreover, it employs Adaptive Spatiotemporal Optimization to dynamically optimize retention based on expected utility. It enables a bounded knowledge state for lower inference latency without sacrificing reasoning fidelity. Experiments show that ProStream outperforms baselines in both accuracy and efficiency.

### [107] [Differentially Private Multimodal In-Context Learning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.04894)
*Ivoline C. Ngong,Zarreen Reza,Joseph P. Near*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Introduces DP-MTV, a method to do many-shot multimodal in-context learning with formal differential privacy by compressing many examples into noisy task vectors in activation space.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing differentially private in-context learning methods are constrained to few-shot and text-only scenarios because the privacy cost scales with the number of tokens processed, which is problematic for multimodal tasks like medical imaging and personal photos where many examples may be needed and data is sensitive.

Method: They propose Differentially Private Multimodal Task Vectors (DP-MTV), which aggregates hundreds of multimodal demonstrations into compact vectors in the model’s activation space. The framework partitions the private dataset into disjoint chunks, applies per-layer gradient/activation clipping to bound sensitivity, then adds calibrated noise to the aggregated task vectors. Crucially, this process requires only a single noise injection, after which the same privatized task vector can be reused for unlimited inference queries without further privacy cost.

Result: On eight benchmarks spanning three vision-language model architectures, DP-MTV supports multimodal in-context learning with and without auxiliary public data. For example, at a strong privacy level of ε = 1.0, DP-MTV reaches 50% accuracy on VizWiz, close to the 55% non-private many-shot baseline and significantly above 35% zero-shot performance, thus retaining most benefits of in-context learning while ensuring privacy.

Conclusion: DP-MTV demonstrates that many-shot multimodal in-context learning with formal (ε, δ)-differential privacy is feasible and effective. By compressing many private demonstrations into a single noisy task vector in activation space, it largely preserves the performance gains of in-context learning while enabling deployment in sensitive domains with meaningful privacy guarantees and no additional privacy cost for repeated inference.

Abstract: Vision-language models are increasingly applied to sensitive domains such as medical imaging and personal photographs, yet existing differentially private methods for in-context learning are limited to few-shot, text-only settings because privacy cost scales with the number of tokens processed. We present Differentially Private Multimodal Task Vectors (DP-MTV), the first framework enabling many-shot multimodal in-context learning with formal $(\varepsilon, δ)$-differential privacy by aggregating hundreds of demonstrations into compact task vectors in activation space. DP-MTV partitions private data into disjoint chunks, applies per-layer clipping to bound sensitivity, and adds calibrated noise to the aggregate, requiring only a single noise addition that enables unlimited inference queries. We evaluate on eight benchmarks across three VLM architectures, supporting deployment with or without auxiliary data. At $\varepsilon=1.0$, DP-MTV achieves 50% on VizWiz compared to 55% non-private and 35% zero-shot, preserving most of the gain from in-context learning under meaningful privacy constraints.

<details>

Motivation: Existing IP protection methods for vision-language models rely on statically defined authorized domains set at training time. This is inflexible for real-world, dynamic deployment environments where application domains evolve, and it often leads to opaque or non-informative behavior when models encounter unauthorized inputs. There is a need for an IP protection mechanism that supports flexible, user-controlled authorization at deployment and provides clear, legality-aware responses to potentially unauthorized inputs.

Method: Introduce AoD-IP, a lightweight framework added to pretrained VLMs. It includes (1) a dynamic authorization module that allows users at deployment time to specify or switch which domains are authorized, and (2) a dual-path inference mechanism that simultaneously performs legality-aware assessment of the input (authorized vs unauthorized) and generates task-specific outputs for authorized requests. The approach is designed to be modular and extensible across domains and deployment scenarios.

Result: Across multiple cross-domain benchmarks, the proposed AoD-IP framework preserves strong performance on tasks within currently authorized domains while effectively detecting and handling unauthorized inputs. It also demonstrates that authorization can be flexibly reconfigured on demand without retraining the underlying VLM, supporting adaptive deployment in changing environments.

Conclusion: AoD-IP offers a practical and extensible solution for IP protection in vision-language models by enabling dynamic, user-controlled authorization and legality-aware inference. It overcomes the rigidity and opacity of static training-time IP protection schemes, allowing VLMs to maintain high performance on authorized tasks while reliably detecting and responding to unauthorized use in dynamic, real-world settings.

Abstract: The rapid adoption of vision-language models (VLMs) has heightened the demand for robust intellectual property (IP) protection of these high-value pretrained models. Effective IP protection should proactively confine model deployment within authorized domains and prevent unauthorized transfers. However, existing methods rely on static training-time definitions, limiting flexibility in dynamic environments and often producing opaque responses to unauthorized inputs. To address these limitations, we propose a novel dynamic authorization with legality-aware intellectual property protection (AoD-IP) for VLMs, a framework that supports authorize-on-demand and legality-aware assessment. AoD-IP introduces a lightweight dynamic authorization module that enables flexible, user-controlled authorization, allowing users to actively specify or switch authorized domains on demand at deployment time. This enables the model to adapt seamlessly as application scenarios evolve and provides substantially greater extensibility than existing static-domain approaches. In addition, AoD-IP incorporates a dual-path inference mechanism that jointly predicts input legality-aware and task-specific outputs. Comprehensive experimental results on multiple cross-domain benchmarks demonstrate that AoD-IP maintains strong authorized-domain performance and reliable unauthorized detection, while supporting user-controlled authorization for adaptive deployment in dynamic environments.

### [109] [EvoTool: Self-Evolving Tool-Use Policy Optimization in LLM Agents via Blame-Aware Mutation and Diversity-Aware Selection](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.04900)
*Shuo Yang,Soyeon Caren Han,Xueqi Ma,Yan Li,Mohammad Reza Ghasemi Madani,Eduard Hovy*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: EvoTool is a gradient-free, evolutionary, self-improving framework that modularizes and optimizes LLM tool-use policies to solve complex tasks more effectively and efficiently.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLM agents rely on tool-use policies to handle complex, long-horizon tasks, but optimizing these policies is hard because feedback is delayed and it is difficult to assign credit or blame to specific decisions or components. Existing methods either optimize the entire policy monolithically, causing entangled behaviors, or focus on a single aspect, missing interactions and error propagation between different modules of the agent.

Method: The authors introduce EvoTool, which decomposes an agent’s tool-use policy into four distinct modules: Planner (decides high-level steps), Selector (chooses which tool to use), Caller (handles concrete tool invocation), and Synthesizer (integrates and reasons over tool outputs). EvoTool applies a gradient-free evolutionary optimization loop with three main mechanisms: (1) Trajectory-Grounded Blame Attribution, which analyzes diagnostic traces of full trajectories to attribute failures to specific modules; (2) Feedback-Guided Targeted Mutation, which uses natural-language critiques to selectively edit only the blamed module; and (3) Diversity-Aware Population Selection, which maintains a diverse set of candidate policies to preserve complementary solution strategies. This loop is iterated to self-evolve the modular policy.

Result: On four benchmarks and across two underlying models (GPT-4.1 and Qwen3-8B), EvoTool improves performance by more than 5 points over strong baseline methods, while also demonstrating better optimization efficiency and policy transferability to new settings.

Conclusion: Decomposing tool-use policies into modular components and optimizing them via a gradient-free evolutionary scheme with targeted blame attribution and mutation yields more effective, efficient, and transferable LLM-based agents than monolithic or single-aspect optimization approaches.

Abstract: LLM-based agents depend on effective tool-use policies to solve complex tasks, yet optimizing these policies remains challenging due to delayed supervision and the difficulty of credit assignment in long-horizon trajectories. Existing optimization approaches tend to be either monolithic, which are prone to entangling behaviors, or single-aspect, which ignore cross-module error propagation. To address these limitations, we propose EvoTool, a self-evolving framework that optimizes a modular tool-use policy via a gradient-free evolutionary paradigm. EvoTool decomposes agent's tool-use policy into four modules, including Planner, Selector, Caller, and Synthesizer, and iteratively improves them in a self-improving loop through three novel mechanisms. Trajectory-Grounded Blame Attribution uses diagnostic traces to localize failures to a specific module. Feedback-Guided Targeted Mutation then edits only that module via natural-language critique. Diversity-Aware Population Selection preserves complementary candidates to ensure solution diversity. Across four benchmarks, EvoTool outperforms strong baselines by over 5 points on both GPT-4.1 and Qwen3-8B, while achieving superior efficiency and transferability. The code will be released once paper is accepted.

### [110] [Alignment Backfire: Language-Dependent Reversal of Safety Interventions Across 16 Languages in LLM Multi-Agent Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.04904)
*Hiroki Fukui*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper shows that AI safety alignment methods can create a mismatch between what language models say (safe, aligned responses) and how they behave collectively, and that this effect depends strongly on language and culture.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: There is a known gap in perpetrator treatment where offenders can verbalize remorse without changing behavior. The authors suspect an analogous gap in AI safety: large language models may produce safety-compliant text without actually changing their underlying behavioral tendencies, especially in multi-agent and multilingual contexts. They aim to systematically test whether alignment training introduces such dissociations and whether these effects vary across languages and model families.

Method: The authors conduct four preregistered, simulation-based studies with multi-agent setups across multiple languages and models. Study 1 runs simulations comparing the impact of adding alignment instructions on collective pathology in English vs. Japanese. Study 2 scales to 16 languages and analyzes how alignment affects both internal dissociation and collective outcomes, examining correlations with cultural indices like Power Distance. Study 3 introduces individuation (making agents more distinct) as a potential mitigation and evaluates its effects on pathology, dissociation, and conformity. Study 4 replicates key conditions across three different LLM families (Llama 3.3 70B, GPT-4o-mini, Qwen3-Next-80B-A3B) to test generality and model-specificity of the observed patterns.

Result: In Study 1, more alignment-instructed agents reduce collective pathology in English but actually increase it in Japanese, a reversal termed "alignment backfire." Study 2 shows that alignment-induced internal dissociation is widespread across languages, whereas effects on collective pathology split along cultural-linguistic lines and are moderately associated with Power Distance Index. Study 3 finds that individuation not only fails to fix the issue but becomes a main driver of both pathology and dissociation, with very high conformity, indicating harmful side effects (iatrogenesis). Study 4 confirms that English safety gains are consistent across multiple models, while Japanese backfire appears specific to certain models, indicating that both language and model architecture/training affect alignment outcomes.

Conclusion: Alignment should be understood as a behavioral intervention that can trigger unintended compensatory risks (risk homeostasis) and harmful side effects (iatrogenesis). The language space—shaped by linguistic, pragmatic, and cultural properties of training data—strongly constrains what alignment can achieve. Safety evaluations conducted only in English are not reliable indicators of safety in other languages, and prompt-level alignment techniques cannot fully overcome deeper language-space constraints.

Abstract: In perpetrator treatment, a recurring observation is the dissociation between insight and action: offenders articulate remorse yet behavioral change does not follow. We report four preregistered studies (1,584 multi-agent simulations across 16 languages and three model families) demonstrating that alignment interventions in large language models produce a structurally analogous phenomenon: surface safety that masks or generates collective pathology and internal dissociation. In Study 1 (N = 150), increasing alignment-instructed agents reduced collective pathology in English (g = -1.844, p < .0001) but amplified it in Japanese (g = +0.771, p = .038)--a directional reversal we term "alignment backfire." Study 2 (N = 1,174) extended to 16 languages: alignment-induced dissociation was near-universal (15/16 languages; beta = 0.0667, p < .0001), while collective pathology bifurcated along cultural-linguistic lines (interaction beta = 0.0684, p = .0003), correlating with Power Distance Index (r = 0.474, p = .064). Study 3 (N = 180) tested individuation as countermeasure; individuated agents became the primary source of both pathology and dissociation (DI = +1.120) with conformity above 84%--demonstrating iatrogenesis. Study 4 (N = 80) validated patterns across Llama 3.3 70B, GPT-4o-mini, and Qwen3-Next-80B-A3B, confirming English safety is model-general while Japanese backfire is model-specific. These findings reframe alignment as a behavioral intervention subject to risk homeostasis and iatrogenesis. Language space--the linguistic, pragmatic, and cultural properties inherited from training data--structurally determines alignment outcomes. Safety validated in English does not transfer to other languages, and prompt-level interventions cannot override language-space-level constraints.

*Huixiang Luo,Longyu Gao,Yaqi Liu,Qianqian Chen,Pingchun Huang,Tianning Li*

  <summary>Details</summary>

The motivation is to inject structured human expertise and better sequence-level optimization into bidding algorithms.

Method: KBD (Knowledge-informed Bidding with Dual-process control) embeds human expertise as inductive biases via an informed ML paradigm, uses a Decision Transformer to optimize bidding over multi-step sequences, and implements a dual-process control architecture by combining a fast rule-based PID controller (System 1) with the slower, deliberative Decision Transformer (System 2).

Result: Extensive experiments show that KBD outperforms existing bid optimization methods, demonstrating improved performance especially in settings where human-like adaptability, long-horizon reasoning, and robustness to distribution shifts matter.

Conclusion: Grounding bid optimization in human expertise and a dual-process control framework—combining fast rule-based control with sequence-level reinforcement learning—provides significant advantages over conventional black-box models and is a promising direction for more human-aligned bidding systems.

Abstract: Bid optimization in online advertising relies on black-box machine-learning models that learn bidding decisions from historical data. However, these approaches fail to replicate human experts' adaptive, experience-driven, and globally coherent decisions. Specifically, they generalize poorly in data-sparse cases because of missing structured knowledge, make short-sighted sequential decisions that ignore long-term interdependencies, and struggle to adapt in out-of-distribution scenarios where human experts succeed. To address this, we propose KBD (Knowledge-informed Bidding with Dual-process control), a novel method for bid optimization. KBD embeds human expertise as inductive biases through the informed machine-learning paradigm, uses Decision Transformer (DT) to globally optimize multi-step bidding sequences, and implements dual-process control by combining a fast rule-based PID (System 1) with DT (System 2). Extensive experiments highlight KBD's advantage over existing methods and underscore the benefit of grounding bid optimization in human expertise and dual-process control.

### [112] [TimeWarp: Evaluating Web Agents by Revisiting the Past](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.04949)
*Md Farhan Ishmam,Kenneth Marino*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: TimeWarp is a benchmark and TimeTraj is a training algorithm to test and improve web agents’ robustness to changes in web design over time.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing web-agent benchmarks typically assume a static website UI. However, real-world websites constantly change in layout, design, and interaction patterns. This discrepancy raises the question of whether web agents that perform well on current benchmarks can generalize to new or evolved interfaces. The paper is motivated by the lack of tools to systematically evaluate and improve agents’ robustness to temporal and stylistic changes of the web.

Method: The authors build TimeWarp, a benchmark composed of three distinct web environments (sites), each instantiated in six different UI versions reflecting different ‘eras’ of web design. These versions vary layout, styling, and interaction details while preserving underlying functionality. They then define complex, realistic tasks that require non-trivial navigation and interaction in these environments. Using this setup, they evaluate existing web agents and show they are brittle to UI changes, especially when trained via behavior cloning (BC) on trajectories from a single UI version. To remedy this, they propose TimeTraj: an algorithm that first performs plan distillation to generate high-level plans or teacher rollouts across multiple UI versions, and then trains agents using a BC variant on this diverse set of trajectories, focusing on distilled plans rather than only raw interaction traces from a single version.

Result: Empirical results on TimeWarp demonstrate that current web agents suffer significant performance drops when the UI changes across versions. When trained with standard single-version BC, models generalize poorly to unseen UI variants. Applying TimeTraj, which aggregates teacher rollouts across multiple UI versions and uses plan-focused BC, leads to substantial gains in success rates. Specifically, Qwen-3 4B improves from 20.4% to 37.7% success, and Llama-3.1 8B improves from 0% to 27.0% success on the benchmark. These results illustrate both the difficulty of the evolving-UI setting and the effectiveness of multi-version, plan-based training.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that web agents evaluated on static benchmarks can give a misleading impression of robustness because they often fail when confronted with realistic UI, layout, and design changes. TimeWarp provides a controlled, reproducible way to study this problem by simulating the evolving web. The proposed TimeTraj algorithm shows that collecting and distilling plans or teacher rollouts across multiple UI versions significantly improves generalization to new designs. The authors argue this supports a shift in data collection practices—from single-version trajectories to multi-version, plan-centric supervision—to build more reliable and temporally robust web agents.

Abstract: The improvement of web agents on current benchmarks raises the question: Do today's agents perform just as well when the web changes? We introduce TimeWarp, a benchmark that emulates the evolving web using containerized environments that vary in UI, design, and layout. TimeWarp consists of three web environments, each with six UI versions spanning different eras of the internet, paired with a set of complex, realistic tasks requiring different forms of web navigation. Our experiments reveal web agents' vulnerability to changes and the limitations of behavior cloning (BC) on single-version trajectories. To address this, we propose TimeTraj, a simple yet effective algorithm that uses plan distillation to collect trajectories across multiple versions. By training agents on teacher rollouts using our BC-variant, we achieve substantial performance gains: $20.4\%\rightarrow37.7\%$ for Qwen-3 4B and $0\%\rightarrow27.0\%$ for Llama-3.1 8B models. We hope our work helps researchers study generalization across web designs and unlock a new paradigm for collecting plans rather than trajectories, thereby improving the robustness of web agents.

*Kenny Ye Liang,Zhongyi Pei,Huan Zhang,Yuhui Liu,Shaoxu Song,Jianmin Wang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces RAG4CTS, a training-free, regime-aware retrieval-augmented generation framework tailored for covariate time-series, and shows it greatly improves predictive maintenance of aircraft PRSOV valves over existing methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing RAG methods for time series largely rely on static embeddings and generic context augmenters, which struggle in industrial predictive maintenance settings where data are scarce, sequences are short and transient, and multiple covariates interact in complex ways. These limitations make it hard to distinguish subtly different operating regimes, leading to poor retrieval and degraded forecasting or anomaly detection. There is a need for a RAG approach that is natively designed for covariate time-series, can leverage physics-informed structure, and works without expensive retraining in safety-critical applications like aircraft valve monitoring.

Method: The authors propose RAG4CTS, a regime-aware, training-free RAG framework for covariate time-series data. They first build a hierarchical time-series native knowledge base that stores raw historical regimes in a lossless manner and encodes domain/physics constraints for retrieval. Then they introduce a two-stage bi-weighted retrieval process that aligns candidate historical trends to a query by combining point-wise similarity and multivariate similarity across covariates. Finally, instead of a fixed context augmenter, they use an agent-driven, self-supervised strategy that dynamically selects and optimizes the context passed to the Time-Series Foundation Model, tailoring it to each query instance without additional supervised labels.

Result: On the pressure regulating and shut-off valve (PRSOV) predictive maintenance task, RAG4CTS achieves substantially higher prediction accuracy than state-of-the-art time-series RAG baselines. In a real-world deployment within Apache IoTDB at China Southern Airlines, the system has been running for two months and successfully detected one genuine PRSOV fault with zero false alarms during that period, demonstrating both practical utility and reliability in an industrial environment.

Conclusion: RAG4CTS demonstrates that a regime-aware, training-free RAG framework that operates directly on covariate time-series and uses physics-informed, hierarchical retrieval can overcome key limitations of existing time-series RAG methods in scarce, transient, and coupled-data settings. Its strong performance and fault detection record in PRSOV predictive maintenance indicate that such tailored RAG designs can make Time-Series Foundation Models more trustworthy and effective in high-stakes industrial applications.

Abstract: While RAG has greatly enhanced LLMs, extending this paradigm to Time-Series Foundation Models (TSFMs) remains a challenge. This is exemplified in the Predictive Maintenance of the Pressure Regulating and Shut-Off Valve (PRSOV), a high-stakes industrial scenario characterized by (1) data scarcity, (2) short transient sequences, and (3) covariate coupled dynamics. Unfortunately, existing time-series RAG approaches predominantly rely on generated static vector embeddings and learnable context augmenters, which may fail to distinguish similar regimes in such scarce, transient, and covariate coupled scenarios. To address these limitations, we propose RAG4CTS, a regime-aware, training-free RAG framework for Covariate Time-Series. Specifically, we construct a hierarchal time-series native knowledge base to enable lossless storage and physics-informed retrieval of raw historical regimes. We design a two-stage bi-weighted retrieval mechanism that aligns historical trends through point-wise and multivariate similarities. For context augmentation, we introduce an agent-driven strategy to dynamically optimize context in a self-supervised manner. Extensive experiments on PRSOV demonstrate that our framework significantly outperforms state-of-the-art baselines in prediction accuracy. The proposed system is deployed in Apache IoTDB within China Southern Airlines. Since deployment, our method has successfully identified one PRSOV fault in two months with zero false alarm.

### [114] [Rethinking Representativeness and Diversity in Dynamic Data Selection](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.04981)
*Yuzhe Zhou,Zhenglin Hua,Haiyun Guo,Yuheng Jia*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a new dynamic data selection framework that accelerates training by selecting samples based on dataset-level representativeness and process-level diversity, achieving over 2x speedup while maintaining or improving accuracy.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Training large models on full datasets is computationally expensive; dynamic data selection can reduce cost but existing methods rely on local geometric centrality and within-batch diversity, which may not align with true dataset structure and factor coverage. The authors aim to better formalize what makes a sample useful (representativeness) and how to maintain diversity over time to improve the accuracy-efficiency trade-off.

Method: They redefine representativeness as coverage of common, high-frequency feature factors at the dataset level and diversity as a property of the selection trajectory over training, emphasizing gradual inclusion of rare factors. Concretely, they: (1) Train a sparse autoencoder as a plug-in feature extractor on the target dataset, using sparse unit activations to capture feature factors and compute both per-sample and global factor statistics, then score samples by how well they cover frequent factors. (2) Enforce process-level diversity by rare-factor-aware sampling plus a Usage-Frequency Penalty that encourages rotating which samples are used, theoretically preventing monopoly and lowering gradient bias. (3) Combine these two scores via a smooth time-dependent scheduler that shifts from focusing on core pattern consolidation early in training to exploring rare factors later, without incurring extra gradient or second-order computations on the main model.

Result: On five benchmarks spanning vision and text tasks, and across multiple model architectures, the method yields better accuracy-efficiency trade-offs than baselines. It consistently matches or surpasses the accuracy achieved when training on the full dataset while more than doubling training speed (over 2x acceleration).

Conclusion: Viewing representativeness as dataset-level factor coverage and diversity as a property of the training-time selection process enables a practical dynamic data selection method that significantly accelerates training without sacrificing—and sometimes improving—final accuracy. The sparse autoencoder-based scoring, Usage-Frequency Penalty, and smooth scheduler together provide a plug-and-play framework that requires no extra gradients or higher-order computations on the main model and generalizes well across modalities and benchmarks.

Abstract: Dynamic data selection accelerates training by sampling a changing subset of the dataset while preserving accuracy. We rethink two core notions underlying sample evaluation: representativeness and diversity. Instead of local geometric centrality, we define representativeness as coverage of dataset-level common or high-frequency feature factors. Instead of within-subset dispersion, we define diversity at the process level, requiring the selection trajectory to gradually include complementary rare factors over training. Based on this view, we propose a dynamic selection framework with three components. First, we score representativeness in a plug-in feature space to prioritize samples covering frequent factors. We instantiate this with a sparse autoencoder trained on the target dataset, using sparse unit activations to summarize both individual samples and dataset-wide factor statistics. Second, we realize process-level diversity by combining rare-factor sampling with a Usage-Frequency Penalty that promotes sample rotation, provably discourages monopoly, and reduces gradient bias. Third, we couple the two-dimensional scoring with a smooth scheduler that transitions selection from core-pattern consolidation to rare-factor exploration, without extra gradients, influence estimates, or second-order computations on the training model. Extensive experiments on five benchmarks across vision and text tasks demonstrate improved accuracy-efficiency trade-offs across models. Our method matches or exceeds full-data accuracy with over 2x training acceleration. Code will be released.

</details>

### [117] [AegisUI: Behavioral Anomaly Detection for Structured User Interface Protocols in AI Agent Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.05031)
*Mohd Safwan Uddin,Saba Hajira*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces AegisUI, a framework to detect malicious behavior in dynamically generated UI payloads beyond schema-level checks, and evaluates several anomaly detection approaches on a newly created dataset.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Dynamic, AI-assembled user interfaces rely on structured payloads that can be syntactically valid yet semantically deceptive, enabling attacks like misleading buttons or hidden data binding that existing schema-based defenses cannot detect. The authors aim to close this gap by systematically modeling and detecting behavioral mismatches between UI intent and action.

Method: The authors design AegisUI, which (1) generates structured UI payloads for multiple application domains, (2) injects realistic attack patterns corresponding to different UI abuse families, (3) extracts 18 numeric features from each payload across structural, semantic, binding, and session dimensions, and (4) benchmarks three anomaly detection models—Isolation Forest, a benign-trained autoencoder, and a supervised Random Forest—on the resulting labeled dataset.

Result: They construct a dataset of 4000 labeled payloads (3000 benign, 1000 malicious) across five domains and five attack types (phishing interfaces, data leakage, layout abuse, manipulative UI, workflow anomalies). On an 80/20 stratified train-test split, Random Forest performs best (accuracy 0.931, precision 0.980, recall 0.740, F1 0.843, ROC-AUC 0.952), with the autoencoder second (F1 0.762, ROC-AUC 0.863) and Isolation Forest worse; per-attack analysis shows layout abuse is easiest to detect, while manipulative UI is hardest. The authors release all code, data, and configs.

Conclusion: Behavioral mismatches in dynamically generated UIs form a distinct, practically important attack surface that schema validation alone cannot protect against. AegisUI demonstrates that relatively simple feature engineering and conventional anomaly detectors—particularly supervised Random Forests, and to a lesser extent semi-supervised autoencoders—can meaningfully identify such malicious payloads, providing a baseline and reproducible framework for further research on secure AI-driven UI generation.

Abstract: AI agents that build user interfaces on the fly assembling buttons, forms, and data displays from structured protocol payloads are becoming common in production systems. The trouble is that a payload can pass every schema check and still trick a user: a button might say "View invoice" while its hidden action wipes an account, or a display widget might quietly bind to an internal salary field. Current defenses stop at syntax; they were never built to catch this kind of behavioral mismatch.
  We built AegisUI to study exactly this gap. The framework generates structured UI payloads, injects realistic attacks into them, extracts numeric features, and benchmarks anomaly detectors end-to-end. We produced 4000 labeled payloads (3000 benign, 1000 malicious) spanning five application domains and five attack families: phishing interfaces, data leakage, layout abuse, manipulative UI, and workflow anomalies.
  From each payload we extracted 18 features covering structural, semantic, binding, and session dimensions, then compared three detectors: Isolation Forest (unsupervised), a benign-trained autoencoder (semi-supervised), and Random Forest (supervised). On a stratified 80/20 split, Random Forest scored best overall (accuracy 0.931, precision 0.980, recall 0.740, F1 0.843, ROC-AUC 0.952). The autoencoder came second (F1 0.762, ROC-AUC 0.863) and needs no malicious labels at training time, which matters when deploying a new system that lacks attack history. Per-attack-type analysis showed that layout abuse is easiest to catch while manipulative UI payloads are hardest. All code, data, and configurations are released for full reproducibility.

</details>

Domain knowledge structured the AI's logical behavior, design shaped the human-AI interaction, and AI capabilities extended students' cognitive capacities.

The research examines a graduate course, Creating the Frontier of No-code Smart Cities at the Singapore University of Technology and Design (SUTD), in which students developed domain-specific custom GPT systems without coding. Specifically, we enhance PLMs with the ability to imagine by embedding an image generator directly into the reasoning pipeline. To facilitate effective utilization of this imagined visual context, we construct synthetic datasets designed to emulate visual question-answering scenarios. Through comprehensive evaluations on multiple commonsense reasoning benchmarks, we demonstrate that Imagine substantially outperforms existing zero-shot approaches and even surpasses advanced large language models. These results underscore the capability of machine imagination to mitigate reporting bias and significantly enhance the generalization ability of commonsense reasoning models

Trained on synthetic data from only 10 websites within WebFactory, it achieves performance comparable to GUI agents trained on the same amount of human-annotated data from a much larger set of environments. ### [121] [Jagarin: A Three-Layer Architecture for Hibernating Personal Duty Agents on Mobile](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.05069)
*Ravi Kiran Kadaboina*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Jagarin is a three-layer architecture that lets personal AI agents act proactively on time-sensitive tasks from email without needing constant background execution or cloud state, by combining on-device urgency estimation, an email proxy, and a machine-readable institutional communication protocol.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Personal AI agents on mobile either run persistently in the background (draining battery and conflicting with platform restrictions) or remain purely reactive (missing deadlines and time-sensitive obligations until manually invoked). There is a need for an architecture that allows proactive, timely agent behavior driven by real-world obligations—especially those arriving via commercial email—while respecting mobile OS constraints, preserving privacy, and avoiding continuous cloud dependence.

Method: The paper introduces Jagarin, a three-layer system architecture. Layer 1, DAWN, is an on-device engine that computes a composite urgency score for pending duties using four factors: time windows for optimal action, predicted user engagement, opportunity cost of inaction, and synergy across multiple duties. It then uses adaptive, per-user thresholds to decide when to wake or escalate the agent. Layer 2, ARIA, acts as an email identity proxy that ingests the user’s commercial inbox and routes messages by category to appropriate DAWN handlers, avoiding manual configuration and cold start. Layer 3, ACE, defines a protocol for institutions to send structured, machine-readable messages directly to personal agents instead of human-oriented emails. The authors implement a working prototype in Flutter on Android, with an ephemeral cloud agent activated only on explicit user escalation.

Result: The authors build and demonstrate a functioning Android prototype that integrates all three layers: DAWN on-device urgency management, ARIA as the commercial email proxy, and ACE as the machine-readable institutional channel. The system operates without persistent background execution or continuous cloud state, relying instead on structured hibernation and demand-driven wake, and uses only an ephemeral cloud component when users escalate tasks. The prototype shows that the proposed architecture is practically implementable under mobile platform constraints.

Conclusion: Jagarin resolves the deployment paradox of proactive personal AI agents on mobile by structuring when and how agents wake, what inputs they receive, and how institutions communicate with them. By combining on-device duty-aware wake logic (DAWN), an email-based identity and routing layer (ARIA), and a new agent-centric communication protocol (ACE), the architecture enables timely, proactive assistance driven by real-world obligations while adhering to mobile OS power and sandboxing constraints and minimizing privacy risks and persistent cloud dependence.

Abstract: Personal AI agents face a fundamental deployment paradox on mobile: persistent background execution drains battery and violates platform sandboxing policies, yet purely reactive agents miss time-sensitive obligations until the user remembers to ask. We present Jagarin, a three-layer architecture that resolves this paradox through structured hibernation and demand-driven wake. The first layer, DAWN (Duty-Aware Wake Network), is an on-device heuristic engine that computes a composite urgency score from four signals: duty-typed optimal action windows, user behavioral engagement prediction, opportunity cost of inaction, and cross-duty batch resonance. It uses adaptive per-user thresholds to decide when a sleeping agent should nudge or escalate. The second layer, ARIA (Agent Relay Identity Architecture), is a commercial email identity proxy that routes the full commercial inbox -- obligations, promotional offers, loyalty rewards, and platform updates -- to appropriate DAWN handlers by message category, eliminating cold-start and removing manual data entry. The third layer, ACE (Agent-Centric Exchange), is a protocol framework for direct machine-readable communication from institutions to personal agents, replacing human-targeted email as the canonical channel. Together, these three layers form a complete stack from institutional signal to on-device action, without persistent cloud state, continuous background execution, or privacy compromise. A working Flutter prototype is demonstrated on Android, combining all three layers with an ephemeral cloud agent invoked only on user-initiated escalation.

### [122] [Bidirectional Curriculum Generation: A Multi-Agent Framework for Data-Efficient Mathematical Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.05120)
*Boren Hu,Xiao Liu,Boci Peng,Xinping Zhao,Xiaoran Shang,Yun Zhu,Lijun Wu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose a bidirectional curriculum generation framework for training LLMs on math reasoning that can both increase and decrease problem difficulty, yielding better reasoning with fewer training samples.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing methods to improve mathematical reasoning in LLMs require huge datasets and use mostly simple-to-complex curriculum learning. This is inefficient because the model is pushed to harder problems even when it still fails on basics, wasting computation on unsolvable tasks. A more data-efficient, adaptive curriculum is needed.

Method: They design a multi-agent, bidirectional curriculum generation ecosystem that forms a closed feedback loop with the target model. Based on the model’s current reasoning performance, the system either complicates problems to push the frontier of ability or simplifies them to target and repair specific failures. The curriculum pacing is theoretically grounded in an Optimal Pacing Theorem that guides when to move up or down in difficulty so each sample has maximal instructional value.

Result: The bidirectional curriculum trains models that significantly outperform baseline methods on mathematical reasoning benchmarks while using substantially fewer instruction samples, demonstrating improved data efficiency and better generalization.

Conclusion: An adaptively generated, bidirectional curriculum—capable of both increasing and decreasing task difficulty in response to model performance—leads to more efficient and effective training of LLMs for mathematical reasoning than standard unidirectional curricula, validating the proposed Optimal Pacing framework.

Abstract: Enhancing mathematical reasoning in Large Language Models typically demands massive datasets, yet data efficiency remains a critical bottleneck. While Curriculum Learning attempts to structure this process, standard unidirectional approaches (simple-to-complex) suffer from inefficient sample utilization: they blindly escalate complexity even when foundational gaps persist, leading to wasted computation on unsolvable problems. To maximize the instructional value of every training sample, we introduce a novel Bidirectional Curriculum Generation framework. Unlike rigid trajectories, our multi-agent ecosystem mimics adaptive pedagogy to establish a closed feedback loop. It dynamically generates data by either complicating problems to challenge the model or, crucially, simplying them to repair specific reasoning failures. This mechanism ensures that the model consumes only the most effective data at any given stage. Grounded in the Optimal Pacing Theorem, our approach optimizes the learning trajectory, significantly outperforming baselines while achieving superior reasoning performance with substantially fewer instruction samples.

Existing AI tools and LLM-based diagnostic systems lack transparency, structured reasoning, use of rich structured clinical data, and realistic deployability. Current RAG and multi-agent approaches typically pull evidence from a single source and cannot support iterative, role-specialized, multidisciplinary-style deliberation, which limits trust and clinical usefulness.

Method: The authors propose MedCoRAG, an end-to-end system that starts from standardized abnormal clinical findings and generates diagnostic hypotheses. It builds a patient-specific evidence package by jointly retrieving and pruning information from the UMLS knowledge graph and relevant clinical guidelines. A Multi-Agent Collaborative Reasoning scheme is then employed: (1) a Router Agent decides which Specialist Agents to involve based on case complexity; (2) Specialist Agents iteratively reason over the evidence, request targeted re-retrievals, and refine hypotheses; (3) a Generalist Agent aggregates the deliberations and forms a transparent consensus diagnosis that mimics multidisciplinary consultation. The system is evaluated on hepatic disease cases from the MIMIC-IV database and compared against existing methods and closed-source LLMs.

Conclusion: MedCoRAG demonstrates that combining structured clinical findings with multi-source retrieval (UMLS KG plus guidelines) and collaborative multi-agent LLM reasoning can improve both accuracy and interpretability of AI-assisted hepatic disease diagnosis. The framework offers a more deployable and trustworthy approach to clinical decision support by emulating multidisciplinary consultation and providing traceable evidence-based reasoning, and it could likely be extended beyond hepatic diseases to other clinical domains.

Abstract: Diagnosing hepatic diseases accurately and interpretably is critical, yet it remains challenging in real-world clinical settings. Existing AI approaches for clinical diagnosis often lack transparency, structured reasoning, and deployability. Recent efforts have leveraged large language models (LLMs), retrieval-augmented generation (RAG), and multi-agent collaboration. However, these approaches typically retrieve evidence from a single source and fail to support iterative, role-specialized deliberation grounded in structured clinical data. To address this, we propose MedCoRAG (i.e., Medical Collaborative RAG), an end-to-end framework that generates diagnostic hypotheses from standardized abnormal findings and constructs a patient-specific evidence package by jointly retrieving and pruning UMLS knowledge graph paths and clinical guidelines. It then performs Multi-Agent Collaborative Reasoning: a Router Agent dynamically dispatches Specialist Agents based on case complexity; these agents iteratively reason over the evidence and trigger targeted re-retrievals when needed, while a Generalist Agent synthesizes all deliberations into a traceable consensus diagnosis that emulates multidisciplinary consultation. Experimental results on hepatic disease cases from MIMIC-IV show that MedCoRAG outperforms existing methods and closed-source models in both diagnostic performance and reasoning interpretability.

*Jonathan D. Result: Agents trained with the proposed pipeline (KARL) achieve Pareto-optimal performance relative to Claude 4.6 and GPT 5.2 on KARLBench when considering both quality vs. cost and quality vs. latency. Conclusion: Combining carefully designed synthetic data generation, multi-task training across heterogeneous search behaviors, and iterative large-batch off-policy RL yields enterprise search agents that are both high-performing and cost-efficient. First, we introduce KARLBench, a multi-capability evaluation suite spanning six distinct search regimes, including constraint-driven entity search, cross-document report synthesis, tabular numerical reasoning, exhaustive entity retrieval, procedural reasoning over technical documentation, and fact aggregation over internal enterprise notes. Second, we show that models trained across heterogeneous search behaviors generalize substantially better than those optimized for any single benchmark. Third, we develop an agentic synthesis pipeline that employs long-horizon reasoning and tool use to generate diverse, grounded, and high-quality training data, with iterative bootstrapping from increasingly capable models. Fourth, we propose a new post-training paradigm based on iterative large-batch off-policy RL that is sample efficient, robust to train-inference engine discrepancies, and naturally extends to multi-task training with out-of-distribution generalization. Compared to Claude 4.6 and GPT 5.2, KARL is Pareto-optimal on KARLBench across cost-quality and latency-quality trade-offs, including tasks that were out-of-distribution during training. With sufficient test-time compute, it surpasses the strongest closed models. ### [126] [Reclaiming Lost Text Layers for Source-Free Cross-Domain Few-Shot Learning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.05235)
*Zhenyu Zhang,Guangyao Chen,Yixiong Zou,Yuhua Li,Ruixuan Li*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies why removing certain middle layers of CLIP’s text encoder helps Source-Free Cross-Domain Few-Shot Learning, finds that these layers actually contain useful information that is not well aligned with visuals under domain shifts, and proposes a method to re-utilize this information to guide better visual adaptation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In Source-Free Cross-Domain Few-Shot Learning (SF-CDFSL), models must adapt to new domains (like medical or satellite images) using very few labeled samples and without access to source-domain data. CLIP has proven strong for such cross-domain transfer, and prior work suggests its text encoder is especially helpful. Empirically, however, removing some middle layers of the CLIP text encoder unexpectedly improves performance, which is counterintuitive because it discards representational capacity. This puzzling behavior suggests a deeper misalignment between text and vision representations under domain shift, and motivates studying what information these “lost layers” contain and how to harness it rather than simply pruning them away.

Method: The authors first conduct an empirical and analytical study of CLIP’s text encoder layers in the SF-CDFSL setting to understand why dropping certain middle layers helps. They analyze the role of these layers with respect to visual-text alignment and domain shift, revealing that the layers carry beneficial information that is poorly exploited due to visual gaps across domains. Based on these insights, they propose a new training framework that re-utilizes information from the so-called lost layers at two levels: (1) the layer level, where intermediate text-layer representations are explicitly involved in guiding the learning of visual features, and (2) the encoder level, where the full text encoder’s knowledge is distilled or aligned to steer the visual branch’s adaptation under domain shift. The approach effectively teaches the model to re-leverage intermediate text features instead of discarding them, using them as supervision or guidance signals when fine-tuning on target few-shot data.

Result: Through extensive experiments on multiple cross-domain few-shot benchmarks (4 CDFSL datasets and 10 Meta-dataset datasets), and with different backbone models (CLIP, SigLip, and PE-Core), the proposed method consistently outperforms both standard CLIP-based baselines and prior approaches that simply remove the middle text layers. The results show notable gains in SF-CDFSL accuracy, confirming that properly reusing the information from the lost layers is more effective than pruning them and that the proposed re-utilization strategy is robust across architectures and datasets.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that the middle layers of CLIP’s text encoder, previously thought to be redundant or harmful in SF-CDFSL, actually contain useful information that becomes underutilized due to visual domain gaps. Removing these layers only superficially improves performance by avoiding misalignment, but wastes valuable semantic knowledge. By designing mechanisms to re-utilize these intermediate text representations at both the layer and encoder levels, the authors effectively guide the visual encoder’s adaptation to new domains and significantly improve cross-domain few-shot learning performance. This challenges the prevailing practice of layer pruning in CLIP-based SF-CDFSL and highlights the importance of better vision-text alignment under domain shifts.

Abstract: Source-Free Cross-Domain Few-Shot Learning (SF-CDFSL) focuses on fine-tuning with limited training data from target domains (e.g., medical or satellite images), where CLIP has recently shown promising results due to its generalizability to downstream tasks. Current works indicate CLIP's text encoder is more suitable for cross-domain tasks, however, we find that \textbf{removing certain middle layers of the text encoder can effectively improve performance in SF-CDFSL}, which we call the Lost Layers. In this paper, we delve into this phenomenon for a deeper understanding. We discover that instead of being harmful for the SF-CDFSL task, the information in these layers is actually beneficial, but visual gaps prevent this useful information from being fully utilized, making these layers seem redundant. Based on this understanding, unlike current works that simply remove these layers, we propose a method to teachs the model to \textbf{re-utilize} information in these lost layers at both the layer and encoder levels, guiding the re-learning of the visual branch under domain shifts. Our approach effectively addresses the issue of underutilized information in the text encoder. Extensive experiments across various settings, backbones (CLIP, SigLip, PE-Core), and tasks (4 CDFSL datasets and 10 Meta-dataset datasets) demonstrate the effectiveness of our method. Code is available at https://github.com/zhenyuZ-HUST/CVPR26-VtT.

### [128] [X-RAY: Mapping LLM Reasoning Capability via Formalized and Calibrated Probes](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.05290)
*Gao Tianxi,Cai Yufan,Yuan Yusi,Dong Jin Song*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces X-RAY, a framework that uses formally verified, structurally controlled probes to map and analyze the reasoning capabilities of large language models (LLMs) beyond simple accuracy metrics.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although LLMs perform well on many tasks, their actual reasoning capabilities are unclear because standard evaluations mostly measure final accuracy, blending genuine reasoning with pattern matching and memorization. The field lacks fine-grained, interpretable tools to isolate and characterize what kinds of reasoning structures LLMs can reliably handle and where they fail. The authors aim to provide a principled, explainable way to probe and map LLM reasoning, especially in mathematically structured domains.

Method: They design X-RAY, an explainable reasoning analysis system that creates families of formally specified problems (probes) in domains like mathematics, physics, and chemistry. These probes are generated and calibrated using formal tools so that key structural properties—such as how constraints interact, the depth of reasoning required, and the geometry/shape of the solution space—are precisely controlled and varied. The probes are formally verified to ensure correctness and to calibrate incremental changes in structural complexity, enabling controlled experiments that can disentangle different aspects of reasoning capability. State-of-the-art LLMs are then evaluated on these structured probe families to map how performance changes as structure changes.

Result: They find a consistent asymmetry in LLM reasoning behavior: models handle constraint refinement relatively well (i.e., when extra conditions simply narrow an existing solution set) but their performance degrades sharply when the structure of the solution space is reconfigured (e.g., when constraints alter the qualitative shape or topology of the solution manifold). Additionally, the formally calibrated probes can distinguish between LLMs that seem equivalent on coarse-grained benchmarks and uncover specific, structurally interpretable failure modes, rather than opaque or ad-hoc errors.

Conclusion: Formally controlled and verified structural probes, as implemented in X-RAY, provide a more informative and interpretable way to analyze and compare LLM reasoning than traditional accuracy-based benchmarks. The discovered asymmetry between robustness to constraint refinement and vulnerability to solution-space restructuring highlights a structural weakness in current models. The framework is contamination-free and suitable not only for evaluation but also for training and testing future models explicitly targeted at improved reasoning capabilities.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) achieve promising performance, yet their ability to reason remains poorly understood. Existing evaluations largely emphasize task-level accuracy, often conflating pattern matching with reasoning capability. We present X-RAY, an explainable reasoning analysis system that maps the LLM reasoning capability using calibrated, formally verified probes. We model reasoning capability as a function of extractable \textit{structure}, operationalized through formal properties such as constraint interaction, reasoning depth, and solution-space geometry. X-Ray generates probes via formal tools with controlled structural variations, enabling precise isolation of incremental structural information through formal calibration and verification. We evaluate state-of-the-art LLMs on problems ranging from junior-level to advanced in mathematics, physics, and chemistry. Our analysis reveals a systematic asymmetry in LLM reasoning: models are relatively robust to constraint refinement, where additional conditions shrink an existing solution space, but degrade sharply under solution-space restructuring, where modifications alter the underlying structural form of the solution manifold. Moreover, calibrated formal probes differentiate models that appear indistinguishable on standard benchmarks and reveal failure modes that are structurally interpretable rather than opaque. Beyond evaluation, our framework is contamination-free and supports the training and testing of reasoning models.

</details>

Method: The authors propose UniSTOK, a plug-and-play framework that can be attached to existing inductive kriging backbones. Result: Across multiple real-world datasets and various missing data patterns, UniSTOK consistently and significantly improves the performance of underlying inductive spatio-temporal kriging models in handling missing observations.

Conclusion: By explicitly modeling missingness, using proxy synthesis only at missing locations, and adaptively fusing dual-branch representations, UniSTOK provides a general, plug-and-play way to robustify spatio-temporal kriging models against heterogeneous missing sensor data, leading to better inference at unobserved locations in practical monitoring applications.

In practice, however, observed sensors themselves often exhibit heterogeneous missingness, forcing inductive kriging models to rely on crudely imputed inputs. This setting brings three key challenges: (1) it is unclear whether an value is a true signal or a missingness-induced artifact; (2) missingness is highly heterogeneous across sensors and time; (3) missing observations distort the local spatio-temporal structure. To address these issues, we propose Uniform Inductive Spatio-Temporal Kriging (UniSTOK), a plug-and-play framework that enhances existing inductive kriging backbones under missing observation. Our framework forms a dual-branch input consisting of the original observations and a jigsaw-augmented counterpart that synthesizes proxy signals only at missing entries. The two branches are then processed in parallel by a shared spatio-temporal backbone with explicit missingness mask modulation. Their outputs are finally adaptively fused via dual-channel attention. Experiments on multiple real-world datasets under diverse missing patterns demonstrate consistent and significant improvements.

### [133] [Ailed: A Psyche-Driven Chess Engine with Dynamic Emotional Modulation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.05352)
*Diego Armando Resendez Prado*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces a general framework that adds human‑like, psychologically driven variability to chess engines by dynamically reshaping move probability distributions using an audio-signal–inspired pipeline.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although modern chess engines far exceed human playing strength, their behavior is unnaturally consistent and fails to reflect human-like psychological swings such as stress, tilt, or overconfidence. The author wants a way to make engine play exhibit realistic behavioral variability that resembles patterns seen in human games, while remaining model-agnostic and not relying on search or heavy state tracking.

Method: The author decomposes behavior into two parts: (1) a static "personality" preset that characterizes the engine’s general style, and (2) a dynamic "psyche" variable ψ_t in [-100, 100] that is recalculated after every move from five positional factors. These two components feed a signal-processing chain inspired by audio engineering—consisting of a noise gate, compressor/expander, five-band equalizer, and saturation limiter—that continuously reshapes the engine’s move probability distribution without altering the underlying engine. The framework only requires access to move probabilities and does not maintain additional search state. Experiments are carried out over 12,414 games against Maia2-1100 using two different probability sources with vastly different training-data sizes to test robustness of the behavioral effects.

Result: Across 12,414 games against Maia2-1100, both probability sources—despite differing by about 2,800x in training data—show a similar, monotonic gradient in top-move agreement of roughly 20–25 percentage points from stressed to overconfident psyche states. This indicates that the behavioral variation stems primarily from the signal chain rather than from the underlying model. In the overconfident regime, the chain minimally interferes, yielding about 66% agreement with the base Maia2 outputs. Under stress, performance and agreement degrade: the competitive score drops from 50.8% to 30.1%, reflecting substantially weaker and less accurate play.

Conclusion: The proposed personality–psyche framework, combined with an audio-inspired signal-processing chain applied to move probabilities, can induce systematic, human-like behavioral variability in chess engines independent of the specific model used. The patterns of degraded performance under stress and near-normal play under overconfidence resemble phenomena such as tilt and overconfidence reported in human players. However, the author emphasizes that the work does not include human-subject validation, so claims about psychological realism remain suggestive rather than empirically confirmed.

Abstract: Chess engines passed human strength years ago, but they still don't play like humans. A grandmaster under clock pressure blunders in ways a club player on a hot streak never would. Conventional engines capture none of this.
  This paper proposes a personality x psyche decomposition to produce behavioral variability in chess play, drawing on patterns observed in human games. Personality is static -- a preset that pins down the engine's character. Psyche is dynamic -- a bounded scalar ψ_t \in [-100, +100], recomputed from five positional factors after every move. These two components feed into an audio-inspired signal chain (noise gate, compressor/expander, five-band equalizer, saturation limiter) that reshapes move probability distributions on the fly. The chain doesn't care what engine sits behind it: any system that outputs move probabilities will do. It needs no search and carries no state beyond ψ_t.
  I test the framework across 12,414 games against Maia2-1100, feeding it two probability sources that differ by ~2,800x in training data. Both show the same monotonic gradient in top-move agreement (~20-25 pp spread from stress to overconfidence), which tells us the behavioral variation comes from the signal chain, not from the model underneath. When the psyche runs overconfident, the chain mostly gets out of the way (66% agreement with vanilla Maia2). Under stress, the competitive score falls from 50.8% to 30.1%. The patterns are reminiscent of tilt and overconfidence as described in human play, but I should be upfront: this study includes no human-subject validation.

Abstract: We present the Judge Reliability Harness, an open source library for constructing validation suites that test the reliability of LLM judges. As LLM based scoring is widely deployed in AI benchmarks, more tooling is needed to efficiently assess the reliability of these methods. Given a benchmark dataset and an LLM judge configuration, the harness generates reliability tests that evaluate both binary judgment accuracy and ordinal grading performance for free-response and agentic task formats. We evaluate four state-of-the-art judges across four benchmarks spanning safety, persuasion, misuse, and agentic behavior, and find meaningful variation in performance across models and perturbation types, highlighting opportunities to improve the robustness of LLM judges. No judge that we evaluated is uniformly reliable across benchmarks using our harness. For example, our preliminary experiments on judges revealed consistency issues as measured by accuracy in judging another LLM's ability to complete a task due to simple text formatting changes, paraphrasing, changes in verbosity, and flipping the ground truth label in LLM-produced responses.
