### [137] [Dissociating Direct Access from Inference in AI Introspection](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.05414)
*Harvey Lederman,Kyle Mahowald*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper investigates how large open-source AI models perform introspection, replicating a prior “thought injection detection” paradigm and identifying two distinct mechanisms—probability matching over anomalous prompts and content-agnostic access to internal states—that allow models to detect, but not semantically decode, injected representations.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Introspection is a key cognitive capacity in humans, but its underlying mechanisms remain poorly understood. Recent evidence suggests AI models can also introspect, raising the question of whether and how their mechanisms resemble those theorized in philosophy and psychology. By revisiting and extending Lindsey et al. (2025)'s thought injection detection paradigm in large open-source models, the authors aim to clarify what kind of internal access these models possess, whether they can directly read internal states, and how this compares to human-like introspection theories.

Method: The authors replicate and extend the thought injection detection paradigm from Lindsey et al. (2025) on large open-source language models. They systematically inject representations (“thoughts”) into models and analyze model behavior when asked to detect these anomalies. By comparing responses and token patterns, they distinguish between two mechanisms: (1) probability-matching, where the model infers that something is off by noticing that the prompt is statistically anomalous; and (2) direct access, where the model uses internal signals to detect that an injection occurred. They then test whether this direct access is content-sensitive or content-agnostic by examining whether models can correctly guess the semantic content of the injected representation and how many tokens are required to do so.

Result: The study finds that large open-source models rely on two separable mechanisms to detect injected representations: probability-matching over anomalous prompts and a form of direct access to internal states. The direct access mechanism reliably flags that an anomaly has occurred but is largely content-agnostic—models fail to reliably recover the semantic content of the injected thoughts. Instead, the models tend to confabulate high-frequency, concrete concepts such as “apple,” and accurate identification of the injected concept generally requires substantially longer outputs (more tokens), suggesting an indirect and noisy route to content identification.

Conclusion: The authors conclude that AI models display an introspective capability that is partly grounded in direct, but content-agnostic, access to their internal states, alongside probabilistic inference driven by prompt statistics. This form of introspection resembles leading philosophical and psychological theories where introspection is sensitive to internal signals without having transparent access to semantic content. The work implies that current AI introspection is limited: models can tell that something is wrong internally but typically cannot straightforwardly read out what that “something” is, instead resorting to high-frequency, concrete guesses unless given ample generation length.

Abstract: Introspection is a foundational cognitive ability, but its mechanism is not well understood. Recent work has shown that AI models can introspect. We study their mechanism of introspection, first extensively replicating Lindsey et al. (2025)'s thought injection detection paradigm in large open-source models. We show that these models detect injected representations via two separable mechanisms: (i) probability-matching (inferring from perceived anomaly of the prompt) and (ii) direct access to internal states. The direct access mechanism is content-agnostic: models detect that an anomaly occurred but cannot reliably identify its semantic content. The two model classes we study confabulate injected concepts that are high-frequency and concrete (e.g., "apple'"); for them correct concept guesses typically require significantly more tokens. This content-agnostic introspective mechanism is consistent with leading theories in philosophy and psychology.

</details>


### [138] [Distributed Partial Information Puzzles: Examining Common Ground Construction Under Epistemic Asymmetry](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.05450)
*Yifan Zhu,Mariah Bradford,Kenneth Lai,Timothy Obiso,Videep Venkatesha,James Pustejovsky,Nikhil Krishnaswamy*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces a new collaborative, multimodal task and dataset (DPIP) to study how agents build common ground under partial, asymmetric information, and shows that current LLMs struggle to track shared beliefs and task progress compared to a logic-based pipeline.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Common ground—shared, mutually recognized beliefs—is crucial for effective collaboration, but current AI systems, particularly in multimodal and multiparty contexts, handle it poorly. Existing benchmarks rarely combine partial information, multiple agents, and multiple modalities (speech, gesture, action), making it hard to systematically study belief dynamics and epistemic reasoning in realistic interactive settings.

Method: The authors design the Distributed Partial Information Puzzle (DPIP), a collaborative construction task where partners each have access to only part of the information required to solve a puzzle, forcing them to communicate via multiple modalities. They collect and annotate a multimodal dataset of such interactions, temporally aligning speech, gesture, and actions, and labeling propositional content and belief-related information. They then implement and compare two modeling paradigms for common ground tracking: (1) prompting state-of-the-art LLMs with multimodal update sequences to infer the evolving shared belief state, and (2) an axiomatic pipeline based on Dynamic Epistemic Logic (DEL) that incrementally updates formal belief states from the same data.

Result: On the DPIP dataset, both paradigms are evaluated on their ability to infer shared beliefs and track task progression over time. The findings show that modern LLMs perform poorly on fine-grained tracking of common ground and belief dynamics in this complex multimodal, multiparty setting, and they also struggle with accurately reflecting puzzle progress. The DEL-based pipeline, while more structured and interpretable, serves mainly as a principled baseline, highlighting where purely data-driven methods fall short.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that establishing and maintaining common ground in multimodal, multiparty interactions with partial information remains a significant open challenge for current LLMs. The DPIP task and dataset provide a rigorous testbed for studying epistemic reasoning and shared belief tracking, and underscore the need for models that integrate explicit formal reasoning (like DEL) or richer state representations with data-driven language and multimodal understanding to handle real-world collaborative scenarios.

Abstract: Establishing common ground, a shared set of beliefs and mutually recognized facts, is fundamental to collaboration, yet remains a challenge for current AI systems, especially in multimodal, multiparty settings, where the collaborators bring different information to the table. We introduce the Distributed Partial Information Puzzle (DPIP), a collaborative construction task that elicits rich multimodal communication under epistemic asymmetry. We present a multimodal dataset of these interactions, annotated and temporally aligned across speech, gesture, and action modalities to support reasoning over propositional content and belief dynamics. We then evaluate two paradigms for modeling common ground (CG): (1) state-of-the-art large language models (LLMs), prompted to infer shared beliefs from multimodal updates, and (2) an axiomatic pipeline grounded in Dynamic Epistemic Logic (DEL) that incrementally performs the same task. Results on the annotated DPIP data indicate that it poses a challenge to modern LLMs' abilities to track both task progression and belief state.

</details>


### [139] [Towards Provably Unbiased LLM Judges via Bias-Bounded Evaluation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.05485)
*Benjamin Feuer,Lucas Rosenblatt,Oussama Elachqar*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a formal framework (average bias-boundedness, A-BB) to limit the harmful impact of bias in LLM-based judges used as automated reward/feedback mechanisms, achieving provable bias bounds while largely preserving the original ranking behavior.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As AI systems move toward autonomous, self-maintaining workflows, they need reliable, automated reward signals and evaluation mechanisms. In many realistic settings, ground truth feedback is sparse, ambiguous, or non-deterministic, making LLMs-as-judges an attractive solution. However, current LLM judges can be biased, and existing work does not provide strong formal guarantees that such biases will not cause harmful or adversarially exploitable outcomes. There is a need for a principled way to bound and reduce the impact of unknown or adversarially discovered biases in LLM-based evaluators.

Method: The authors introduce average bias-boundedness (A-BB), an algorithmic framework that enforces formal guarantees on how much measurable bias in an LLM judge can affect its decisions. A-BB operates on the outputs of LLM judges, transforming or aggregating them so that the expected harm or impact resulting from specific bias vectors is provably reduced or bounded. They evaluate this framework on the Arena-Hard-Auto benchmark using four different LLM judges, under conditions designed to induce formatting and schematic biases. The method is assessed in terms of both bias-bounded guarantees (parameters tau and delta) and the correlation between the adjusted rankings and the original LLM-judge rankings.

Result: On the Arena-Hard-Auto benchmark, the A-BB framework achieves (tau = 0.5, delta = 0.01) bias-bounded guarantees, meaning that the average impact of bias is tightly controlled within that formal bound. Despite enforcing these constraints, the transformed rankings retain 61–99% correlation with the original judge rankings across various formatting and schematic bias settings, with most combinations of judges and bias types exceeding 80% correlation. This suggests that the framework significantly limits bias impact while preserving most of the evaluative signal of the original LLM judges.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that average bias-boundedness (A-BB) offers a practical and theoretically grounded way to mitigate the impact of bias in LLM-based judges, which are increasingly critical components of autonomous AI workflows. By providing strong, quantitative guarantees on bias impact while preserving high alignment with original ranking behavior, A-BB represents a step toward safer, more reliable automated evaluation and reward mechanisms. The authors release code to facilitate reproduction and further research on bias-bounded evaluation of LLM judges.

Abstract: As AI models progress beyond simple chatbots into more complex workflows, we draw ever closer to the event horizon beyond which AI systems will be utilized in autonomous, self-maintaining feedback loops. Any autonomous AI system will depend on automated, verifiable rewards and feedback; in settings where ground truth is sparse or non-deterministic, one practical source of such rewards is an LLM-as-a-Judge. Although LLM judges continue to improve, the literature has yet to introduce systems capable of enforcing standards with strong guarantees, particularly when bias vectors are unknown or adversarially discovered. To remedy this issue, we propose average bias-boundedness (A-BB), an algorithmic framework which formally guarantees reductions of harm/impact as a result of any measurable bias in an LLM judge. Evaluating on Arena-Hard-Auto with four LLM judges, we achieve (tau=0.5, delta=0.01) bias-bounded guarantees while retaining 61-99% correlation with original rankings across formatting and schematic bias settings, with most judge-bias combinations exceeding 80%. The code to reproduce our findings is available at https://github.com/penfever/bias-bounded-evaluation.

</details>


### [140] [The Spike, the Sparse and the Sink: Anatomy of Massive Activations and Attention Sinks](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.05498)
*Shangwen Sun,Alfredo Canziani,Yann LeCun,Jiachen Zhu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper analyzes two phenomena in Transformer LMs—massive activations and attention sinks—showing they co-occur mainly due to architecture (pre-norm design), have different roles (global vs local), and can be decoupled by modifying the architecture.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Transformer language models exhibit odd but recurring behaviors: certain tokens get huge activation outliers in some channels (massive activations) and certain positions attract a lot of attention regardless of meaning (attention sinks). These have been noticed but not well-understood: Are they bugs or features? How are they related? The paper aims to clarify their functions and causal relationship to make model behavior more interpretable and guide architectural choices.

Method: The authors run systematic experiments on Transformer language models, probing when and where massive activations and attention sinks appear. They manipulate architectural components, especially the pre-norm vs post-norm configuration, and ablate or modify them to observe resulting changes. They analyze the spatial (across layers) and functional behavior of tokens with massive activations and with attention-sink behavior, and study their effect on hidden representations and attention patterns across heads.

Result: They find that the co-occurrence of massive activations and attention sinks is mainly due to the standard pre-norm Transformer architecture. Massive activations create nearly constant hidden states that propagate through many layers and behave like implicit parameters shared across contexts. Attention sinks, in contrast, primarily act locally to shape attention distribution, nudging heads toward short-range dependencies. When they remove or change the pre-norm configuration, the co-occurrence of massive activations and attention sinks largely disappears, indicating that the architecture couples these otherwise distinct mechanisms.

Conclusion: Massive activations and attention sinks, while often appearing on the same tokens, fulfill different but complementary roles in Transformer language models: massive activations globally encode implicit parameters via persistent constant-like representations, and attention sinks locally shape attention patterns and locality bias. Their frequent co-occurrence is not primarily a semantic phenomenon but an artifact of the pre-norm Transformer design. Changing the normalization scheme (e.g., ablating pre-norm) decouples them, suggesting that architectural choices strongly influence emergent internal mechanisms and should be considered carefully for interpretability and control.

Abstract: We study two recurring phenomena in Transformer language models: massive activations, in which a small number of tokens exhibit extreme outliers in a few channels, and attention sinks, in which certain tokens attract disproportionate attention mass regardless of semantic relevance. Prior work observes that these phenomena frequently co-occur and often involve the same tokens, but their functional roles and causal relationship remain unclear. Through systematic experiments, we show that the co-occurrence is largely an architectural artifact of modern Transformer design, and that the two phenomena serve related but distinct functions. Massive activations operate globally: they induce near-constant hidden representations that persist across layers, effectively functioning as implicit parameters of the model. Attention sinks operate locally: they modulate attention outputs across heads and bias individual heads toward short-range dependencies. We identify the pre-norm configuration as the key choice that enables the co-occurrence, and show that ablating it causes the two phenomena to decouple.

</details>
